Maimonides on the Origin of Evils
Maimonides explains that evils are due to human excess, not divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maimonides' argument that most evils arise from human excess rather than from God, and why he attributes them to human free will.

Core Idea
Evils come from our own excess, not from God's decree.

In this passage, the author presents Maimonides' explanation for the existence of evils. Drawing on Jewish tradition, Maimonides distinguishes between necessities and excesses, arguing that calamities resulting from the pursuit of unnecessary luxuries are due to human choice, not divine will. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once. Notice the initial reference to Jewish scholars' method of interpreting clear (muḥkam) and ambiguous (mutashābih) verses, and the midrash citation.
The midrash says 'No evil descends from above' – this sets the stage.
2
Identify Maimonides' key distinction
Look for Maimonides' contrast between necessities (limited) and excesses (unlimited). How does he use this to explain the origin of evils?
The phrase 'التطاول فغير متناه' (excess is infinite) is crucial.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final sentence: 'And here ends the error of the multitude.' Why does Maimonides consider it an error to blame God for such evils?
Think about personal responsibility and divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلماء بني إسرائيل يفهمون القضية على أنها محكم ومتشابه ففي الفصل التاسع من ه براشیت ربه، ما نصه : ولا يوجد شر نازلا من فوق وهم بهذا قد أولوا نص أشعياء بإرجاعه إلى المحكم. والموسى بن ميمون المتوفى سنة ٦٠٢ هـ تعليل وجيه لبعض أنواع الشرور . والغاية من تعليله : أنها من الناس وليست من الله . يقول ابن ميمون : ه أما الضروريات كلها فمحصورة متناهية. أما التطاول فغير متناه . إن تعلق شوقك : أن تكون أوانيك فضة ، وكونها ذهباً أجمل - وآخرون اتخذوها بلوراً - ولعلك أيضاً تتخذ من الزمرد والياقوت كل ما يمكن وجوده . فلا يزال كل جاهل فاسد الفكرة : في نكد وحزن ، على كونه لا يصل أن يعقل ما فعله فلان من التطاولات. وفي الأكثر يعرض نفسه لأخطار عظيمة ، كركوبه البحر وخدمة الملوك . وغاية ذلك : أن ينال تلك التطاولات الغير ضرورية . فإذا أصابته المصائب في طرقه تلك التي سلكها ، تشكي من قضائه وقدره ، وأخذ يذم الزمان ، ويتعجب من قلة إنصافه ، كيف لم يساعده على تحصيل مال جزيل يجد به خراً كثيراً ، يسكر بها دائماً ، و جوارى عدة ، محليات بأنواع الذهب والأحجار ، حتى يحركنه للجماع بأكثر مما في الطاقة : ليلتذ ؟ كان الغاية الوجودية إنما هي لذة هذا الخسيس فقط . وإلى هنا انتهى غلط الجمهور .
The scholars of the Children of Israel understand the matter as that it contains clear (muḥkam) and ambiguous (mutashābih) [verses]. In the ninth chapter of [the book] 'Bereshit Rabbah', its text is: 'No evil descends from above.' And by this they interpreted the text of Isaiah, returning it to the clear [verses]. And Moses son of Maimon, who died in the year 602 AH, gave a plausible explanation for some types of evils. And the purpose of his explanation is that they are from people and not from God. Ibn Maimon says: 'As for all necessities, they are limited and finite. As for excess, it is infinite. If your desire is for your vessels to be silver, and for them to be gold is more beautiful – and others have taken them as crystal – and perhaps you also take from emerald and ruby everything that can be found. So every ignorant, corrupt-minded person remains in distress and sorrow, because he cannot comprehend what so-and-so has done in excesses. And most often he exposes himself to great dangers, such as riding the sea and serving kings. And the utmost goal of that is to attain those unnecessary excesses. Then if calamities befall him in those paths he has taken, he complains of God's decree and destiny (qaḍā’ihi wa qadrihi), and begins to blame time, and marvels at the lack of justice, how it did not help him to obtain abundant wealth by which he would find much wine, with which he gets drunk always, and many maidens adorned with types of gold and jewels, so that they move him to intercourse more than what is in the capacity, so that he may take pleasure? The existential goal is only the pleasure of this vile one alone. And here ends the error of the multitude.'
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage discusses the Jewish scholarly tradition's approach to apparent contradictions in scripture (the muḥkam and mutashābih) and then presents Maimonides' explanation of the origin of evils. The author begins by noting that Jewish scholars understand that Scripture contains clear and ambiguous passages, citing a midrash (Bereshit Rabbah) that 'no evil descends from above', meaning that God does not directly cause evil. They reinterpreted a verse in Isaiah accordingly. Then the focus shifts to Maimonides (d. 1204 CE), who offers a rational explanation for certain types of evils: they originate from human actions, not from God. Maimonides' argument, quoted at length, distinguishes between necessities (which are limited) and excesses (which are unlimited). People who pursue unnecessary luxuries often suffer calamities in their pursuit, yet they blame divine decree rather than their own choices. The ultimate cause of their suffering is their own immoderate desires. The author concludes that the common error is to attribute such evils to God, whereas they are actually due to human excess.

Correction: Maimonides argues that evils are not from God but result from human pursuit of unnecessary excesses.

D) They are punishments for sins committed in a past life.

Key Takeaway
Maimonides argues that evils are not sent by God but result from human pursuit of unnecessary excesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When a difficulty strikes, do you automatically look outward—at God, fate, or other people—or do you honestly examine your own choices, especially those driven by desire for more than you need? What would it take for you to pause before complaining and reflect on whether your own pursuit of excess played a part?
?
Think of a recent disappointment or hardship. Can you trace it back to a desire for something unnecessary—luxury, status, or pleasure beyond what is sufficient? How does it feel to admit that you might have contributed to your own suffering? What one step can you take today to reduce that excess?
?
Are you willing to accept full responsibility for the consequences of your own excesses, even when they feel like unfair hardships? If you lost something you chased intensely, could you still say, 'It is from me, not from God'? Write down a specific area where you need to realign your desires with necessity.

Refuting the Claim That God Creates Human Actions
Al-Qazwini refutes the view that God creates human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Dr. Al-Qazwini's refutation of the view that God creates human acts of obedience and sin, and the correct interpretation of divine decree and determination.

Core Idea
God's decree is a command, not creation.

In this passage, Dr. Al-Qazwini clarifies the meanings of qada and qadr in relation to human actions. He argues that God's decree is a command and obligation, not direct creation. He then refutes the idea that God creates human actions using scriptural and logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Qazwini distinguishes between different meanings of qada.
2
Identify the positive definitions
Identify how Al-Qazwini defines qada as command/obligation and qadr as clarification/measure.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Examine the argument that if qada meant creation, the Quran would phrase it differently. Also consider the proof from Quran 32:7 that God's creation is good.
The key is the preposition 'in' vs 'upon'.
4
Examine the other refutations
Consider the refutation against qada as command (Quran 7:28) and as foreknowledge (logical argument).
The passage ends abruptly but the logic is clear.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Understand that Al-Qazwini establishes that God does not create sinful actions; humans are responsible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول الدكتور علاء الدين أمير محمد مهدي . القزويني : ه أما ما يتعلق بأفعال الإنسان . فيمكن القول أن الله تعالى قضى بها عليه . على معنى : حكم بها عليه ، وألزمه إياها وأوجبها ، وهذا الإلزام : هو أمره له وليس على سبيل الإلجاء والإجبار . كما يمكن أيضاً القول أن الله قدر أفعال الإنسان على معنى أنه بين مقاديرها وأوضح تفاصيلها واختلاف أحوالها ، من حسنها وقبحها ، وفرضها وحلالها ، وحرامها ومندوبها . وأما أنه قضاها بمعنى خلقها في الإنسان فباطل لا يجوز ، لأنه إن أريد أنه خلقها فيه ، لقال سبحانه : وقضى في خلقه بالعصيان ولا يقول قضى عليهم ، لأن الخلق إنما يكون في الإنسان لا عليه ، مع أنه تعالى قد أبطل هذا القول بقوله : ( الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه ) [السجدة ٧] فهو صريح في عدم وجود القبيح في خلقه، وأن كل فعله حسن ، والمعاصي قبائح باتفاق المسلمين ، فوجب نفيها عنه سبحانه . وإن أريد أنه قضى عليه بالمعاصي بمعنى أمره بها فقد أبطل هذا القول أيضاً ، قوله تعالى : ( إن الله لا يأمر بالفحشاء . أتقولون على الله ما لا تعلمون ؟ ) [ الأراف ۲۸ ] فهذا صريح في عدم إرادة هذا المعنى . وإن أريد أن الله قضى على الإنسان بالمعاصي بمعنى أعلمه جها ، فغير صحيح أيضاً . لأن الإنسان لا يعلم في المستقبل بأنه يطيع أو يعصي . ولا يستطيع يحيط علما بما يكون فيه على التفصيل . ولأنه لو خلق الطاعة والمعصية في عباده ، ليسقط اللوم -
And Dr. Ala al-Din Amir Muhammad Mahdi al-Qazwini says: 'As for what pertains to human actions, it is possible to say that God, the Exalted, decreed them upon him. In the sense that He judged them upon him, and necessitated them for him, and made them obligatory; and this imposition is His command to him, and not by way of coercion or compulsion. It is also possible to say that God determined (qaddara) human actions in the sense that He clarified their measures, explained their details and the differences in their states, their goodness and badness, their obligations and permitted, their forbidden and recommended. As for the view that He decreed them (qaḍā) in the sense of creating them in the human, it is false and not permissible. Because if it were meant that He created them in him, He would have said, 'And He decreed (qaḍā) in His creation disobedience' but He does not say 'decreed upon them'. For creation is in the human, not upon him. Moreover, the Exalted has nullified this statement by His saying: 'Who perfected everything He created' (Quran 32:7). So it is explicit that there is no evil in His creation, and that all His actions are good. And sins are evil by agreement of the Muslims, so it is necessary to negate them from Him, the Exalted. And if it is meant that He decreed upon him disobedience in the sense of commanding it, then this statement is also nullified by His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not command immorality. Do you say about Allah that which you do not know?' (Quran 7:28). So this is explicit in the non-intention of this meaning. And if it is meant that God decreed upon the human disobedience in the sense of informing him of it, that is also incorrect. Because the human does not know in the future whether he will obey or disobey, nor can he encompass knowledge of what he will be in detail. And because if He had created obedience and disobedience in His servants, so that blame would be removed...'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This is an editorial insertion by Dr. Al-Qazwini, providing a rebuttal against the idea that God creates human acts of obedience and sin. The passage argues that God's decree (qaḍā') regarding human actions means a command and obligation, not direct creation. God's determination (taqdīr) is His clarification of the measures and details of actions, including moral categories. The view that God creates actions in humans is refuted with three arguments: 1) If God created sin in a person, the Quran would say 'He decreed in His creation disobedience' not 'upon them'; creation is 'in' not 'upon'. 2) Quran 32:7 states all God's creation is good, so He cannot create evil (sins are evil by consensus). 3) If decree meant command, then Quran 7:28 refutes that God commands immorality. 4) If decree meant foreknowledge, it is still incorrect because humans do not know their future actions in detail, and if God created both obedience and disobedience in servants, blame would be removed (which is absurd). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument clearly denies that God is the creator of human sins.

حسن (ḥasan)

Misconception: Some may think that God's decree of actions means He creates them directly.
Correction: Al-Qazwini clarifies that decree here means command and determination, not creation. The arguments from Quran and logic show that God does not create sinful actions.

Checkpoint

B) He commands and obligates the human to do it
C) He merely informs the human of the action
D) He forces the human to act against his will

Key Takeaway
God's decree and determination of human actions are His command and clarification, not His creation of those actions.

?

Al-Qazwini argues that if God created both obedience and disobedience in servants, blame would be removed. Have you ever used the idea of predestination to avoid feeling guilty about a sin? After studying this lesson, what is your personal responsibility regarding your actions?
?
The passage states that God does not create evil or command immorality. If you truly believed that every sin you commit is entirely your own choice, not decreed by God as a creation, how would that change the way you repent or seek forgiveness? What step will you take today to act on that belief?

The Verse Sequence Proves Worship is Not Created
Author provides two logical sub-proofs from the verse's sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the immediate sequence 'Creator of everything, so worship Him' proves that worship is not created by Allah.

Core Idea
The logical link in the verse shows worship cannot be part of 'everything'.

This passage digs into the logical structure of one of the most famous verses about Allah's creation. The author shows that the way the verse is constructed—with 'so worship Him' following 'Creator of everything'—implies that worship cannot be among the things Allah creates. We'll explore two arguments from this structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the two arguments the author enumerates.
The text breaks off mid-sentence, but the core argument is complete.
2
Understand the first argument: command implies action
The author argues that Allah commands worship. How could He command something that He Himself creates? That would be absurd.
This relies on the principle that command implies the commanded act is the servant's act.
3
Understand the second argument: cause and effect cannot be identical
The verse uses 'fa' (so) to link 'Creator of everything' to 'worship Him'. If worship were included in 'everything', then the condition would be the same as the consequent, which is logically invalid.
Think of it as: if X is the reason for Y, Y cannot be part of X.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان الدلائل المتصلة بهذه الآية ، الدالة على كونها مخصوصة بما سوى أفعال العباد : أنه تعالى لما قال : ( خالق كل شيء ) اردفه بقوله : ( فاعبدوه ) وهذا يدل على أن العبادة ليست فعلا الله تعالى . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى أمرهم بها ، وكيف يعقل أن يأمرهم بما لا يكون فعلاً لهم الثاني : إنه تعالى جعل كونه خالقاً للأشياء ، علة لكونه مستحقاً للعبادة . بدليل : أنه تعالى ، لما ذكر كونه خالقاً للأشياء ، أتبعه بقوله : فاعبدوه ) فلو كانت عبادتهم لـه ، داخلة في قوله : ( خالق كل شيء ) لصار الشرط عين الجزاء. وهذا باطل . لأنه يصير في التقدير كأنه يقول :
The second aspect in explaining the connected evidences of this verse, indicating that it is specified to exclude the actions of the servants: That when He said 'Creator of everything', He followed it with 'So worship Him' (fa'buduhu). This indicates that worship is not an act of Allah. Its explanation is from aspects: The first: He commanded them with it, and how can it be understood that He commands them with what is not an act for them? The second: He made His being the Creator of things a cause for His being deserving of worship. Evidence: When He mentioned His being the Creator of things, He followed it with 'So worship Him'. If their worship of Him were included in His statement 'Creator of everything', then the condition would be the same as the consequent. This is false, because in estimation it would be as if He said: (the text breaks off).
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the second connected evidence from the immediate context. The verse says: 'There is no god but He, the Creator of everything. So worship Him' (fa'buduhu). The author argues that this sequence shows worship is not an act of Allah. Two sub-proofs: (1) Allah commands worship – how could He command them to do what He Himself creates? That would be absurd. (2) The structure of the verse: 'Creator of everything' is presented as the reason (cause) for worship; the letter 'fa' indicates result. If worship itself were included in 'everything', then the cause would be identical to the effect, making the condition the same as the consequent, which is logically invalid. The text cuts off mid-sentence but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
فاعبدوه
شرط
جزاء
العبادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah's creation of worship does not conflict with His command to worship.
Correction: The author shows that if Allah creates worship, then the command becomes meaningless because the act is not the servant's. Also, the logical structure of the verse prevents worship from being part of 'everything'.

Checkpoint
How does the command 'fa'buduhu' show that worship is not Allah's act?
A) Because Allah cannot command Himself
B) Because it would make the condition identical to the consequent
C) Because the verse is abrogated
D) Because worship is not a created thing

Key Takeaway
The immediate sequence of the verse proves that acts of worship are not created by Allah.

?

?
This verse sequence shows that Allah's being Creator is the reason for worship. Is that reason enough for you today? Or do you need something else? What would that say about your faith?

Limiting the Verse's Generality via Context of Praise
Argument that the context of praise limits the verse's generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the context of praise in the verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things' serves as a contextual indicator (qarīna) to specify its generality, excluding human actions, and why attributing evil actions to God would contradict praise.

Core Idea
The praise context of 'Creator of all things' proves it cannot include evil acts.

The author presents the first of several connected arguments to show that the verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things' is not absolutely general. He argues that the verse occurs in a context of praise, and that if God were the creator of evil acts, it would necessitate blame, contradicting the praise. This contextual evidence forces a specification of the verse's meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the forceful rhetorical style and the analogy.
2
Identify the main claim about context
Pinpoint where the author states that the phrase 'Creator of everything' is mentioned in the context of praise. Understand why this is crucial.
The author uses this as the starting premise.
3
Understand the argument against Jabariyya
Trace the reductio ad absurdum: if God creates all actions, only God would be blameworthy, which is absurd. This strengthens the need for specification.
This refutes the determinist position.
4
Analyze the analogy
Reflect on the analogy of commanding a paralyzed person to fly. How does this illustrate the irrationality of commanding someone who cannot act? Connect this to the main point.
The analogy makes the argument vivid and intuitive.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the context of praise, the blame argument, and the analogy to see how the author concludes that the generality is specified to exclude human actions.
This is the final qarīna (contextual evidence) he establishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتوسط مدحاً ، وإلا لصار الكلام : ركيكاً . فثبت : أن قوله : ( خالق كل شيء ) : مذكور في معرض المدح والثناء على الله تعالى . وكونه فاعلاً للجهل والكفر والقبائح والفواحش، يوجب الذم العظيم . بل نقول : إن على مذهب المجبرة ، لا مذموم إلا وفاعله هو الله تعالى، فوجب أن لا يستحق الذم أحد إلا الله . وأيضاً : لو كان تعالى موجداً لأفعال العباد ، ثم يأمرهم بالطاعة والإيمان ، صار كأنه يقول لعبيده : يا من لا يمكن أن يفعل : افعل . وكأنه يضرب الزمن ، ويقول له : طر في الهواء ، ويضرب الأعمى ، ويأمره بنقط المصاحف ، ويقيد يدي عبده ورجليه ويرميه من شاهق جبل ، ثم يضربه فيها بين الهواء ، ويقول له : قف. ومعلوم : أن مثل هذا الشخص لا يـوجـد في الدنيا ، أشد استحقاقاً للذم منه . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن قوله تعالى : ( خالق كل شيء ) مذكور في معرض المدح والثناء ، وثبت أن كونه خالقاً لأفعال العباد ، يوجب الذم العظيم ، فصارت هذه القرينة : قرينة دالة على أن عموم قوله : ( الله خالق كل شيء ) : مخصوص ، بما سوى أفعال العباد .
The intermediate [is] praised, otherwise the speech would be weak. So it is established that His saying 'Creator of everything' is mentioned in the context of praise and commendation of God Almighty. And that He is the doer of ignorance, disbelief, abominations, and lewd acts would necessitate great blame. Rather we say: according to the doctrine of the Jabariyya, no one is blameworthy unless its doer is God Almighty, so it would be necessary that no one deserves blame except God. Also: if He, exalted, were the originator of the actions of servants, then He commands them with obedience and faith, it becomes as if He says to His slaves: O you who cannot act, act! And as if He strikes a paralyzed person and says to him: Fly in the air, and strikes a blind person and commands him to dot the copies of the Qur'an, and ties the hands and feet of His servant and throws him from a high mountain, then strikes him mid-air and says to him: Stop. And it is known that such a person does not exist in the world more deserving of blame than him. So it is established by what we have mentioned that God's saying 'Creator of everything' is mentioned in the context of praise and commendation, and it is established that His being the creator of the actions of servants would necessitate great blame; so this contextual evidence became evidence indicating that the generality of His saying 'God is the Creator of everything' is specified to exclude the actions of servants.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is the first connected argument (dalīl muttaṣil) for the interpretation that the verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things' excludes human actions. The author begins by noting that the phrase 'Creator of everything' occurs in a context of praise and commendation. If God were the doer of ignorance, disbelief, and evil acts, that would necessitate blame, contradicting praise. He then argues that on the Jabariyya view (which denies human agency), only God would be blameworthy, which is absurd. He provides an analogy: commanding one who cannot act is like ordering a paralyzed person to fly or a blind person to dot Qur'an copies—the most blameworthy behavior. Therefore, the context of praise serves as a contextual indicator (qarīna) that the generality of 'Creator of all things' is specified (makhṣūṣ) to exclude human actions.

Key Terms
خالق كل شيء
المدح والثناء
الذم
المجبرة
القرينة
المخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things' is absolutely general and includes human actions without exception.
Correction: The context of praise in which the verse appears necessitates specifying its generality to exclude human actions, because attributing evil to God would contradict praise.

Checkpoint

A) The verse is followed by a command to worship.
B) The phrase appears in a context of praise.
C) Human actions are intrinsically evil.
D) The Jabariyya deny human agency.

Key Takeaway
The context of praise in the verse 'Creator of all things' necessitates specifying its generality to exclude human actions, because attributing evil to God would contradict praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God is the Creator of all things, including your own acts, then why does He deserve praise? This passage offers an answer. Have you truly internalized that answer, or do you still sometimes attribute your faults to Him? What would it take for you to fully own your actions?

The Command to Worship Proves Human Agency
Author argues that 'فاعبدوه' indicates worship is not God's act, with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic command to worship ('So worship Him') provides a rational argument that human actions are not created by Allah, based on three logical proofs.

Core Idea
The command to worship proves worship is our act, not God's.

This lesson examines the second connected argument from the verse 'Creator of everything' followed by 'So worship Him'. The author presents three proofs that worship cannot be an act of Allah because it is commanded, because it would create a logical contradiction if worship were also created, and because the creation statement refers to past acts while the command is future. These proofs establish that human actions fall outside the scope of 'Creator of everything'.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the three proofs introduced by 'الوجه الأول', 'الوجه الثاني', and 'الوجه الثالث'.
The author uses a concise style; each proof is a separate logical point.
2
Examine each proof in order
Work through each proof one by one. Understand why each one shows that worship cannot be an act of Allah but must be the servant's act.
Pay special attention to the second proof's logical form: if condition equals consequence, contradiction arises.
3
Synthesize the conclusion
See how the three proofs together lead to the decisive conclusion that what falls under 'So worship Him' is not included under 'Creator of everything'.
This conclusion is stated explicitly at the end: 'و هذا يفيد القطع ...'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان الدلائل المتصلة بهذه الآية ، الدالة على كونها مخصوصة بما سوى أفعال العباد : أنه تعالى لما قال : ( خالق كل شيء ) اردفه بقوله : ( فاعبدوه ) وهذا يدل على أن العبادة ليست فعلا الله تعالى . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى أمرهم بها ، وكيف يعقل أن يأمرهم بما لا يكون فعلاً لهم . الثاني : إنه تعالى جعل كونه خالقاً للأشياء ، علة لكونه مستحقاً للعبادة . بدليل : أنه تعالى ، لما ذكر كونه خالقاً للأشياء ، أتبعه بقوله : فاعبدوه ) فلو كانت عبادتهم لـه ، داخلة في قوله : ( خالق كل شيء ) لصار الشرط عين الجزاء. وهذا باطل . لأنه يصير في التقدير كأنه يقول : يا عبدي ، فعلت أنا عبادتك ، فافعلها أنت بعينها أيضاً . ومعلوم أن هذا الكلام باطل . الثالث : إن قوله تعالى : ( خالق كل شيء ) يدل على أن خلق تلك الأشياء ، قد حصل ودخل في الوجود ، وصار مفروغاً منه ، كما إذا قال قائل : فلان قاتل زيداً : فهم منه : أن ذلك القتل قد حصل ، ودخل في الوجود ، وفرغ منه . وأما قوله : ( فاعبدوه ) فهو أمر ، والأمر إنما يتعلق بما يكون حاصلاً في الحال . لكنه مما سيحصل . قلت : إن كل ما دخل تحت قوله : ( خالق كل شيء ) فقد حصل في الحال ، ولا داخل في الوجود. وهذا يفيد القطع بأن الداخل تحت قوله : و فاعبدوه و غیر داخل تحت قوله : ( خالق كل شيء )
And the second aspect in explaining the connected evidences pertaining to this verse, indicating that it is specified to exclude the actions of servants: that when He, exalted, said 'Creator of everything', He followed it with His saying 'So worship Him', and this indicates that worship is not an act of God Almighty. Its explanation is from several aspects: The first: He, exalted, commanded them with it, and how can it be reasonable that He commands them with what is not an act for them? The second: He, exalted, made His being the Creator of things the cause of His being deserving of worship. The evidence: that He, exalted, when He mentioned His being the Creator of things, He followed it with His saying 'So worship Him'. So if their worship of Him were included in His saying 'Creator of everything', then the condition would be the very same as the consequence. And this is false. Because in estimation it becomes as if He says: O My servant, I did your worship, so you do it yourself as well. And it is known that this speech is false. The third: that His saying 'Creator of everything' indicates that the creation of those things has occurred, entered into existence, and is finished, just as when someone says: So-and-so killed Zaid, it is understood that the killing occurred, entered into existence, and is finished. As for His saying 'So worship Him', it is a command, and the command only pertains to what will be in the future, not what has already occurred. I say: everything that falls under His saying 'Creator of everything' has occurred in the present and entered into existence. And this yields the certainty that what falls under His saying 'So worship Him' is not included under His saying 'Creator of everything'.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is the second connected argument, based on the verse 'So worship Him' following 'Creator of everything'. The author presents three proofs that worship is not God's act. First, God commands worship; it is absurd to command what is not an act of the servant. Second, God makes His creation the cause of His deserving worship; if worship were also created by Him, the condition (creation) would be identical to the consequence (worship), leading to contradiction. Third, 'Creator of everything' indicates completed past creation, while 'So worship Him' is a future command; therefore, worship is not part of what He created. The author concludes decisively that what falls under the command is not included under the creation statement.

Key Terms

الأمر
الشرط عين الجزاء
الخلق
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because Allah commands worship, He must also create the worship in the servant.
Correction: The author shows the opposite: a command logically implies the commanded action is a human act, not divine creation.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof (الوجه الثاني) in this argument?
A) God commands worship, so it must be the servant's act.
B) If God created worship, the condition would be the same as the consequence.
C) 'Creator of everything' refers to past acts, while command is future.
D) Human actions are independent of God.

Key Takeaway
The command to worship in the Quran logically requires that worship be an act of the servant, not of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you start acting today as if your choices truly shape your worship and your relationship with your Creator?

The Verse on Insight Proves Human Independence
Author cites the verse about insight to argue that man is independent in his actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the verse on insight (Quran 6:104) to argue that humans are independent agents, not compelled by God.

Core Idea
The verse on insight proves that choice and responsibility belong to the servant.

The author now presents a third argument, this time from a verse that immediately follows the 'Creator of all things' verse. By examining the language of reward and responsibility, he shows that the earlier verse cannot include human actions.

Independence (استقلال) means the servant is the true agent, not God.

This argument is the third connected one. It shows that the previous verse's generality must exclude human actions, because human actions entail responsibility.

Summary of Connected Arguments and Introduction of Separate Arguments
Author summarizes connected arguments and lists three separate arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between connected and separate evidences regarding the verse about human actions, and you will be introduced to the three categories of separate arguments and the rationale behind the first.

Core Idea
Separate arguments for human agency rest on immediate rational knowledge, reason, and scripture.

This passage marks a pivotal transition. After presenting three connected arguments that specify the verse to exclude human actions, the author now introduces a new set of three separate arguments that provide independent proofs for human agency. The first of these is grounded in immediate rational knowledge of moral evaluation, which the Mu'tazilites argued requires the servant to be the originator of his actions.

Guided Path
1
Recap the connected arguments
Briefly recall that three connected arguments were presented earlier. This passage refers back to them as 'these three aspects.'
2
Identify the three separate arguments
Read the list: immediate rational knowledge, rational proof, and scriptural proof. Note their names and order.
The list is introduced with 'they are three.'
3
Examine the first separate argument
Focus on the exposition of 'badīhat al-ʿaql.' The passage explains that the goodness of praise and blame is known necessarily, and that if the servant were not the originator of his actions, these evaluations would not be good.
This argument is attributed to the followers of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri.
4
Understand the theological context
Recognize that this first separate argument is a foundational rational proof for human agency. It ties moral responsibility to the ability to originate actions.
This aligns with the Mu'tazilite position on free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : دلائل متصلة بهذه الآية دالة على كونها مخصوصة بما سوى أفعال العباد . وأما الدلائل المخصصة المنفصلة . فهي ثلاثة : بديهية العقل، ودليل العقل ، ودليل السمع . أما الأول ، وهو بديهة العقل . فتقريره : أن أصحاب : أبي الحسين ) قالوا : حسن المدح والذم والمؤاخذة والمطالبة : معلوم بالضرورة ، ونعلم أيضاً بالضرورة : أن العبد لو لم يكن موجداً الأفعال نفسه ، لما حسنت هذه الأشياء ، فلما كان العلم بحسن المدح والذم ضرورياً، وكان العلم يكون العبد موجداً
So these three aspects are connected evidences pertaining to this verse, indicating that it is specified to exclude the actions of servants. As for the specifying separate evidences, they are three: immediate rational knowledge (badīhat al-ʿaql), rational proof (dalīl al-ʿaql), and scriptural proof (dalīl al-samʿ). As for the first, which is immediate rational knowledge, its exposition: that the followers of Abu al-Husayn said: The goodness of praise and blame, requital and demand, is known by necessity, and we also know by necessity that if the servant were not the originator of his own actions, these things would not be good. So since knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame is necessary, and the knowledge that the servant is the originator...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage serves as a summary of the three connected arguments (dawāʾil muttaṣila) just presented, and then introduces the separate arguments (dawāʾil munfaṣila) that also specify the verse. The author lists three types: immediate rational knowledge (badīhat al-ʿaql), rational proof (dalīl al-ʿaql), and scriptural proof (dalīl al-samʿ). He begins to explain the first: the followers of Abu al-Husayn (the Mu'tazili theologian) hold that the goodness (ḥusn) of praise and blame, recompense and demand, is known necessarily. If the servant were not the originator of his actions, such moral evaluations would not be valid. The argument is cut off at the bottom of the page, but it sets up the rational necessity of human agency.

دليل العقل
دليل السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the separate arguments are merely scriptural and less important than the connected arguments.
Correction: The author lists three separate arguments, two of which are purely rational (immediate rational knowledge and rational proof), and they are foundational for establishing human agency independently of scripture.

Key Takeaway
The first separate argument establishes that human agency is rationally necessary for moral responsibility to be meaningful.

The Third Connected Argument: Specifying the Verse by Contextual Evidences
Presents two sub-arguments for specifying the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two contextual evidences from the Qur'an specify the verse 'Khaliq kulli shay'' to exclude human actions, demonstrating that humans have free will.

Core Idea
Contextual evidence within the Qur'an itself restricts the scope of 'Creator of everything'.

This lesson examines the third connected argument that uses the immediate context of the verse 'Khaliq kulli shay'' to show that it does not include human actions. The argument relies on two points: first, the past tense of 'khalaqa' implies creation is already complete, while the command to worship is future-oriented; second, the following verse explicitly affirms human choice and independence. Together, these evidences specify the general statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sub-arguments
Read the passage and notice that the author presents two distinct reasons why the verse is specified: one based on tense/aspect and one based on the next verse.
The first sub-argument uses linguistic analysis of 'khalaqa' as past vs command as future.
2
Understand the linguistic point
The author compares 'Khaliq kulli shay'' to saying 'so-and-so killed Zayd' – both imply the action is completed. Hence creation is already done. In contrast, the command 'fa'buduhu' is about future action, so worship is not yet created.
3
Analyze the second sub-argument
The author quotes verse 6:104 which states that whoever sees clearly does so for himself, and whoever is blind does so against himself. This is taken as proof that the servant is independent and not compelled.
This is a direct textual evidence from the same passage.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Both evidences together show that the verse 'Khaliq kulli shay'' is specified to exclude human actions. Connect this to the broader debate on predestination and free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن قوله تعالى : ( خالق كل شيء ) يدل على أن خلق تلك الأشياء ، قد حصل ودخل في الوجود ، وصار مفروغاً منه ، كما إذا قال قائل : فلان قاتل زيداً : فهم منه : أن ذلك القتل قد حصل ، ودخل في الوجود ، وفرغ منه . وأما قوله : ( فاعبدوه ) فهو أمر ، والأمر إنما يتعلق بما يكون حاصلاً في الحال . لكنه مما سيحصل .
قلت : إن كل ما دخل تحت قوله : ( خالق كل شيء ) فقد حصل في الحال ، ولا داخل في الوجود. وهذا يفيد القطع بأن الداخل تحت قوله : و فاعبدوه و غیر داخل تحت قوله : ( خالق كل شيء
والوجه الثالث في بيان الدلائل المتصلة بهذه الآية الدالة على كونها مخصوصة ، بما سوى أفعال العباد : أنه تعالى قال بعد هذه الآية : ( قد جاءكم بصائر من ربكم . فمن أبصر فلنفسه ، ومن عمي فعليها ) وهذا تصريح بأن العبد مستقل بأفعال نفسه ، وأن زمام الاختيار إليه ، وأنه البتة غير مجبور على شيء من أفعاله .
فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : دلائل متصلة بهذه الآية دالة على كونها مخصوصة بما سوى أفعال العباد
The third: Indeed His statement, the Exalted, 'Creator of everything' indicates that the creation of those things has occurred and entered into existence and become finished, just as when someone says: 'So-and-so killed Zayd' it is understood from it that that killing has occurred and entered into existence and been finished. As for His statement 'so worship Him' it is a command, and the command only pertains to what will occur in the present, but it is something that will occur. I say: Indeed everything that enters under His statement 'Creator of everything' has occurred in the present, and nothing enters into existence. And this yields certainty that what enters under His statement 'and worship Him' does not enter under His statement 'Creator of everything'. And the third aspect in explaining the connected evidences to this verse indicating that it is specified excluding acts of servants: that He, the Exalted, said after this verse: 'Verily there have come to you clear proofs from your Lord; whoever sees clearly does so for [the benefit of] his soul, and whoever blinds himself does so against it.' And this is a declaration that the servant is independent in his own acts, and that the reins of choice are to him, and that he is absolutely not compelled in any of his acts. So these three aspects are connected evidences to this verse indicating that it is specified excluding acts of servants.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the third connected argument (daliil muttassil) that specifies the general verse 'Khaliq kulli shay'' (Creator of everything) to exclude human actions. The author first gives a sub-argument: the verb 'khalaqa' in the verse indicates creation that has already occurred and is completed, like saying 'so-and-so killed Zayd' which implies the killing is finished. In contrast, the command 'fa'buduhu' (so worship Him) is an order for future action. Thus what falls under 'Creator of everything' is already realized, while what falls under the command is not yet realized, proving human actions are not part of that creation. The author then adds a second sub-argument: the very next verse (Qur'an 6:104) states that clear proofs have come, and whoever sees clearly does so for himself, and whoever is blind does so against himself. This explicitly affirms that the servant is independent in his actions, has free choice, and is not compelled. Therefore these three connected proofs (the two mentioned here plus an earlier one not in this window) demonstrate that the verse is specified to exclude human acts.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the verse 'Khaliq kulli shay'' includes all events without exception.
Correction: This argument shows that contextual verses restrict the scope of 'Creator of everything' to exclude human actions, as evidenced by the past tense of creation and the following verse affirming human choice.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the third connected argument regarding the verse 'Khaliq kulli shay''?
A) It proves that God creates everything including human actions.
B) It argues that the verse is general and cannot be specified.
C) It shows that the verse specifies human actions because creation is past and the command is future, and the following verse affirms human choice.
D) It claims that the verse has no connection to human actions.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Khaliq kulli shay'' is specified to exclude human actions by two contextual evidences: the past tense of creation versus the future command, and the following verse affirming human choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'It was written for me' or 'God created that action in me'? How does this lesson's clear distinction between completed creation and future command challenge that excuse?
?
If your actions are truly your own, as the verse 'whoever sees clearly does so for himself' declares, then what are you doing right now to take full responsibility for your choices—especially the ones you blame on circumstances or fate?
?
The command 'worship Him' is a future action you must bring into existence. Are there acts of worship you have been delaying or avoiding, hiding behind the idea that 'only God can make it happen'? What will you do today to exercise your choice and bring that worship into reality?

The First Objection: Uncertainty of Specification
First objection: uncertainty of specification and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that lack of known specification does not prove its absence, thus making scriptural evidence conjectural, and the author's refutation that rational proof cannot be added to scripture without circularity.

Core Idea
Not knowing a specification does not mean it doesn't exist.

The first objection challenges the certainty of the argument from the verse 'Creator of everything' by pointing out that we may simply be unaware of its specifying factor. The author then shows that appealing to rational proof to support the verse either renders the verse unnecessary or is invalid without that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first paragraph of the passage (from 'سلمنا' to 'ظنية') and its translation. Note the structure: concession, then rebuttal, then a potential counter-objection, then the author's reply.
The objection admits no known specifier, but argues that this does not prove non-existence.
2
Identify the dialectical moves
The imaginary opponent says they can use rational proof to support the verse. Notice how the author responds: either you have rational proof and don't need the verse, or you don't have it and can't use the verse. This shows circularity.
This is a typical kalam refutation of trying to combine scriptural and rational proofs.
3
Reflect on the logical outcome
Consider why this exchange matters for the overall argument about God creating actions. The objection weakens the certainty of the verse, and the author's reply shows that rational proof must stand alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أنا لا نعرف لعموم قوله : ( خالق كل شيء ) مخصصاً . إلا أن عدم العلم بالشيء ، لا يدل على العلم بعدم الشيء ، فلا يمكننا القطع بعدم المخصص ، وحينئذ تصير هذه الدلائل ظنية. لا يقال : إنا نقيم الدلالة العقلية على أنه تعالى خالق لأعمال العباد ، ونجعل تلك الدلالة العقلية مؤكدة ، لظاهر هذا النص ، ونعلم أن الدليل العقلي لا يعارضه معارض . فبهذا الطريق نعلم أن ظاهر هذا العموم سليم عن المعارض . لأنا نقول : فعلى هذا التقدير ما لم يعرفوا تلك الدلالة العقلية الدالة على أن موجد فعل العبد هو الله ، لا يمكنكم التمسك بهذا العموم. لكنكم إذا عرفتم ذلك الدليل ، فقد استغنيتم عن التمسك بهذا العموم .
سلمنا : أن هذه الآية تدل على أنه تعالى خالق لأعمال العباد ، وموجد لها . لكن لم قلتم : إن ذلك ينافي كون العبد موجدا لها ؟ فإن هذا إنما يتم إذا أقمتم الدلالة على امتناع وقوع مخلوق بين خالفين . والذي يقوي هذا السؤال : هو أن عندكم فعل العبد ، فعل الله تعالى ، فإذا لم يمتنع حصول فعل لفاعلين ، فكيف يمتنع مخلوق لخالقين ؟ والله أعلم .
والجواب : قوله : و لم لا يجوز أن يكون الله خالقاً لأعمال العباد ، بمعنى : أنه مقدر لها ، وهذا التقدير مفسر بالحكم والإخبار ؟ قلنا : هذا باطل . لأن المخبر عن هذا المعنى لا يسمى خالقاً ، وإلا لكان العبد ، إذا أخبر أن الإله قديم
We concede: we do not know a specifying factor for the generality of His statement 'Creator of everything'. However, lack of knowledge of a thing does not indicate knowledge of the absence of the thing, so we cannot be certain of the absence of a specifying factor, and then these evidences become conjectural. It cannot be said: we establish rational proof that He, the Exalted, is the creator of the servants' actions, and we make that rational proof as a confirmation of the apparent meaning of this text, and we know that rational proof is not opposed by anything. So by this way we know that the apparent meaning of this generality is safe from opposition. For we say: on this assumption, as long as they are not aware of that rational proof indicating that the creator of the servant's act is God, you cannot cling to this generality. But if you are aware of that proof, then you are in no need of clinging to this generality. We concede: that this verse indicates that He, the Exalted, is the creator of the servants' actions and their originator. But why do you say that this contradicts the servant being their originator? For this only holds if you establish proof of the impossibility of a created thing occurring between two creators. And what strengthens this question is that according to you, the servant's act is God's act, so if the occurrence of an act from two agents is not impossible, then how can a created thing from two creators be impossible? And God knows best. And the answer: his statement: 'Why is it not permissible that God is the creator of the servants' actions in the sense that He is the one who decrees them, and this decree is interpreted as judgment and informing?' We say: this is false, because one who informs about this meaning is not called a creator, otherwise the servant, if he informed that God is eternal...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections to the argument. The first objection: 'We admit we do not know a specific specifying factor for the verse, but lack of knowledge does not prove non-existence; the evidences become merely conjectural (ẓanniyya).' The author responds to a counter-objection (the interlocutor says they can use rational proof to support the apparent meaning). The author replies: 'If you rely on rational proof to show God creates actions, then you don't need the verse; if you don't have that proof, you can't use the verse.' The second objection: 'Even if the verse shows God creates actions, why does that negate the servant also creating them? It only works if you prove an act cannot have two creators, but you yourself say the servant's act is God's act, so two agents are possible.' The answer (incomplete in the text) argues against interpreting God's creation as mere decree, because that would misuse the term 'creator'.

Key Terms
مخصوص
ظني
دلالة عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if a scripture appears general, it must remain general and cannot be specified.
Correction: The passage clarifies that lack of known specification does not prove its non-existence, so generalization is uncertain. More importantly, resorting to rational proof does not solve the problem.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first objection (سلمنا) in this passage?
A) The verse is clearly about human actions.
B) Lack of known specification does not prove absence, so the evidences become uncertain.
C) Rational proof cannot specify Quranic verses.
D) The servant's act cannot have two creators.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that adding rational proof to uncertain scripture creates circularity: either the proof suffices alone or the scripture remains uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you lack evidence for something, it must be false? How does this lesson challenge you to avoid that assumption in your faith, especially regarding matters of divine decree?
?
When you rely on rational arguments to support your beliefs, do you check if they are actually sufficient on their own? Or are you using them to prop up weak evidence? This lesson calls you to be honest about the certainty of your proofs—what will you do today to examine your own reasoning?
?
The author shows that if you have a strong rational proof, you don't need the textual evidence. How does this affect the way you combine reason and revelation in your own understanding of divine decree? Will you commit to seeking certainty from both, or will you let this lesson humble you to accept when you only have conjectural evidence?

The Second Objection: Possibility of Two Creators
Second objection: possibility of two creators and initial reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that even if God creates actions, it does not negate the servant also being a creator, and the beginning of the author's response that distinguishes between creation and decree.

Core Idea
If God’s act and the servant’s act are both possible, why can’t there be two creators?

The second objection directly challenges the conclusion of the argument: even if the verse proves God creates actions, why does that prevent the servant from also being a creator? The objection cleverly turns the opponent's own principle against them, noting that they hold the servant's act is also God's act. The answer begins to refute the interpretation of 'creator' as mere decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and answer
Read the second paragraph from 'سلمنا : أن هذه الآية' to the end. Note the structure: concession, then the key question, then a strengthening point, then the beginning of the answer.
The objection uses the opponent's own view that the servant's act is also God's act to argue for two creators.
2
Understand the logical force
The opponent argues: if an act can have two agents (as you claim), then a created thing could also have two creators. This undermines the argument that God alone must be the creator.
This is a reductio ad absurdum based on the opponent's own premise.
3
Examine the beginning of the answer
The author begins to answer by rejecting the interpretation that 'creator' means 'one who decrees and informs'. The text cuts off, but the direction is clear: 'creator' must mean something more than mere decree.
The incomplete answer leaves the full refutation unclear, but it starts with a linguistic argument.

Defending the Impossibility of Two Creators
Response to objections about verses and defense of impossibility of two creators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the impossibility of two creators by addressing apparent scriptural contradiction and logical arguments, including the concept of tahsil al-hasil.

Core Idea
One creator for one creation is necessary; two creators is impossible.

This passage tackles a key objection against the Ash'ari position on divine omnipotence and human agency. The author shows that apparent contradictions in the Quran do not undermine the principle that one created thing cannot have two creators, and he even notes an exception. By the end, you'll see how both scriptural and rational objections are handled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author references future answers, so not everything is settled in this passage.
2
Identify the first objection
The objection is based on an apparent contradiction between two Quranic verses: 'So worship Him' seems to limit the universal scope of 'Creator of everything.' How does the author respond?
He says contradiction is contrary to the foundational principle.
3
Understand the key argument against two creators
The author states that agreement exists that one created thing cannot have two creators. Why is this important? What does it imply for human agency?
4
Note the exception of Abu al-Husayn
The author mentions that this answer is not complete according to Abu al-Husayn, who allows one created thing from two creators. Why is this significant?
This shows that not all Muslim theologians agreed on this point.
5
Grasp the additional argument of tahsil al-hasil
If an act occurs by God's power and then identically by the servant's power, that would be a reproduction of the already existent, which is impossible. How does this strengthen the case?
This is a logical argument against two agents producing the same act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معناه
أما قوله : ( إن قوله تعالى : ( فاعبدوه ) يقتضي خروج عموم قوله : ( خالق كل شيء ) قلنا : التعارض خلاف الأصل . والوجوه التي عولوا عليها في تقرير هذا المقام، هي إشارة إلى أنه لا يجوز من الله تعالى أن يأمر عبده بالفعل ، لا إذا كان الفعل واقعاً ، بقدرة العبد . وسيأتي الجواب عنه ، عند الجواب عن شبههم العقلية
•
وأما الدلائل العقلية التي ذكروها ، وزعموا : أنها مخصصة لهذا العموم ،
فسيأتي الجواب عنها .
قوله : « لم قلتم : إن كونه تعالى موجداً لأعمال العباد ، ينفي كون العبد موجداً لها ؟ قلنا : حصل الاتفاق بيننا وبين خصومنا على أن حصول مخلوق واحد لخالقين : محال . إلا أن هذا الجواب لا يتم على قول : أبي الحسين ، فإن عنده يجوز حصول مخلوق واحد بين خالقين. وأيضاً : فالفعل الواقع بقدرة الله تعالى ، لو وقع هو بعينه بقدرة العبد ، كان ذلك تحصيلاً للحاصل . وهو محال . والله أعلم
Its meaning: As for His statement: (Indeed His statement Exalted is He: "So worship Him" necessitates the exclusion of the generality of His statement: "Creator of everything"), we say: Contradiction is contrary to the principle. And the faces which they relied upon in establishing this station indicate that it is not permissible for God Exalted is He to command His servant with an action, not if the action occurs by the servant's power. And the answer to it will come when answering their rational ambiguities. And as for the rational proofs which they mentioned and claimed that they specify this generality, the answer to them will come. His statement: "Why do you say that His being the originator of the actions of servants negates the servant being the originator of them?" We say: Agreement occurred between us and our opponents that the occurrence of one created thing from two creators is impossible. Except that this answer is not complete according to the opinion of Abu al-Husayn, for in his view it is permissible for one created thing to occur between two creators. And also: The action occurring by the power of God Exalted is He, if it were to occur identically by the power of the servant, that would be a reproduction of the already existent (taHsīl al-Hāsil), and that is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The author first addresses an opponent's claim that the verse 'So worship Him' (فاعبدوه) seems to contradict the universal scope of 'Creator of everything' (خالق كل شيء). The author responds that contradiction (التعارض) is contrary to the foundational principle (خلاف الأصل). He explains that the opponent's arguments imply that God cannot command an act that occurs by the servant's power, but the full answer will come later when dealing with rational doubts. Then he turns to the rational proofs that allegedly specify the general verse; again, a future answer is promised. Next, he answers a direct question: 'Why do you say that God being the originator of actions negates the servant being the originator?' The author states that there is agreement between both parties that one created thing cannot have two creators—except according to Abu al-Husayn, who allows it. Additionally, if an act occurs by God's power and then by the servant's power identically, that would be a reproduction of the already existent (تحصيل الحاصل), which is logically impossible. Thus the impossibility of two creators is reaffirmed.

Key Terms
التعارض
تحصيل الحاصل
خالق
مخلوق واحد لخالقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the impossibility of two creators is universally agreed upon among all Muslim theologians.
Correction: The author notes that Abu al-Husayn allows one created thing to occur from two creators, showing that this point was not universally accepted.

Checkpoint
According to the author, whose opinion does the agreement that one created thing cannot have two creators NOT hold for?
A) Abu al-Husayn
B) The Mu'tazila
C) The Ash'aris
D) The philosophers

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of one created thing having two creators is a foundational principle, but Abu al-Husayn is noted as dissenting; additionally, if an act occurs by God's power and then by the servant's power identically, that would be a logical impossibility (tahsil al-hasil).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If it is impossible for one created thing to have two creators, then in which areas of your life are you still trying to take credit for what only Allah created—your wealth, your talents, your righteous deeds? How can you genuinely acknowledge His sole creatorship in your heart today?
?
The tahsil al-hasil argument shows that once an act exists by Allah's power, your attempting to produce the same act is meaningless. When you commit a sin, do you truly accept that Allah created that act, yet you are accountable? How does that reality shape your repentance — do you blame yourself or Him?

The Quranic Proof that No One Creates Like God
The author presents the second proof from the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 'Or have they assigned partners who created like His creation?' serves as a proof that humans are not creators of their actions.

Core Idea
If humans created, their creation would be like God's—but the verse denies that.

The author draws on a powerful Quranic verse to argue that no one creates like God. If humans were truly creators of their actions, their acts of creation would mirror God's creative act, bringing something from non-existence to existence. The Quran's rhetorical denial proves this cannot be. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'الحجة الثانية' to 'ذلك باطل'. Notice how the author sets up the inference from the verse.
2
Understand the inference
The verse is an interrogative of denial. It implies that no creator has created like God. If humans were creators, their creation would be like God's, making the denial false. Therefore, humans are not creators.
Focus on the logic of 'لو كان العبد خالقاً'.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states that the text indicates this is false. Ask yourself: why does the author consider this a definitive proof?
Consider that the verse comes from Allah and thus is the ultimate authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قوله تعالى : ( أم جعلوا الله شركاء . خلقوا كخلقه ) وجه الاستدلال : إن هذا استفهام بمعنى الإنكار ، فكان مقتضاه أنه لم يوجد خالق خلق كخلق الله ، ولو كان العبد خالقاً لأفعاله ، لكان خلقه كخلق الله لما ثبت أن الذي يخلقه العبد ، فالله تعالى يخلق مثل ذلك الشيء . وأيضاً فخلق الله تعالى ، لما كان إخراجاً من العدم إلى الوجود ، وفعل العبد أيضاً كذلك ، كان كل واحد من الفعلين شبيه الآخر ، فكان خلق العبد كخلق الله تعالى . وقد بينا : أن النص يدل على أن ذلك باطل . فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن العبد لو كان خالقاً، لكان خلقه، كخلق الله .
وبيانه من وجوه :
الأول : إن فعل العبد . إما أن يكون طاعة ، أو معصية ، أو عبثاً .
وفعل الله ليس كذلك
الثاني : إن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير - على ما تقدم ذكره ـ والتقدير عبارة عن إيقاع الفعل على مقدار مخصوص مطابق للغرض والمصلحة . وهذا إنما يتأتى في حق العالم ، الذي لا يجهل شيئا ، والقادر الذي لا يعجز عن شيء . أما العبد فإنه في الأكثر يقدر شيئاً ، ويجيء فعله بخلاف ما قدره . وحينئذ لا يكون خلق العبد ، مثلا لخلق الله
الثالث : إن قوله تعالى : ( خلقوا كخلقه ) إنما يتناول من كان خلقه ، مساوياً لكل ما كان خلقاً الله تعالى . لأن قوله : ( كخلقه ) يتناول جميع مخلوقات الله تعالى. ومعلوم أن العبد ليس كذلك ، فوجب أن لا يندرج تحت
النص
أعلم .
•
وبقية الأسئلة على هذه الحجة، كما تقدم على الحجة الأولى . والله
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إن الحركة عبارة عن حصول الجوهر في حيز ، بعد أن كان في حيز آخر . هذا هو المعقول من ماهية الحركة وحقيقتها . فإذا فعل العبد مثل هذا الشيء ، ثم إنه تعالى أيضا فعله مثله ، فحينئذ يكون خلق العبد مثلاً لخلق الله تعالى ، على هذا التقدير
وأما السؤال الثاني . فجوابه : إن أفعال العباد ، وإن كانت لا توجد على وفق تقديرهم على الدوام، إلا أنها قد توجد على وفق تقديرهم في بعض الأوقات . وحينئذ يكون ذلك الخلق في تلك المرة ، مثلاً لخلق الله
وأما السؤال الثالث . فجوابه : إن قوله : « كخلقه ، لا يوجب العموم . لأنه إشارة إلى المصور، فيكفي في العمل به ثبوته في صورة واحدة. فإذا كان خلق العبد مثلاً لخلق الله تعالى مرة واحدة ، وقد صدق عليه في تلك المرة أنه خلق مثل خلق الله تعالى، فحينئذ يندرج تحت النص . والله أعلم .
The second proof: His statement Exalted is He: (Or have they assigned to God partners who created like His creation?) The aspect of inference: This is an interrogative meaning denial, so its implication is that there is no creator who created like God's creation. And if the servant were a creator of his actions, his creation would be like God's creation because it is established that what the servant creates, God Exalted is He creates the like of that thing. And also, since God's creation is bringing forth from non-existence to existence, and the servant's action is also like that, each of the two actions is similar to the other, so the servant's creation would be like God's creation. And we have shown that the text indicates that this is false. So if it is said: We do not accept that if the servant were a creator, his creation would be like God's creation. And its explanation from aspects: The first: The servant's action is either obedience, or disobedience, or futility. And God's action is not like that. The second: Surely creation is estimation (taqdīr) - as previously mentioned - and estimation is the execution of an action according to a particular measure conforming to the purpose and benefit. This is only attainable in the case of the Knower who does not ignore anything and the Powerful who is incapable of nothing. As for the servant, he mostly intends something and his action comes contrary to what he intended. Then the servant's creation is not like God's creation. The third: Surely His statement Exalted is He: (created like His creation) only includes one whose creation is equal to all of what is God's creation. Because His statement: (like His creation) includes all of God's creations. And it is known that the servant is not like that, so necessarily he does not fall under the text. (And God knows best.) And the remaining questions on this proof are as previously on the first proof. And God knows best. And the answer to the first question: Surely motion (al-Ḥara) is the occurrence of the substance (al-jawhar) in a location (ḥayyiz) after it was in another location. This is the conceptualized meaning of the essence of motion. So if the servant does such a thing, and then He Exalted is He also does its like, then the servant's creation would be like God's creation according to this estimation. As for the second question, its answer: Surely the actions of servants, even if they do not occur according to their estimation always, they do occur according to their estimation at some times. Then that creation at that time is like God's creation. As for the third question, its answer: Surely His statement: "like His creation" does not necessitate generality. Because it is a reference to the form (al-muṣawwar), so it is sufficient for acting by it that it is established in one form. So if the servant's creation is like God's creation once, and it is true of it at that time that it is a creation like God's creation, then it falls under the text. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a proof followed by three objections and their answers. The author first presents a Quranic proof: the verse 'Or have they assigned to God partners who created like His creation?' is a rhetorical denial, meaning no creator has created like God. If humans were creators of their actions, their creation would be like God's (both bring from non-existence to existence), making the verse false. Therefore, humans are not creators. Then the opponent raises three objections: (1) Human actions are either obedience, disobedience, or futility, while God's actions are not; thus they are not similar. (2) Creation is estimation (تقدير), and humans often fail to execute their estimation, so their acts are not like God's. (3) The phrase 'like His creation' (كخلقه) encompasses all of God's creation; human creation is not equal to all of it, so it does not fall under the verse. The author then answers each: (1) He defines motion as the substance's occurrence in a location after another; if the servant produces such motion and God also produces a like motion, then in that instance the servant's creation is like God's. (2) Even if humans generally fall short, sometimes their actions do conform to their estimation, and in those cases it is like God's creation. (3) 'Like His creation' does not imply totality—it refers to a form; if the servant's act matches God's once, it suffices to fall under the text. Thus the proof stands, and the objections are refuted.

استفهام بمعنى الإنكار
خلق

A) To ask a question
B) To express denial
C) To express surprise
D) To emphasize

Key Takeaway
The verse denies that any creation resembles God's, proving humans are not creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform — including every sin and every good deed — is not of your own creation but solely from God? If so, how does this belief affect your sense of personal responsibility and your reliance on Him? Are you willing to accept that your own efforts are not the ultimate cause of your actions?
?
If your actions are not your creation, then what does it mean for you to be accountable before God? Does this make you more humble, recognizing that even your obedience is a gift from Him, or does it tempt you to be complacent? Reflect on a specific action today that you thought was 'yours' — can you now see God's role in it?
?
Consider a sin you committed recently. In light of this lesson, you have no power to create that sin; rather, God allowed it to occur. Does this realization increase your reliance on His mercy and your urgency to repent, or does it lead you to excuse yourself? What concrete change will you make in your heart or practice as a result?

Objections and Their Refutations
The author refutes three objections to the Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections to the proof and how the author refutes each.

Core Idea
Objections based on quality, estimation, and generality are answered by precise definitions.

After presenting the proof, the author anticipates three objections: that human actions are qualitatively different, that creation requires perfect estimation, and that 'like His creation' implies total similarity. Each objection is met with a careful rebuttal rooted in the nature of motion and language.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the three objections starting from 'فإن قيل' to 'أن لا يندرج تحت النص'. Notice how each objection targets a specific aspect of the proof.
The three objections are: (1) Difference in moral quality, (2) Imperfect estimation, (3) Generality of 'like His creation'.
2
Study the first objection and its answer
The first objection claims human actions are only obedience, disobedience, or futility, unlike God's actions. The answer defines motion as a neutral change of location, showing that both God and servant can produce the same kind of motion.
Note the definition of motion: 'حصول الجوهر في حيز بعد أن كان في حيز آخر'.
3
Study the second objection and its answer
The second objection says creation requires precise estimation, which humans lack. The answer concedes humans often fail but sometimes succeed, and in those instances their creation matches God's.
Focus on 'قد توجد على وفق تقديرهم في بعض الأوقات'.
4
Study the third objection and its answer
The third objection claims 'like His creation' must apply to all of God's creation. The answer explains that it refers to a single form, so one occurrence suffices.
The key is that 'كخلقه' does not necessitate generality.

?

Seventh Proof: The Verse and the Objection
Al-Razi presents the seventh Quranic proof and the Mu'tazilite objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh Quranic proof for divine creation of human actions and the Mu'tazilite objection that seeks to limit its scope.

Core Idea
The verse denies any creator who provides from the sky, but opponents restrict it.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the seventh proof from the Quran, citing the verse 'Is there any creator besides Allah who provides for you from the sky?' This verse seems to assert that only Allah creates and provides. However, the Mu'tazila object that this verse only denies a creator who provides from the sky, not a creator as such. In this lesson, we examine the verse and the objection that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof verse
Read the first sentence of the passage, which presents the seventh proof from the Quran. Understand that the verse links creation and provision.
The verse is from Surah Fatir, 3.
2
Examine the objection
The passage then presents an objection. Note that the opponent claims the verse only denies a creator who provides, not a creator absolutely. They also argue that even if it denies any creator besides Allah, we still call the slave 'mujid' and 'muhdith', not 'khalq'.
3
Understand the definition of khalq
The objection defines 'khalq' as estimation free from defect, which is impossible for the slave. See how this definition is used to avoid the verse's implication.
This definition is crucial for the second answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : قوله تعالى : هل من خالق غير الله ، يرزقكم من السماء (۲) ) فإن قيل : هذا يدل على أنه لا خالق غير الله يرزقنا من السماء ، ولا يدل على أنه لا خالق غير الله. سلمنا : أن هذه الآية تنافي إثبات خالق غير الله ، لكن العبد نسميه بالموجد والمحدث ، ولا نسميه بالخالق ، لأن الخلق عبارة عن التقدير العاري عن وجوه الخلل . وذلك في حق العبد محال والجواب عن الأول : إنه لو وجد خالق غير الله ، لوجد خالق غير الله يرزقنا من السماء ، لأنه يقال : رزق السلطان فلانا ، إذا أمكنه من التصرف فيها جعله رزقاً له . فكذا ههنا ، الملائكة الذين هم سكان السموات ، إذا سعوا في إنزال الأمطار ، فقد مكنوا أهل الأرض من الانتفاع بها . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : على مذهب الخصم : الملائكة يخلقون أفعال أنفسهم . وهم أيضاً يرزقون أهل الأرض على التفسير الذي ذكرناه ، وحينئذ يلزم القطع بأنه وجد خالق غير الله يَرزقنا من السماء ، فثبت : أنه لو كان العبد خالقاً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان المحذور لازماً . والجواب عن الثاني : إن الخالق هو الموجد المقدر ، وكما أن الله تعالى يقدر بعلمه التام ، فكذلك العبد يقدر بحسب ظنه وخياله
The seventh proof: His statement, the Exalted: 'Is there any creator besides Allah who provides for you from the sky?' Then it is said: This indicates that there is no creator besides Allah who provides for us from the sky, and it does not indicate that there is no creator besides Allah. We grant that this verse precludes affirming a creator besides Allah, but we call the slave the 'exister' and 'innovator', and we do not call him 'creator', because 'creation' (khalq) is an expression for estimation devoid of flaws, and that is impossible in the case of the slave. The answer to the first [point]: If there existed a creator besides Allah, then there would exist a creator besides Allah who provides for us from the sky, because it is said: 'The sultan provided for so-and-so' when he enabled him to dispose of it and made it a provision for him. Similarly here, the angels who are inhabitants of the heavens, if they strive to send down rain, they have enabled the people of the earth to benefit from it. If this is established, we say: According to the doctrine of the opponent, the angels create their own actions, and they also provide for the people of the earth according to the interpretation we mentioned. Then necessarily it follows that there exists a creator besides Allah who provides for us from the sky. Thus it is established that if the slave were the creator of his own actions, the harm would be necessary. The answer to the second [point]: The creator is the one who brings into existence and estimates. Just as Allah, the Exalted, estimates with His perfect knowledge, similarly the slave estimates according to his opinion and imagination.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quran for the doctrine that Allah is the sole creator of all things, including human actions. The author presents the seventh proof, citing the verse 'Is there any creator besides Allah who provides for you from the sky?' (Surah Fatir, 3). He then anticipates an objection from the opponent (likely the Mu'tazila) who argues: this verse only denies a creator who provides from the sky, not a creator in general. Moreover, even if it negates any creator besides Allah, we can still call the slave a 'bringer into existence' (mujid) and 'originator' (muhdith) but not 'creator' (khalq), because 'creation' is defined as 'estimation free from defect', which is impossible for the slave. The author gives two answers. First: If there were a creator besides Allah, then there would be a creator besides Allah who provides from the sky, because 'providing' (rizq) can be understood as enabling someone to benefit. For example, the angels who cause rain are enabling people to benefit, and on the opponent's view, angels create their own actions (e.g., moving the clouds). Thus, if angels create their own actions and provide rain, then there is a creator besides Allah providing from the sky, which contradicts the verse. Therefore, if the slave creates his own actions, the same contradiction would arise. Second answer: The true meaning of 'creator' is the one who brings into existence and estimates. Allah estimates with perfect knowledge, while the slave estimates based on his opinion and imagination. Thus, the slave does not truly create.

Key Terms
خالق
الرزق
الموجد
المحدث
الخلق
التقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse proves that there is no creator at all besides Allah, without needing to consider that it specifically mentions provision from the sky.
Correction: The opponent argues that the verse only denies a creator who also provides from the sky, not a creator in general. This passage presents that objection and prepares to answer it.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what do the Mu'tazila call the slave?
A) Creator
B) Exister and innovator
C) Provider
D) Estimator

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof begins with a Quranic verse that seems to deny any creator besides Allah, but the Mu'tazila object by limiting its scope and redefining 'creation'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, your career, or your family's future, do you ever feel like you are the ultimate 'estimator' who can foresee every flaw? How does the lesson's definition of khalq as flawless estimation expose the arrogance in your own planning? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge that only Allah's estimation is perfect?
?
The opponent claims that a slave can be called an 'exister' or 'innovator' but not a 'creator'. Consider your own achievements: which ones do you secretly attribute to your own flawless execution? How does recognizing that only Allah creates without defect change the way you thank Him for your successes? Write down one success you had this week and repent of any pride you felt about it.
