Answering the Objection: Two Refutations
Al-Razi gives two answers to the objection, using angels and the definition of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's two answers to the Mu'tazilite objection from the seventh proof.

Core Idea
The two answers show that the Mu'tazilite position leads to contradiction and misapplies the term 'creation'.

In the previous lesson, we saw the seventh Quranic proof and the objection that the verse only denies a creator who provides from the sky. Now, Al-Razi presents two answers to this objection. The first answer demonstrates that if the slave were a creator, then there would be a creator besides Allah who provides from the sky, defeating the opponent's own logic. The second answer clarifies the true meaning of 'creation' as bringing into existence with precise estimation, which only Allah does perfectly.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer
Read the first answer carefully. Notice how Al-Razi uses the angels as an example. According to the Mu'tazila, angels create their own actions, and they also cause rain, thus they would be creators providing from the sky. This contradicts the verse.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Read the second answer
The second answer focuses on the definition of 'creator'. Al-Razi argues that true creation requires perfect estimation, which is only for Allah. The slave's estimation is based on opinion and imagination, so he is not a creator.
This answer refutes the definitional distinction made in the objection.
3
Synthesize the two answers
Consider how these two answers together refute the objection. The first shows practical contradiction, the second shows conceptual misunderstanding. Reflect on the strength of the proof.

Source Text

The Eighth Proof: Negation of Creation by Objects of Worship
Al-Razi presents the eighth Quranic proof using the grammatical rule of indefinite noun in negation to show that none besides Allah creates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses the Quranic verse and Arabic grammatical principles to prove that no being other than Allah creates anything, and how this refutes polytheism.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in a negative context proves universal negation of creation by other than Allah.

This lesson presents al-Razi's eighth proof from the Quran, grounded in the Arabic grammatical rule that an indefinite noun in a negative context indicates absolute generality. By analyzing the verse about objects of worship, al-Razi demonstrates that no creature—be it angel, prophet, or tyrant—can create anything, thus affirming tawhid al-khaliq. You'll see how this argument uses linguistic principles familiar even to the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its context
Start by reading the cited verse from Surah An-Nahl (16:20) and the translation. Notice that it speaks about those called upon besides Allah.
The verse explicitly denies creation to these beings and states they themselves are created.
2
Identify the grammatical rule
Al-Razi explains that the phrase 'do not create anything' (لا يخلقون شيئاً) contains an indefinite noun (شيئاً) in a negative context, which in Arabic grammar conveys generality (عموم). This means it negates all creation, not just some.
This rule is standard in Arabic grammar and accepted by all schools, including the Mu'tazila.
3
See the rule applied to the verse
Al-Razi gives everyday analogies to show how this works. Then he applies it: the verse means that none of the beings worshipped besides Allah—like the Messiah, angels, Pharaoh, or Nimrod—created anything at all.
The examples show the universality of the negation.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
The argument firmly establishes that only Allah is the Creator, refuting any claim that other beings share in creation. This is a foundational point for tawhid.
Al-Razi notes that even the Mu'tazila rely on this same grammatical principle in their own doctrines about the threat (al-wa'id).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قوله تعالى : ( والذين يدعون من دون الله ، لا يخلقون شيئاً . وهم يخلقون (۱) ) ومعلوم : أنه قد دعى من دونه : المسيح ، والملائكة ، وفرعون ، ونمرود . وهذا النص يقتضي أن واحداً من هؤلاء لم يخلق شيئا . لأن قوله : ( لا يخلقون شيئاً ) : نكرة في النفي ، فيفيد العموم . كما إذا قال الرجل : ما رأيت شيئا ، وما سمعت خبراً ، وما أكلت لقمة ، فإنـه يفيد العموم . وأيضاً : قوله : ( والذين يدعون من دون الله ) يفيد العموم في الكل . لأنا بينا في أصول الفقه : أن هذه الصفة تفيد العموم ، والمعتزلة يسلمون ذلك ، وعليه بنوا مذاهبهم في مسألة الوعيد
The eighth proof: His statement, the Exalted: 'And those whom they call upon besides Allah do not create anything, and they themselves are created.' It is known that there have been called upon besides Him: the Messiah, the angels, Pharaoh, and Nimrod. This text necessitates that not one of these created anything, because His saying 'do not create anything' is an indefinite noun in a negative context, which conveys generality. Just as when a man says: 'I did not see anything, I did not hear any news, I did not eat any morsel' – it conveys generality. Also, His saying 'and those whom they call upon besides Allah' conveys generality over all of them. For we have explained in Usul al-Fiqh that this description (al-sifa) conveys generality, and the Mu'tazila concede that, and upon it they built their doctrines in the matter of the threat (al-wa'id).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quran that no one besides Allah creates. The author presents the eighth proof, citing the verse 'And those whom they call upon besides Allah do not create anything, and they themselves are created' (Surah An-Nahl, 20). He argues that the phrase 'do not create anything' (لا يخلقون شيئاً) is an indefinite noun in a negative context, which according to Arabic grammar indicates absolute generality – it negates any creation whatsoever. He gives an analogy: if a man says 'I did not see anything', it means he saw nothing at all. Similarly, the verse negates all creation by any being other than Allah. He further notes that the expression 'those whom they call upon besides Allah' is a description that also indicates generality, covering all such beings like the Messiah, angels, Pharaoh, and Nimrod. He points out that even the Mu'tazila accept this grammatical principle of generality in negative contexts, and they rely on it in their own doctrines regarding the threat of punishment (al-wa'id). Thus, the verse clearly proves that no one other than Allah is a creator.

Key Terms
نكرة في النفي
العموم
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the verse only refers to specific idols or false gods, not to all beings worshipped besides Allah.
Correction: Al-Razi uses grammatical principles to show the verse applies universally to all beings called upon besides Allah, including angels, prophets, and tyrants.

Checkpoint
How does the author argue that 'do not create anything' (لا يخلقون شيئاً) indicates absolute negation of creation?
A) Because it is preceded by a negative particle.
B) Because it is an indefinite noun in a negative context, which conveys generality.
C) Because it is a verb in the present tense.
D) Because it is followed by 'and they themselves are created'.

Key Takeaway
The verse conclusively proves that no being other than Allah creates, using the linguistic principle that an indefinite noun in a negative context indicates absolute generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself looking to creation—people, circumstances, or your own abilities—as the ultimate source of change in your life? How does Al-Razi's grammatical argument that no one besides Allah creates challenge that tendency? What will you do today to redirect your trust to the Creator alone?

The verse says that those called upon besides Allah 'do not create anything, and they themselves are created.' Reflect on something or someone you have been relying on as if they could 'create' good or harm for you. Are you willing to consciously turn to the Creator alone, and what specific step will you take within the next 24 hours to demonstrate that reliance?

Ninth Quranic Proof: The Challenge to Show Creation
Al-Razi presents the ninth Quranic proof with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the Quranic challenge to prove that only Allah creates human actions, and how he answers the objection that humans are bringers into existence.

Core Idea
The Quranic challenge to show what others created proves that only Allah creates—even disbelievers never claimed to create their own actions.

Al-Razi now presents a ninth proof from the Quran. He cites the verse 'This is the creation of Allah, so show me what those besides Him have created' and argues that if humans were creators of their own actions, disbelievers would have claimed that when challenged. Their silence is evidence that they are not creators. He then addresses the objection that humans are 'bringers into existence' (mujid) not creators.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse
Begin by reading the verse quoted: 'This is the creation of Allah, so show me what those besides Him have created.' Notice the challenge.
This verse from Surah Luqman is the foundation of the argument.
2
Understand the interpretation of 'show me'
Al-Razi clarifies that 'show me' (أروني) here means 'knowledge' and intellectual recognition, not physical sight. The context is distinguishing Allah's essence by the attribute of being Creator.
This interpretation is crucial for the argument to hold.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The author presents a logical conditional: if a living being were the creator of its own actions, then disbelievers would have said 'We create our volitions, thoughts, movements, etc.' when hearing the verses. Since they did not, the conditional is false.
This is a proof by contradiction: the lack of claim proves lack of creatorship.
4
Examine the objection and answer
An objector says the slave is not a creator but a 'bringer into existence' (mujid). Al-Razi dismisses this by referring to his earlier argument, where he likely distinguished between true creation and mere bringing into existence.
The answer is brief, pointing back to prior discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : ( هذا خلق الله . فأروني ماذا خلق الذين من دونه (۲)) والمراد من هذه الرؤية : العلم . لأن سياق الآية يدل على أن المراد منه : تميز ذات الله تعالى عن سائر الذوات بصفة الخالقية . وهذا التميز لا يحصل إلا إذا حملنا هذه الرؤية على العلم . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان الحيوان خالقاً لفعل نفسه ، لوجب أن يقول الكفار عند سماع هذه الآيات : إنا قد خلقنا الإرادات والكراهات والخواطر والحركات والسكنات ، فلما لم يقل أحد من الكفار ذلك ، علمنا أنه لا خالق إلا الله . فإن قالوا : العبد ليس خالقاً لفعل نفسه ، وإن كان موجداً لها . فالجواب : ما سبق . وبالله التوفيق
The ninth proof: His statement, the Exalted: 'This is the creation of Allah, so show me what those besides Him have created.' The intended meaning of this 'showing' is knowledge, because the context of the verse indicates that the intended purpose is to distinguish the essence of Allah, the Exalted, from all other essences by the attribute of being Creator. This distinction cannot be obtained unless we interpret this 'showing' as knowledge. If this is established, we say: If an animal were the creator of its own action, then the disbelievers would be obliged, upon hearing these verses, to say: 'We have created our volitions, aversions, thoughts, movements, and stillnesses.' Since no disbeliever said that, we know that there is no creator except Allah. If they say: 'The slave is not a creator of his own action, even if he is its bringer into existence,' then the answer is what preceded. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quran for the exclusive creatorship of Allah, focusing on human actions. The author presents the ninth proof, citing the verse 'This is the creation of Allah, so show me what those besides Him have created' (Surah Luqman, 11). He first clarifies that 'show me' (أروني) here means 'make known' or 'knowledge', not physical sight, because the context is distinguishing Allah's essence by the attribute of being Creator. This distinction can only be made through knowledge. Then he argues: if a living being (including humans) were the creator of its own actions, then when disbelievers hear this verse challenging them to show what they have created, they would necessarily respond by saying 'We have created our volitions, aversions, thoughts, movements, and stillnesses' – because they would indeed be creators of those internal and external acts. But no disbeliever ever made such a claim. Therefore, it is known that no one creates except Allah. The author then anticipates an objection: 'The slave is not a creator of his own action, even if he is its bringer into existence (mujid).' The author dismisses this by referring to his previous answer (likely earlier in the text), where he argued that the true creator is the one who brings into existence and estimates, and that the slave's estimation is imperfect. He ends with 'And with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
خلق
أروني
العلم
الخالقية
موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'show me' (أروني) in the verse requires a physical demonstration of things created by others.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that it means 'knowledge' and intellectual recognition, because the context is distinguishing Allah's essence by the attribute of being Creator.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, why does the fact that disbelievers did not claim to create their own actions prove that only Allah creates?
A) Because disbelievers are always truthful about their beliefs.
B) Because if they were creators, they would have been obliged to mention that when challenged by the verse.
C) Because the verse explicitly states that no one else creates.
D) Because the disbelievers admitted that they do not create.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic challenge to show what others have created is a proof that only Allah creates, because if humans were creators, disbelievers would have claimed that when challenged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever inwardly taken pride in an achievement as if you alone created it? How does this verse challenge that feeling of ownership?
?
If you truly believed that every thought, movement, and stillness is created by Allah, what would change about how you start your day or react to setbacks?
?
The disbelievers did not claim to create their own actions—yet you, a believer, sometimes behave as though you do. Will you today ask Allah to make you constantly aware that He is the Creator of every moment of your life?

The Eighth Proof: The Verse of Those Who Call upon Other than Allah
Sanusi presents the eighth proof from the Quran denying creation to any besides Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur’anic verse 'they create nothing' serves as a proof that no one besides Allah creates anything, using the linguistic principle of generality derived from an indefinite noun in a negative context.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in a negative context denies everything — including human 'creation' of actions.

This lesson presents the eighth proof from the Quran, where Sanusi draws on a verse from Surah al-Nahl to refute the idea that any being other than Allah can create. The argument hinges on a subtle linguistic point: when an indefinite noun appears in a negative sentence, it negates the entire category. This same principle is accepted by the Mu'tazila themselves, making it a powerful rebuttal within the kalam tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Sanusi's argument. Note the verse citation and the examples he gives.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze the linguistic principle
Focus on the phrase 'la yakhluquna shay'an' (they create nothing). Understand why 'shay'an' as an indefinite noun in a negative context indicates absolute generality (umum). Compare with everyday examples like 'I haven't seen anything.'
This is the key to the proof.
3
Examine the second generality argument
Notice that Sanusi also argues that the descriptive phrase 'those who invoke besides Allah' is general. He references usul al-fiqh to support this.
Both arguments reinforce each other.
4
Consider the Mu'tazila concession
Reflect on why Sanusi points out that the Mu'tazila accept this principle. How does this strengthen the proof against their own position?
This shows self-consistency within the kalam framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : قوله تعالى : ( والذين يدعون من دون الله ، لا يخلقون شيئاً . وهم يخلقون ) ومعلوم : أنه قد دعى من دونه : المسيح ، والملائكة ، وفرعون ، ونمرود . وهذا النص يقتضي أن واحداً من هؤلاء لم يخلق شيئا . لأن قوله : ( لا يخلقون شيئاً ) : نكرة في النفي ، فيفيد العموم . كما إذا قال الرجل : ما رأيت شيئا ، وما سمعت خبراً ، وما أكلت لقمة ، فإنـه يفيد العموم . وأيضاً : قوله : ( والذين يدعون من دون الله ) يفيد العموم في الكل . لأنا بينا في أصول الفقه : أن هذه الصفة تفيد العموم ، والمعتزلة يسلمون ذلك ، وعليه بنوا مذاهبهم في مسألة الوعيد
The eighth proof: His statement, the Most High: 'And those they invoke instead of Allah create nothing, and they themselves are created.' And it is known that among those invoked besides Him are the Messiah, the angels, Pharaoh, and Nimrod. This text requires that not one of these created anything. Because His statement 'they create nothing' is an indefinite noun (nukra) in a negative context, thus indicating generality (umum), just as when a man says 'I have not seen anything,' 'I have not heard any news,' 'I have not eaten a mouthful'—this indicates generality. Also, His statement 'those who invoke instead of Allah' indicates generality over all. For we have explained in usul al-fiqh that this descriptive phrase indicates generality, and the Mu'tazila concede that, and upon it they built their doctrines in the matter of al-wa'id (the threat).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is the eighth proof (al-hujja al-thamina) from the Quran against the claim that humans create their own actions. The proof cites Surah al-Nahl (16:20): 'And those they invoke instead of Allah create nothing, and they themselves are created.' Sanusi argues that this verse denies creation to all beings invoked besides Allah, such as the Messiah, angels, Pharaoh, and Nimrod. The key linguistic point is that the phrase 'they create nothing' (la yakhluquna shay'an) uses an indefinite noun (shay'an) in a negative context, which in Arabic grammar indicates absolute generality (umum). This is similar to saying 'I haven't seen anything'—it denies seeing any thing whatsoever. Additionally, the descriptive phrase 'those who invoke instead of Allah' also indicates generality according to usul al-fiqh. Sanusi notes that even the Mu'tazila accept this principle and base their own doctrine of al-wa'id (the threat of punishment) on it. Thus, the verse proves that no created being—including humans—can be a creator of any action.

عموم
الوعيد

Misconception: The verse only applies to idols, not to humans or other beings invoked besides Allah.
Correction: Sanusi specifically includes the Messiah, angels, Pharaoh, and Nimrod, showing that the negation covers all beings invoked besides Allah, including humans.

Checkpoint

B) The word 'shay'an' is an indefinite noun in a negative context.
C) The word 'shay'an' is a general noun.
D) The word 'shay'an' is a specific noun.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'they create nothing' denies all creation to any being besides Allah, using the linguistic principle that an indefinite noun in a negative context indicates absolute generality.

?

Ninth Proof: The Verse 'Show Me What Those besides Him Have Created'
Sanusi presents the ninth proof from the Quran challenging others to show any creation besides Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the Quranic challenge to prove that only Allah creates actions, including internal actions like wills and thoughts.

Core Idea
The Quranic challenge proves that even disbelievers never claimed to create their own actions.

Sanusi presents a powerful Quranic argument against human creation of actions. He interprets the verse's 'showing' as knowledge, then argues that if humans were creators, disbelievers would have responded by claiming creation of internal actions. Since they didn't, only Allah creates. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi structures his proof: verse, interpretation, conditional argument, objection, and answer.
The passage is concise; each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Understand the interpretation of 'show'
Sanusi explains that 'show' (رؤية) means knowledge ('ilm), not physical sight, because the context requires distinguishing Allah's essence by the attribute of creation. Understand why this interpretation is necessary for the proof.
This is a key hermeneutical move.
3
Follow the conditional argument
Sanusi argues: if any living being were a creator of its own action, disbelievers would have responded to the verse by claiming to create wills, thoughts, etc. Since they did not, it proves only Allah creates. Trace the logic step by step.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Consider the objection and answer
The objection is that the servant brings actions into existence (موجد) without being a creator. Sanusi's answer is 'what has preceded'—referring to earlier arguments that equate creation with bringing into existence. Reflect on why this answer suffices.
This connects to previous lessons on the nature of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : ( هذا خلق الله . فأروني ماذا خلق الذين من دونه ) والمراد من هذه الرؤية : العلم . لأن سياق الآية يدل على أن المراد منه : تميز ذات الله تعالى عن سائر الذوات بصفة الخالقية . وهذا التميز لا يحصل إلا إذا حملنا هذه الرؤية على العلم . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان الحيوان خالقاً لفعل نفسه ، لوجب أن يقول الكفار عند سماع هذه الآيات : إنا قد خلقنا الإرادات والكراهات والخواطر والحركات والسكنات ، فلما لم يقل أحد من الكفار ذلك ، علمنا أنه لا خالق إلا الله . فإن قالوا : العبد ليس خالقاً لفعل نفسه ، وإن كان موجداً لها . فالجواب : ما سبق . وبالله التوفيق
The ninth proof: His statement, the Most High: 'This is the creation of Allah; so show me what those besides Him have created.' And the intended meaning of this 'showing' is knowledge (al-ilm), because the context of the verse indicates that what is meant by it is the distinction of the essence of Allah, the Most High, from other essences by the attribute of creation (al-khaliqiyya). And this distinction is not attained unless we interpret this 'showing' as knowledge. And when this is established, we say: If an animal (al-hayawan) were the creator of its own action, then the disbelievers would have to say upon hearing these verses: 'We have created wills, aversions, thoughts, movements, and stillnesses.' And since none of the disbelievers said that, we know that there is no creator except Allah. If they say: 'The servant is not a creator of his own action, though he is its bringer-into-existence (mujid),' then the answer is: what has preceded (ma sabaq). And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is the ninth proof (al-hujja al-tasi'a) from the Quran against human creation of actions. The verse from Surah Luqman (31:11) says: 'This is the creation of Allah; so show me what those besides Him have created.' Sanusi first explains that 'show' (ru'ya) here means knowledge (ilm) rather than physical sight, because the context requires distinguishing Allah's essence from other essences by the attribute of creation (al-khaliqiyya), and this distinction is a matter of knowledge. Having established this, he argues: if any living being (al-hayawan, including humans) were the creator of its own actions, then disbelievers, upon hearing this challenge, would have responded by claiming that they create wills (iradat), aversions (karahat), thoughts (khawatif), movements (harakat), and stillnesses (sakanat). Since no disbeliever ever made such a claim despite the Quranic challenge being recited to them, we know that there is no creator but Allah. A potential objection is then raised: the servant (al-'abd) is not a creator (khalid) of his own action, although he is its bringer-into-existence (mujid). The answer given is simply 'what has preceded' (ma sabaq), referring to earlier arguments that established that bringing into existence without being a creator is impossible or that the concept of bringing into existence is inseparable from creation. Thus, the proof stands.

Key Terms

الإرادات
الكراهات
الخواطر
الحركات
السكنات
الموجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only challenges physical creation, not internal actions like thoughts and wills.
Correction: Sanusi includes the creation of wills, aversions, thoughts, movements, and stillnesses as examples of what disbelievers would have claimed if they were creators. Thus, the proof covers all actions, internal and external.

Checkpoint

B) Because they wanted to boast about their power.
C) Because they were asked by the Prophet to do so.
D) Because they needed to prove their gods were creators.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves from the Quranic challenge that no one besides Allah creates actions, including internal actions like wills and thoughts.

This verse challenges you to examine your heart: Do you live as if you are the source of your actions, or do you submit to the reality that Allah is the sole creator? What concrete step can you take today to surrender control over your actions to Him?

Tenth Proof: The Verse 'Allah Created You and What You Do' and an Objection
Sanusi presents the tenth proof and the beginning of an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth proof for human actions being created by Allah based on a grammatical interpretation of the verse, and the objections raised against it.

Core Idea
The tenth proof hinges on whether 'ma' in 'wa ma ta'malun' is a verbal noun or relative pronoun.

Sanusi presents a proof from Quran 37:96, where grammarians interpret 'ma ta'malun' as a verbal noun, implying Allah creates your actions. However, an immediate objection challenges this interpretation on multiple grounds, including the verse's rebuking context and grammatical disagreements between Sibawayh and al-Akhfash. This lesson explores the proof and the first part of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic verse and Sanusi's inference using the grammatical rule that 'ma' with its clause is in the position of a verbal noun.
2
Understand the objection's first point
The objector argues that the verse rebukes idol worshippers, implying they are true agents of their actions, otherwise rebuke would be meaningless.
3
Examine the grammatical dispute
Compare Sibawayh and al-Akhfash's positions on the construction 'ma qumta' meaning 'your standing' – only Sibawayh allows it broadly, while al-Akhfash restricts it to transitive verbs.
This shows the grammatical point is not settled.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The objector suggests that 'ma' could be a relative pronoun meaning 'what you carve' (the carved idols) rather than the act of carving, supported by another verse.
This undercuts the proof's foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله تعالى : ( والله خلقكم . وما تعملون ) وجه الاستدلال : أن النحويين قالوا : لفظ ( ما ) مع ما بعده في تقدير المصدر فقوله : ( وما تعملون ( معناه : وعملكم. وعلى هذا التقدير ، صار معنى الآية : والله خلقكم وخلق عملكم . فإن قيل : هذه الآية حجة عليكم من وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى إنما ذكر هذه الآية توبيخاً لهم على عبادة الأصنام ، لأنه تعالى بين أنه خالقهم وخالق لتلك الأصنام، والخالق هو المستحق للعبادة دون المخلوق ، فلما تركوا عبادته - سبحانه - مع أنه خالقهم . ثم عبدوا الأصنام، لا أنه - سبحانه - وبخهم على هذا الخطأ العظيم . فقال : ( أتعبدون ما تنحتون . والله خلقكم ؟ وما تعملون ؟ ) ولو لم يكونوا فاعلين لأفعالهم وموجدين لها ، لما جاز توبيخهم عليها . سلمنا : أن هذه الآية ليست حجة عليكم . لكن لا نسلم أنها حجة لكم . أما قوله : « إن كلمة ( ما » مع ما بعده في تقدير المصدر، قلنا : هذا ممنوع. وبيانه : أن ( سيبويه ) و ( الأخفش : اختلفا في أنه هل يجوز أن يقال : أعجبني ما قمت . أي قيامك ؟ فجوزه ( سيبويه : ومنعه ( الأخفش ، وزعم : أن هذا لا يجوز إلا في الفعل المتعدي . وذلك يدل على أن « ما ، مع ما بعده في تقدير المفعول عند « الأخفش : سلمنا : أن ذلك قد يكون بمعنى المصدر، لكنه أيضاً قد يكون بمعنى المفعول . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : قوله : ( أتعبدون ما تنحتون ؟ ) والمراد بقوله : (ما تنحتون ) المنحوت . لا النحت، لأنهم ما عبدوا النحت، وإنما عبدوا الأصنام المنحوتة . الثاني : قوله : ( فإذا هي تلقف ما يأفكـون ) وليس المراد بها أنها
The tenth proof: His statement, the Most High: 'And Allah created you and what you do.' The aspect of inference: that the grammarians said: the word 'ma' with what follows it is in the position of a verbal noun (masdar). So His statement 'wa ma ta'malun' means 'and your action.' And on this interpretation, the meaning of the verse becomes: 'And Allah created you and created your action.' Then it is said: 'This verse is an argument against you (i.e., against your position) in several respects: First: The Most High only mentioned this verse as a rebuke to them for worshipping idols, because He made clear that He is their Creator and the Creator of those idols, and the Creator is the one deserving worship, not the created. So when they abandoned His worship—exalted is He—while He is their Creator, and then worshipped idols, the Most High rebuked them for this great error. He said: 'Do you worship what you carve, while Allah created you and what you do?' And if they were not agents of their actions and bringers-into-existence of them, it would not be permissible to rebuke them for them. We concede: that this verse is not an argument against you. But we do not concede that it is an argument for you. As for your statement: 'the word 'ma' with what follows it is in the position of a verbal noun,' we say: this is denied. Its clarification: that Sibawayh and al-Akhfash disagreed about whether it is permissible to say 'a'jabani ma qumta' meaning 'your standing'? Sibawayh allowed it, al-Akhfash prohibited it, and he claimed that this is only permissible with transitive verbs. And this indicates that 'ma' with what follows it is in the position of the object (maf'ul) according to al-Akhfash. We concede: that it may be in the meaning of the verbal noun, but also it may be in the meaning of the object. And that is indicated by several aspects: First: His statement 'Do you worship what you carve?' and the intended meaning of 'what you carve' is the carved thing (al-manhut), not the carving (al-nahd), because they did not worship the carving; rather, they worshipped the carved idols. Second: His statement 'then it swallows up what they fabricate' and the intended meaning is not that it ...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (al-hujja al-ashira) from the Quranic verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Surah al-Saffat 37:96) and the beginning of an objection. The proof itself is brief: grammarians hold that 'ma' (what) with what follows it can be interpreted as a verbal noun (masdar), so 'ma ta'malun' means 'your action' (amalukum), giving the meaning 'Allah created you and created your action.' This directly supports the position that Allah creates human actions. Then an objection is raised against this proof from several angles. First, the objector argues that the verse is actually a rebuke for idol worship, and if humans were not true agents (fa'ilun) and bringers-into-existence (mujidun) of their actions, rebuke would be meaningless. Second, the objector challenges the grammatical point: the construction 'ma qumta' meaning 'your standing' is disputed between Sibawayh (who allows it) and al-Akhfash (who prohibits it except with transitive verbs). Third, even if 'ma' could be masdariyya, it could also be mawusula (relative pronoun) meaning the object, as in 'what you carve' (the carved idols) rather than the act of carving. This is supported by another verse: 'then it swallows up what they fabricate,' where 'ma yafikun' means the fabricated things, not the act of fabricating. The passage cuts off in the middle of the second evidence. The author's response is not included in this window, leaving the argument incomplete.

المفعول به
سيبويه
الأخفش

النحت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume the grammatical point is settled, but the objection shows that the interpretation of 'ma' is disputed among grammarians, affecting the strength of the proof.
Correction: The proof is not decisive because the meaning of 'ma' in the verse can be understood in different ways, as shown by the grammatical debate.

Checkpoint

A) Whether 'ma' is a relative pronoun or a conditional.
B) Whether 'ma' is a masdariyya (verbal noun) or a mawusula (relative pronoun).
C) Whether 'ma' is an interrogative.
D) Whether 'ma' is a negative particle.

Key Takeaway
The strength of the tenth proof hinges on the grammatical interpretation of 'ma', which is disputed and contextual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this challenge your confidence in your previous understanding of this verse, and what will you do to ensure your interpretations are grounded in careful study?

The Grammatical Argument and the First Objection
Grammatical argument and first objection face

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical inference that the verse implies God creates human actions and the opponent's objection that the verse is a rebuke, which presupposes human agency.

Core Idea
Context of rebuke trumps grammatical form.

This lesson introduces a key theological debate: does the Quranic verse 'Allah created you and what you make' prove that God creates human actions? We first see the grammatical argument that treats 'mā' as a verbal noun, then a powerful objection that the verse is actually a rebuke against idol worshippers, which only makes sense if they are responsible for their actions. This sets the stage for a deeper linguistic analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the grammatical inference
Read the opening lines where the author presents the grammarians' view that 'mā' with its following verb is like a verbal noun, making the verse mean 'God created your deed'.
Note the technical term 'تقدير المصدر'.
2
Understand the objection's first face
The opponent argues that the verse rebukes idol worshippers for abandoning God to worship idols, and rebuke is only valid if they are doers of actions. Consider why this challenges the author's position.
This is a theological point, not just grammatical.
3
See the concession
The opponent concedes the verse is not against them but denies it is for them. Notice the shift in argumentative stance.

Source Text

الاستدلال : أن النحويين قالوا : لفظ ( ما ) مع ما بعده في تقدير المصدر فقوله : ( وما تعملون ( معناه : وعملكم. وعلى هذا التقدير ، صار معنى الآية : والله خلقكم وخلق عملكم • فإن قيل : هذه الآية حجة عليكم من وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى إنما ذكر هذه الآية توبيخاً لهم على عبادة الأصنام ، لأنه تعالى بين أنه خالقهم وخالق لتلك الأصنام، والخالق هو المستحق للعبادة دون المخلوق ، فلما تركوا عبادته - سبحانه - مع أنه خالقهم . ثم عبدوا الأصنام ، لا أنه - سبحانه - وبخهم على هذا الخطأ العظيم . فقال : ( أتعبدون ما تنحتون . والله خلقكم ؟ وما تعملون ؟ ) ولو لم يكونوا فاعلين لأفعالهم وموجدين لها ، لما جاز توبيخهم عليها . جرم لكم . سلمنا : أن هذه الآية ليست حجة عليكم . لكن لا نسلم أنها حجة لكم . أما قوله : « إن كلمة ( ما » مع ما بعده في تقدير المصدر، قلنا : هذا ممنوع. وبيانه : أن ( سيبويه ) و ( الأخفش : اختلفا في أنه هل يجوز أن يقال : أعجبني ما قمت . أي قيامك ؟ فجوزه ( سيبويه : ومنعه ( الأخفش ، وزعم : أن هذا لا يجوز إلا في الفعل المتعدي . وذلك يدل على أن « ما ، مع ما بعده في تقدير المفعول عند « الأخفش : سلمنا : أن ذلك قد يكون بمعنى المصدر ، لكنه أيضاً قد يكون بمعنى المفعول . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : قوله : ( أتعبدون ما تنحتون ؟ ) والمراد بقوله : (ما تنحتون ) المنحوت . لا النحت ، لأنهم ما عبدوا النحت ، وإنما عبدوا الأصنام المنحوتة . الثاني : قوله : ( فإذا هي تلقف ما يأفكـون (۲) ) وليس المراد بها أنها تلقفت نفس الإفك . بل أراد العصى والحبال التي كانت أسباباً لترويج ذلك الإفك . الثالث : إن العرب يسمون محل العمل عملاً . يقال في الثياب والخاتم . هذا عمل فلان . والمراد : محل عمله . فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن ( ما ) مع ما بعده ، كما يجيء بمعنى المصدر فقد يجيء بمعنى المفعول . فلم يكن حمله ههنا على المصدر ، أولى من حمله على المفعول . بل نقول : حمله ههنا على المفعول أولى . وذلك لأن المقصود في هذه الآية تزييف مذهبهم في عبادة الأصنام، لا بيان أنهم لا يوجدون أفعال أنفسهم . لأن الذي جرى ذكره في أول الآية إلى هذا الموضع هو مسألة عبادة الأصنام ، لا مسألة خلق الأعمال والجواب : أما قوله : « إضافة العبادة، والنحت إلى العباد ، يدل على كون العبد محدثاً ، فجوابه : سيجيء عند الجواب عن شبههم العقلية • وقوله : ( لا نسلم أن هذه الكلمة في تقدير المصدر ، قلنا : هذا مذهب سيبويه ) وقوله حجة . وأما الآيات التي أوردوها ، فتحملها على المجاز . وقد بينا في أصول الفقه : أن المجاز خير من الاشتراك ، وقولهم يقتضي كون هذا اللفظ مشتركاً بين المصدر وبين المفعول . والله أعلم
The inference: the grammarians said that the word (mā) with what follows it is in the estimation of a verbal noun. So His saying (wa-mā taʿmalūn) means: and your deed. And on this estimation, the meaning of the verse becomes: And Allah created you and created your deed. Then it is said: This verse is an argument against you from aspects: First: He, exalted, only mentioned this verse as a rebuke to them for worshipping idols, because He, exalted, made clear that He is their Creator and the Creator of those idols, and the Creator is the one deserving worship, not the created. So when they abandoned His worship - glorified be He - despite Him being their Creator, and then worshipped idols, He - glorified be He - rebuked them for this great error. He said: 'Do you worship what you carve? While Allah created you and what you make?' And if they were not doers of their actions and existentiators of them, it would not be permissible to rebuke them for them. ... We conceded: this verse is not an argument against you. But we do not concede that it is an argument for you. As for his saying: 'The word (mā) with what follows it is in the estimation of a verbal noun,' we say: this is prohibited. Its clarification: that Sibawayh and al-Akhfash disagreed as to whether it is permissible to say 'I liked mā qumta' i.e. your standing. Sibawayh permitted it and al-Akhfash forbade it, claiming that this is not permissible except in transitive verbs. That indicates that (mā) with what follows it is in the estimation of an object according to al-Akhfash. We conceded: that may be in the meaning of a verbal noun, but it may also be in the meaning of an object. Evidence for it from aspects: First: His saying: 'Do you worship what you carve?' And what is intended by His saying (mā tanḥitūn) is the carved thing, not the carving, because they did not worship the carving but rather worshipped the carved idols. Second: His saying: 'Then it swallowed what they were faking' and what is intended by it is not that it swallowed the faking itself, but rather the sticks and ropes that were the means of promoting that faking. Third: The Arabs call the place of action an action. It is said of garments and a ring: this is the work of so-and-so. And what is intended is his place of action. So it is established by these three aspects that (mā) with what follows it, just as it comes in the meaning of a verbal noun, may also come in the meaning of an object. So its interpretation here as a verbal noun is not more appropriate than its interpretation as an object. Rather we say: its interpretation here as an object is more appropriate, because the purpose in this verse is to disprove their school in worshipping idols, not to clarify that they do not bring into existence their own actions. Because what has been mentioned from the beginning of the verse to this place is the matter of idol worship, not the matter of the creation of deeds. And the answer: As for his saying: 'The attribution of worship and carving to the servants indicates that the servant is a hādith (originating agent),' its answer: it will come later when answering their rational doubts. And as for his saying: 'We do not concede that this word is in the estimation of a verbal noun,' we say: this is the school of Sibawayh and his saying is authoritative. And as for the verses they cited, we interpret them metaphorically. And we have explained in uṣūl al-fiqh that metaphor is better than ambiguity (ishtirāk), and their statement requires that this word be ambiguous between the verbal noun and the object. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument. It begins with an inference (istidlāl) from the grammarians that the word 'mā' with what follows it functions as a verbal noun (maṣdar), so the verse 'and what you make' means 'and your making', implying God created both humans and their actions. The opponent then raises an objection (fa-in qīla) saying this verse is actually a proof against the author's position, from three aspects: First, the verse rebukes idol worshippers for abandoning God's worship despite God being their Creator, and if they were not the doers of their actions, rebuke would be unjustified. The opponent then concedes (sallamnā) the verse is not an argument against them but denies it is for them. They dispute the grammatical point, citing disagreement between Sibawayh and al-Akhfash on whether 'mā' can indicate a verbal noun or an object. They then present three linguistic evidences that 'mā' can mean the object (mafʿūl): the verse about carved idols, the verse about the staff swallowing what was faked, and the Arab usage of calling the place of action 'action'. They conclude that interpreting 'mā' as an object is more appropriate because the context is idol worship, not the creation of deeds. The author then gives the answer (al-jawāb): the attribution of worship and carving to servants does indicate they are origination agents, but this will be addressed later; as for the grammatical point, Sibawayh's view is authoritative, and the verses cited by the opponent are to be interpreted metaphorically, since metaphor is preferable to lexical ambiguity. The entire exchange centers on the theological debate over whether humans are the creators of their own actions (khalq al-afʿāl).

Key Terms
ما
تقدير المصدر
المصدر
الخالق
العبد
فاعل

Checkpoint
According to the first objection face, why is the rebuke in the verse valid?
A) Because the idol worshippers were created by Allah
B) Because the idol worshippers are doers of their actions
C) Because the idols were created by Allah
D) Because the grammarians said so

Key Takeaway
The verse's primary context is rebuke, which presupposes human agency, challenging a purely grammatical reading.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Allah created you and what you make', do you instinctively lean toward a reading that removes your own responsibility? In what area of your life right now are you using 'Allah created it' to avoid accountability?
?
The objection in this lesson argues that rebuke only makes sense if you are truly the doer of your actions. Is there a sin or failure you have dismissed as 'just my nature' or 'Allah's decree' that you need to own as your action today?
?
The opponent concedes: 'This verse is not an argument against you, but it is also not an argument for you.' Is there a belief you hold that you have never honestly examined because you fear losing its comfort? Will you examine it now, even if it unsettles you?

The Linguistic Dispute and Three Evidences
Linguistic dispute and three evidences response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical dispute over 'mā', the three Quranic and linguistic evidences that it can mean the object, and the author's response that metaphor is preferable to ambiguity.

Core Idea
'Mā' is ambiguous; metaphor beats lexical ambiguity.

The opponent now challenges the grammatical claim head-on, citing disagreement among leading grammarians and providing three concrete linguistic evidences that 'mā' can indicate the object rather than the verbal noun. This leads to a conclusion that interpreting 'mā' as object fits the verse's context of idol worship. The author responds by affirming Sibawayh's authority and invoking the principle that metaphor is preferable to lexical ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Read the dispute between Sibawayh and al-Akhfash
Read the passage where the opponent cites the disagreement on whether 'mā' can be in the estimation of a verbal noun or an object, especially with intransitive verbs.
This shows the linguistic point is not settled.
2
Examine the three linguistic evidences
The opponent presents three proofs from Quran and Arabic usage that 'mā' can mean the object. Read each one carefully and note how they support the opponent's case.
First evidence: verse about carving. Second: verse about staff swallowing. Third: Arab usage of 'work' for the place of work.
3
Understand the opponent's conclusion
The opponent concludes that since 'mā' can mean both, and the context is idol worship, interpreting it as object is more appropriate.
This challenges the author's original inference.
4
Read the author's response
The author responds by asserting Sibawayh's authority, stating that the verses cited are metaphorical, and that metaphor is better than lexical ambiguity.
Note the reference to uṣūl al-fiqh principle.

?

?

The Argument about 'في' and Human Actions as Created
Razi defends that 'خلق لكم ما في الأرض' does not include human actions, and refutes objections about 'في'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and theological arguments surrounding the interpretation of the word 'في' in the Qur'anic verse about creation, and why it does not prove that God creates human actions.

Core Idea
The word 'في' has a broad meaning that includes both physical containment and accidental inherence.

This passage presents a sophisticated debate over the meaning of a single preposition. The author defends the Ash'ari position that the verse 'He created for you all that is in the earth' does not entail that God creates human actions, by carefully analyzing the semantics of 'في' and the context of benefit. You will follow the exchange of objections and responses that illustrate the precision required in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and initial objection
Read the opening lines where the author states the opponent's claim that actions exist in the earth, then the objection about the meaning of 'في'.
Note that the author first agrees that actions exist in the earth.
2
Examine the three proofs for preferring one interpretation
The author presents three reasons why their interpretation (that 'في' does not include actions) is stronger. Read each proof carefully.
These proofs involve the lam of benefit, completion of creation, and universal benefit.
3
Understand the final answer regarding the common meaning of 'في'
The author responds by arguing that 'في' has a common meaning of presence in a container, which includes both physical objects and accidents.
This is based on the principle of avoiding homonymy and using figurative language.
4
Reflect on the counter-objection about the lam of benefit
The author briefly rebuts the objection that the lam indicates benefit by citing the verse about painful punishment.

وأفعال العباد موجودة في ذواتهم ، وذواتهم موجودة في الأرض . والموجود في الموجود في الشيء ، موجود في الشيء . فصح أن أفعال العباد موجودة في الأرض . فقوله تعالى : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً ) لم يدل على كونه تعالى خالقاً لأعمال العباد . فإن قيل : أهل اللغة نصوا على أن كلمة ( في ) للظرفية . وإنما يدخل في هذه الآية : ما يكون حاصلاً في الأرض ، حصول المظروف في الظرف . وهي الأشياء المودعة في الأرض. كالحيوانات والجمادات . أما أفعال العباد ، فهي أعراض ، ولا حصول لها في الأرض، على وفق حصول المظروف في الظرف بل على وفق حصول العرض في المحل . سلمنا : أن كلمة ( في ) تتناول ما يحصل في الشيء ، حصول الدرّة في الحقة . وما يحصل في الشيء حصول العرض في المحل . إلا أن هذين المعنيين لفظاً معنيان مختلفان بالماهية ، فكان لفظ ( في ) بالنسبة إلى هذين المفهومين مشتركاً ، وحمل اللفظ المشترك على كل مفهوميه غير جائز . فلم كان حمله على ما ذكرتم أولى من حمله على ما ذكرنا ؟ وعليكم الترجيح.. بل ما ذكرناه أولى ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن اللام في قوله ( لكم ) لعود المنفعة . وهذا لا يطرد في الكفر والفسوق والعصيان ، لأنها غير نافعة للعباد ، بل ضارة لهم . الثاني : إن قوله : ( خلق لكم ) يقتضي أن تلك الأشياء صارت مخلوقة ، وحصل الفراغ منها ، وأفعال العباد ليست كذلك . الثالث : إن قوله : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض ) يقتضي كون كل واحد من هذه المخلوقات مخلوقاً لكل أحد. وأفعال العباد ليست كذلك . لأنه لا انتفاء بكل واحد منها إلا لفاعله فقط . والله أعلم . والجواب : إن كلمة ( في ) مستعملة في قولنا : الماء في الكوز ، وفي قولنا : العرض في المحل . والأصل عدم الاشتراك . والمجاز يوجب كون هذا اللفظ موضوعاً بإزاء القدر المشترك بينهما . وذلك القدر المشترك هو أن يكون المظروف حاصلاً في داخل الظرف ، سواء كان سارياً فيه ، كما في العرض والمحل ، أو موضوعاً في جوفه ، كما في الماء والكوز . وإذا جعلنا لفظ ( في ) بإزاء هذا المعنى المشترك ، وجب أن يدخل تحته جميع أنواعه ، رعاية لعموم قوله : ( خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً ) . قوله : ( اللام لعود المنفعة ) قلنا : هذا يشكل بقوله تعالى : ( ولهم عذاب أليم ) والله أعلم .
And the actions of servants exist in their selves, and their selves exist in the earth. And that which exists in that which exists in a thing, exists in the thing. So it is correct that the actions of servants exist in the earth. So His statement, exalted is He: (He created for you all that is in the earth) did not indicate that He, exalted is He, is the creator of the actions of servants. For it is said: The linguists have stated that the word (in) is for containerhood. And only what is present in the earth, in the manner of the contained in the container, enters into this verse. And they are the things deposited in the earth, like animals and inanimate objects. As for the actions of servants, they are accidents, and they have no presence in the earth according to the manner of the presence of the contained in the container, but rather according to the manner of the presence of the accident in the substrate. We concede: that the word (in) covers what occurs in a thing, like the occurrence of a pearl in its box, and what occurs in a thing like the occurrence of an accident in a substrate. Except that these two meanings, verbally, are two meanings different in essence, so the word (in) with respect to these two concepts is a homonym, and applying a homonym to both its meanings is not permissible. So why is its application to what you mentioned more appropriate than its application to what we mentioned? And the burden of proof is on you. Rather, what we mentioned is more appropriate, and it is indicated by aspects: The first: Indeed the 'lam' in His saying (for you) is for the return of benefit. And this does not apply consistently to disbelief, immorality, and disobedience, because they are not beneficial to the servants, but rather harmful to them. The second: Indeed His saying (created for you) implies that those things became created, and completion from them occurred, and the actions of servants are not like that. The third: Indeed His saying (He created for you what is in the earth) implies that each one of these created things is created for everyone. And the actions of servants are not like that, because there is no benefit from each one of them except for its doer only. And God knows best. And the answer: Indeed the word (in) is used in our saying: water in the jug, and in our saying: the accident in the substrate. And the principle is that there is no homonymy. And [the principle of] figurative use requires that this word be assigned to the common denominator between them. And that common denominator is that the contained is present in the interior of the container, whether it permeates it, as in the accident and substrate, or is placed in its interior, as in water and the jug. And if we make the word (in) correspond to this common meaning, then it is necessary that all its types enter under it, in observance of the generality of His statement: (He created for you all that is in the earth). His statement: (the 'lam' is for return of benefit) we say: This is problematic due to His statement, exalted is He: (And for them is a painful punishment). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the claim that the Qur'anic verse (خلق لكم ما في الأرض جميعاً) does not indicate that God creates human actions. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, the author establishes that human actions exist in the earth because they exist in their selves, which exist in the earth. Hence, the generality of the verse would include them if taken literally. However, the opponent objects that the word (في) in Arabic indicates containment like a container, and human actions are accidents which are not contained in the earth like physical objects. The author then concedes that (في) has two meanings—containment of a solid object and inherence of an accident in a substrate—but argues that these are two distinct essences, making the word a homonym. Applying a homonym to both meanings is not permissible without a preference. The author then gives three proofs that their own interpretation (that (في) refers to the inherence of accidents) is preferable: 1) the لام (لـ) in (لكم) indicates benefit, which does not apply to sinful acts; 2) (خلق لكم) implies completion, but human actions are not pre-created; 3) (خلق لكم ما في الأرض) implies each thing is created for everyone, but each human action benefits only its doer. The author then provides a final answer: the word (في) has a common meaning of being present inside a container, whether permeating (like accident in substrate) or non-permeating (like water in jug). This common meaning includes both types, so the generality of the verse covers human actions. The objection about the لام for benefit is countered by citing that punishment is also mentioned with لام. Thus, the original claim that the verse does not prove God creates human actions is upheld.

في
الظرفية
العرض
المحل
اللفظ المشترك
المجاز
اللام لعود المنفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'في' exclusively means containment of a solid object, like water in a jug.
Correction: The word 'في' also has a common meaning of presence in a container, which includes the inherence of an accident in a substrate, as in 'the accident in the substrate'.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the common meaning of the word 'في' that allows it to include both physical objects and accidents?
A) Containment like a pearl in a box
B) Inherence like an accident in a substrate
C) Presence in the interior of a container, whether permeating or not
D) The lam of benefit

Key Takeaway
The verse does not prove that God creates human actions because the word 'في' has a broad meaning that includes accidents, and the context of benefit excludes sinful acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—including your sins—is created by God? If so, how does that affect your sense of personal responsibility and repentance? Are you tempted to blame God for your wrongdoing? What does Razi's argument demand of you in terms of owning your actions?
?
When you read the verse 'He created for you all that is in the earth', do you include your own sinful actions in that 'all'? How does your interpretation affect your view of God's justice and mercy? Are you willing to let go of the excuse that 'God made me sin'?
?
The lam of benefit in 'lakum' is used by Razi to argue that sin is not created for your benefit. But what if you have benefited from a sin? How do you reconcile your experience with this theological principle? What does this teach you about trusting God's wisdom in His creation?

The Thirteenth Proof: God as Creator of All Between Heaven and Earth
Razi presents the thirteenth proof and faces the objection that actions are not bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the verse 'وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما باطلا' implies God creates human actions, and the objection that actions are not bodies.

Core Idea
The verse encompasses everything between heaven and earth, including human actions.

Razi continues his series of proofs for divine creation of human actions. Here he invokes a Quranic verse declaring that God did not create the heavens and earth and what is between them in vain. He argues that since human actions exist 'between heaven and earth', they must be created by God. However, an opponent objects that the phrase only applies to physical bodies, not to accidental actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage containing the thirteenth proof. Identify the verse and Razi's logical steps.
2
Understand the implication
Notice that Razi claims the verse requires God to be creator of everything between heaven and earth, on condition that He is creator of it, not in vain.
3
Examine the objection
The opponent argues that 'between heaven and earth' describes bodies, not actions. Consider why this matters for the proof.
This is a crucial distinction between substances and accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر : قوله تعالى : ( وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما باطلا ). هذا النص يقتضي أنه تعالى خالق لكل ما بين السماء والأرض ، بشرط أن يكون خالقاً لها . لا لغرض باطل . وأفعال العباد موجودة بين السماء والأرض ، فوجب كونها مخلوقة الله تعالى . فإن قيل : الشيء لا يوصف بأنه بين السماء والأرض ، إلا إذا كان جسماً . وأفعال العباد لا تدخل تحت هذا النص . وأيضاً : فهذه الآية حجة عليكم من وجوه : الأول : إنها صريحة في أنه تعالى ما خلق باطلاً أصلا . وذلك يقتضي أن خالق الكفر والمعاصي ليس هو الله تعالى . الثاني : إنه تعالي قال بعده : ( ذلك ظن الذين كفروا . فويل للذين كفروا من النار ) أضاف الظن إليهم ، وأضاف الكفر إليهم ، وتوعدهم على ذلك الكفر . وكيف يعقل أن يتوعد الله عباده على ما خلقه فيهم ؟ الثالث : إنه قال بعده : ( أم نجعل الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات
The thirteenth proof: His statement, exalted is He: (And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in vain). This text requires that He, exalted is He, is the creator of everything between the heaven and the earth, with the condition that He is the creator of it, not for a vain purpose. And the actions of servants exist between the heaven and the earth, so it is necessary that they be created by God, exalted is He. For it is said: A thing is not described as being between the heaven and the earth unless it is a body. And the actions of servants do not enter under this text. Also, this verse is an argument against you from aspects: The first: It is explicit that He, exalted is He, did not create any vain thing at all. And that requires that the creator of disbelief and acts of disobedience is not God, exalted is He. The second: He, exalted is He, said after it: (That is the opinion of those who disbelieve, so woe to those who disbelieve from the Fire). He ascribed the opinion to them, and ascribed disbelief to them, and threatened them for that disbelief. And how can it be conceived that God threatens His servants for what He created in them? The third: He, exalted is He, said after it: (Or shall We make those who believe and do righteous deeds)
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the thirteenth) that God is the creator of human actions, based on the verse (وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما باطلا). The author argues that human actions exist between heaven and earth, so they must be created by God. The opponent objects that only bodies are described as 'between heaven and earth', and actions are accidents, not bodies. The opponent then presents three counter-arguments (وجوه) from the same verse: 1) The verse states God did not create anything in vain, so if He created disbelief it would be in vain; therefore, God cannot be the creator of disbelief. 2) The verse ascribes disbelief to the disbelievers and threatens them, so they are responsible, not God. 3) The verse contrasts believers and disbelievers, implying human responsibility (the third objection is incomplete). The author does not provide an answer to these objections in this excerpt, leaving the argument open.

الحجة الثالثة عشر
بين السماء والأرض
باطلاً
جسماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما باطلا' only refers to physical objects, not to abstract things like actions.
Correction: Razi argues that actions are also between heaven and earth, so they are included. The objection that only bodies qualify is disputed.

Checkpoint
What condition does Razi add to the statement that God is creator of everything between heaven and earth?
A) That He creates it for a purpose
B) That He creates it not in vain
C) That He creates it directly
D) That He creates it with angels

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the verse's universality to argue that human actions, being between heaven and earth, are created by God, but the opponent challenges that actions are not bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Razi’s proof is true that every one of your actions is created by God, then where does your personal responsibility lie? How do you hold yourself accountable for sins you know are ultimately brought into existence by Allah? Examine one recent sin you committed: how does this truth change the way you repent and strive to avoid it?
?
The objection raised in this passage sees human actions as not covered by the verse because they are not bodies. Do you ever subconsciously treat your actions as independent of Allah’s creation—as if they are your own in a way that diminishes your reliance on Him? What would it look like to live each moment genuinely believing your next move is created by God?
?
This verse says Allah did not create the heavens, earth, and what is between them in vain. Yet you sometimes feel your own efforts and struggles are meaningless. How does knowing that every action—including your current reading of this lesson—is created with a purpose reshape your motivation and trust in Allah’s wisdom?

Three Counter-Arguments: The Verse as an Argument Against Ash'arism
The opponent presents three counter-arguments from the same verse against divine creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections the opponent raises from the same verse against the idea that God creates human actions.

Core Idea
The verse that seems to support divine creation of all also indicts God if He creates evil.

After objecting that the verse only applies to bodies, the opponent turns the verse against Razi's own position. He extracts three arguments from the verse and its context: (1) if God created everything, He would create evil, which would be vain; (2) the verse ascribes disbelief to people and threatens them; (3) the verse contrasts believers and disbelievers, implying human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the three counter-arguments
Read the Arabic passage containing the three objections. Notice how each uses the same verse or its context.
2
Analyze the first argument
The opponent argues that if God created all, He would create disbelief, which is vain, contradicting the verse. Understand the logic.
3
Analyze the second argument
The verse ascribes disbelief to people and threatens them, which implies they are responsible, not God.
4
Consider the third argument
The third argument contrasts believers and disbelievers, but the passage is cut off. Guess the intended force.
The third argument is incomplete; it likely emphasizes human distinction.

The Linguistic Premise of 'Ja'ala' and the Proof from Creation of Islam
Author states the two meanings of 'ja'ala' and gives the first proof from the Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual meaning of the verb 'ja'ala' in Arabic and how the verse 'Rabbanā wa-j‘alnā muslimayni laka' serves as a proof that Islam is created by Allah.

Core Idea
The verb 'ja'ala' can mean creation or description; the verse proves Islam is created.

This lesson begins with a crucial linguistic foundation: the Arabic verb 'ja'ala' has two distinct grammatical patterns. The author first clarifies these meanings with Quranic examples, then presents the first proof for the createdness of Islam. By understanding this premise, you will see how a single verse can carry theological weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic premise
The passage explains two uses of 'ja'ala': transitive to one object (meaning creation) and transitive to two objects (meaning making something described). Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
Note the examples: 'He made darkness and light' vs. 'He made me a prophet'.
2
Identify the proof
The verse 'Our Lord, and make us Muslims for You' is presented as a proof. The author argues that it shows the servant only becomes Muslim by Allah's act of making.
This proof relies on the first meaning of 'ja'ala' (creation).
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does this mean Islam is created? Because the act of making (ja'ala) in this context is an act of creation, not just naming.

الفصل الثالث في التمسك بالايات المشتملة على لفظ [البصل؟] وما يجي ميره اعلم : أن صيغة جعل ، قد تجيء متعدية إلى مفعول واحد ، ويكون معناها: الإحداث والتكوين. قال تعالى : وجعل الظلمات والنور (۱)) وقد تجيء متعدية إلى مفعولين ويكون معناها : جعل الذات موصوفة بصفة . قال تعالى : وجعلني نبي (٢) ) واجعلنا مسلمين لك (۳) إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة ، فلنذكر الدلائل : الحجة الأولى : قوله تعالى : ( ربنا واجعلنا مسلمين لك (٤) ) وهذا تصريح بأن العبد لا يصير مسلماً ، إلا بأن يجعله الله مسلماً . وذلك يدل على أن الإسلام يحصل بخلق الله . فإن قيل : كما أن الجعل يرد بمعنى التصيير ، فكذلك يرد لوجوه أخرى : أولها : وصف الشيء بالشيء . قال تعالى : ( وجعلوا الملائكة الذين هم عباد الرحمن إناثاً (٥) ) وقال : ( وجعلوا الله شركاء : الجن (1) ) وثانيها : تنزيل الشيء منزلة غيره . قال تعالى : ( أم نجعل الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات ، كالمفسدين في الأرض ، أم نجعل المتقين كالفجار ؟ (۱) ) والتقدير : هل ننزل المصلحين منزلة المفسدين ؟ وكقول القائل إذا نزله منزلة العدو : إنك جعلتني عدوا لنفسك . وثالثها : الإقدار على الشيء ، والتمكين منه . يقال : جعل فلان أميراً وقاضياً . إذا نصب لذلك . قال تعالى : ( إني جاعلك للناس إماماً (٢)) وقال : ( وجعلناهم أئمة (۳)) والمراد منه : النصب لذلك الأمر ، والتعيين له إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من قوله : ربنا واجعلنا مسلمين لك ) : أحد هذه الوجوه ؟ أما الحكم والتسمية فظاهر ولا يقال : هذا باطل من وجهين : الأول : إن كل مسلم فإنه يستحق أن يسمى مسلماً . فأي فائدة في طلب ذلك من الله تعالى ؟ الثاني : إن المسلمين يصفون المسلمين بالإسلام . وعلى هذا التقدير لا يبقى بين الجعل الصادر من الله تعالى ، وبين الجعل الصادر من كل أحد من أحاد المسلمين فرق لأنا نجيب عن الأول : بأن الإسلام أعظم الخصال الحميدة ، فإذا وصف الله عبداً بذلك ، كان ذلك أشرف المناصب وأعلاها . فلا جرم يحن طلبه من الله تعالى. ونظيره : ما حكى الله في قوله : ( واجعل لي لسان صدق في الآخرين (٤) ) وعن الثاني : إن الله تعالى إذا وصف عبداً بخصلة شريفة ، كان ذلك يوجب من التعظيم والتقديم ما لا يوجبه وصف كل الخلق له بتلك الخصلة . ألا ترى أن الملك العظيم إذا وصف إنساناً ببعض صفات المدح
Chapter three: On adhering to verses containing the word [al-basal?] and what follows. Know that the form 'ja'ala' may come transitive to one object, meaning: innovation and creation. He said Almighty: 'And He made darkness and light.' And it may come transitive to two objects, meaning: making the essence described with a quality. He said Almighty: 'And He made me a prophet' and 'And make us Muslims for You.' If you know this premise, then let us mention the proofs: The first proof: His saying Almighty: 'Our Lord, and make us Muslims for You.' And this is a declaration that the servant does not become Muslim except by Allah making him Muslim. And that indicates that Islam occurs by Allah's creation. For it is said: Just as 'ja'ala' is used in the sense of 'making into,' so also it is used in other ways: First: describing something as something. He said Almighty: 'And they made the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females.' And He said: 'And they made for Allah partners: the jinn.' Second: giving something the status of something else. He said Almighty: 'Or shall We make those who believe and do righteous deeds like the corrupters in the earth, or shall We make the righteous like the wicked?' The interpretation: Do we give the reformers the status of the corrupters? And like the saying of one who gives someone the status of an enemy: 'You have made me an enemy to myself.' Third: enabling someone to do something, and empowering them. It is said: So-and-so was made amir and judge, i.e., appointed to that. He said Almighty: 'Indeed, I will make you a leader for the people' and He said: 'And We made them leaders.' The intent is appointment to that matter and designation for it. If this is established, then we say: Why is it not possible that the intent of His saying 'Our Lord, and make us Muslims for You' is one of these aspects? As for judgment and naming, it is apparent. And it is not said: This is invalid in two ways: First: Every Muslim deserves to be called Muslim. So what benefit is there in asking that from Allah Almighty? Second: The Muslims describe the Muslims with Islam. On this estimation, there remains no distinction between the 'ja'ala' coming from Allah Almighty and the 'ja'ala' coming from any of the common Muslims. For we answer the first: that Islam is the greatest of praiseworthy qualities, so if Allah describes a servant with that, it is the most honorable and highest rank. So naturally it is desired from Allah Almighty. And its analogy is what Allah narrated in His saying: 'And grant me a tongue of truth among the later generations.' And as for the second: that if Allah Almighty describes a servant with a noble quality, that entails a degree of exaltation and precedence that the description of all creatures with that quality does not entail. Do you not see that the great king, if he describes a person with some qualities of praise...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Quran for the doctrine that Islam (faith) is created by Allah. The author begins by establishing a linguistic premise about the verb 'ja'ala' (to make), which can be used in two ways: (1) transitive to a single object, meaning to create or bring into existence, and (2) transitive to two objects, meaning to make something described with a certain quality. This premise is supported by Quranic examples. Then the author presents the first proof: the verse 'Our Lord, and make us Muslims for You' (2:128). The argument is that the verb 'ja'ala' here requires Allah to make the believers Muslims, implying that Islam is a direct creation by Allah. An objection is anticipated: the objector points out that 'ja'ala' can have other meanings, such as describing something as something (e.g., calling angels females), giving something the status of something else (e.g., likening believers to the righteous), or enabling/appointing someone to a position (e.g., making someone a leader). The objector asks why the verse cannot be interpreted in one of these ways rather than as creation. The author's initial response is that for the first two meanings (judgment and naming) it is obvious that they do not fit the context. Then a secondary objection is raised: someone might say that this response is invalid for two reasons: (1) every Muslim already deserves to be called Muslim, so there would be no benefit in asking Allah to name them as such; (2) if the meaning were simply naming or describing, then there would be no difference between Allah's 'ja'ala' and any Muslim's description, which is absurd. The author answers the first objection by explaining that being described as Muslim by Allah is the highest honor, akin to asking for a truthful reputation (as in the verse about Abraham). The author answers the second objection by clarifying that Allah's description carries a degree of exaltation that no human description can achieve, analogous to a great king praising someone. The passage thus defends the createdness of Islam through careful linguistic and theological reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the linguistic premise, when 'ja'ala' is transitive to one object, what does it mean?
A) To make something described
B) To create or bring into existence
C) To appoint someone to a position
D) To give something a status

Key Takeaway
The first proof for the createdness of Islam relies on the meaning of 'ja'ala' as creation in the verse 'Rabbanā wa-j‘alnā muslimayni laka'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sincerely pray 'Rabbanā wa-j‘alnā muslimayni laka' in your salah, do you really mean it as a recognition that your very being a Muslim is a direct creation of Allah every moment, or is it just a habitual recitation? How does that change the way you see your own faith today?
?
You often attribute your good deeds and increasing faith to your own efforts. But this verse shows that even the attribute 'Muslim' is made by Allah. What part of your 'Islamic identity' have you been taking credit for that actually belongs to Allah's creation? Will you give it back to Him right now?
?
If Islam is created by Allah, then those who are not Muslims are not simply lacking a choice; they are lacking Allah's creative act in their hearts. Do you feel the weight of that urgency in your duas for them? When you last prayed for someone's guidance, did you really trust that only Allah can create Islam in them—or were you relying on your own arguments?

Divine Decree
intermediate
8 min
Objection: Other Meanings of 'Ja'ala' and the Response
Objector presents three alternative meanings of 'ja'ala' and the author gives a brief initial reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the first proof by citing alternative meanings of 'ja'ala' and the author's initial reply that for the first two meanings (judgment and naming) the interpretation is apparent and not fitting.

Core Idea
The objector lists three other uses of 'ja'ala', but the author shows they don't apply to the verse.

In response to the proof, an objector points out that 'ja'ala' can also mean describing something as something, giving something a certain status, or enabling someone to do something. The author immediately responds that for the first two meanings (judgment and naming) it is obvious they do not fit. This lesson examines the objection and its initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector lists three alternative meanings of 'ja'ala' with Quranic examples. Identify each meaning.
First: describing (like calling angels females). Second: giving status (like likening believers to righteous). Third: enabling/appointing (like making a leader).
2
Understand the author's reply
The author says 'as for judgment and naming, it is apparent' – meaning these two meanings are obviously not intended.
The author does not elaborate fully here; he stops to address a further objection.
3
Consider why judgment/naming doesn't fit
Think: would it make sense for the verse to mean 'call us Muslims' or 'consider us Muslims'? The author implies it wouldn't, but the next lesson will give reasons.

And this is a declaration that the servant does not become Muslim except by Allah making him Muslim. And that indicates that Islam occurs by Allah's creation. For it is said: Just as 'ja'ala' is used in the sense of 'making into,' so also it is used in other ways: First: describing something as something. The interpretation: Do we give the reformers the status of the corrupters? And like the saying of one who gives someone the status of an enemy: 'You have made me an enemy to myself.' He said Almighty: 'Indeed, I will make you a leader for the people' and He said: 'And We made them leaders.' The intent is appointment to that matter and designation for it. If this is established, then we say: Why is it not possible that the intent of His saying 'Our Lord, and make us Muslims for You' is one of these aspects? As for judgment and naming, it is apparent. And it is not said: This is invalid in two ways: First: Every Muslim deserves to be called Muslim. So what benefit is there in asking that from Allah Almighty? Second: The Muslims describe the Muslims with Islam. For we answer the first: that Islam is the greatest of praiseworthy qualities, so if Allah describes a servant with that, it is the most honorable and highest rank. So naturally it is desired from Allah Almighty. And its analogy is what Allah narrated in His saying: 'And grant me a tongue of truth among the later generations.' And as for the second: that if Allah Almighty describes a servant with a noble quality, that entails a degree of exaltation and precedence that the description of all creatures with that quality does not entail.
