The Ninth Proof: God Makes Leaders of Guidance and Misguidance
Proof from Quranic verses with objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the literal meaning of Quranic verses about God making leaders of guidance and misguidance against rational objections.

Core Idea
God's making of leaders is real creation of attributes, not mere naming.

This lesson presents a powerful proof from two Quranic verses that God creates the attributes of guidance and misguidance in people, making them leaders. The author then addresses a strong objection that such making seems futile on the doctrine of jabr, and provides a sophisticated answer based on the interplay of divine power (qudra) and human motive (da'i). Finally, he refutes two alternative interpretations proposed by opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its explanation
Begin by reading the two Quranic verses and the author's explanation of 'making' as procuring an attribute. Notice how he defines the term 'جعل'.
2
Understand the objection and its two alternatives
The opponent argues that if God creates faith and disbelief, then making an imam of guidance is either futile (calling believers) or impossible (calling disbelievers). They propose two ta'wils: judgment/naming or referring to the Hereafter.
Identify the core dilemma: the literal meaning seems pointless.
3
Follow the author's first response
The author invokes the principle that an act results from power (qudra) plus motive (da'i). The imams' call strengthens the motive in the heart, so their role is effective and not futile.
This answer preserves the literal meaning by showing real causal influence.
4
Examine the refutation of the two interpretations
First, 'judgment and naming' is already refuted earlier. Second, for the Hereafter interpretation: (a) the past tense 'made' cannot be future without violating apparent meaning; (b) if it refers to the future, God's news must be true, so they will indeed be leaders—supporting the literal sense.
The refutation uses linguistic and theological consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : قوله تعالى : ( وجعلناهم أئمة يهدون بأمرنا ) وقوله : ( وجعلناهم أئمة يدعون إلى النار ) وجعل الشيء : شيئاً آخر عبارة : عن تحصيل تلك الصفة فيه . يقال : فلان جعل هذا الثوب أسود ، أو أبيض . أي حصل فيه صفة السواد وصفة البياض ، فكذا ههنا . المراد من جعلهم أئمة الهدى، وأئمة النار : خلق صفة الهدى وصفة الضلال فيهم فإن قيل : هذه الآية لا يمكن إجراؤها على ظاهرها ، لأنه لو كان خالق الإيمان والكفر هو الله تعالى ، لم يكن لجعل هذا إماماً في الهدى ، وجعل ذاك إماما في الضلال معنى. لأن إمام الهدى، إن دعى المؤمن إلى الهدى. فهو عبث . لأن الله تعالى ، لما خلق الهدى في المؤمن ، كانت دعوته إلى الهدى عبثاً . وإن دعي الكافر إلى الهدى فهو محال . لأن من خلق الله الكفر فيه ، امتنع صيرورته مؤمناً . وكذا القول في الإمام الداعي إلى النار . فإنه لا يخلو من هذين الوجهين . فثبت : أن على مذهب الجبر لا يبقى في جعل الشخص إماماً في الهدى ، وإماماً في الضلال فائدة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا بد لهذا الجعل من تأويل . وهو أحد أمرين : أحدهما : الحكم والتسمية. والثاني : أن يحمل ذلك على أحوال القيامة . وذلك لأنهم في الآخرة يدعون أصحابهم إلى النار . كما قال في حق فرعون : ( يقدم قومه يوم القيامة . فأوردهم النار ) ومما يقوي ذلك : أن هذه الآية التي تمسكتم بها ، ذكرها الله عقيب خبر آل فرعون . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : إنا إذا قلنا : الفعل معلول مجموع القدرة مع الداعي . فهذا السؤال زائل . وذلك لأن أئمة الضلال إذا دعوا إنسانا إلى الضلال وزينوه في قلبه . فدعوتهم هي التي أثرت في ترسخ تلك الداعية في قلب ذلك الإنسان . فلا جرم جاز وصفه بكونه إماما فيه . وأما تفسير الجعل بالحكم والتسمية . فقد تقدم جوابه . وأما حمله على أحوال القيامة : فالجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : إن قوله : ( وجعلناهم ) لفظ للماضي ، فحمله على المستقبل خلاف الظاهر . والثاني : هب أن الأمر كما قالوه : إلا أنه تعالى لما أخبر عنهم أنهم يكونون كذلك في القيامة ، استحال أن لا يكونوا كذلك . وإلا لزم أن ينقلب خبر الله الذي يجب أن يكون صدقاً : كذباً . وذلك محال . والمفضي إلى المحال محال . وتمام تقريره ، قد تقدم . وبالله التوفيق
The ninth proof: His statement, the Most High: 'And We made them leaders guiding by Our command' and His statement: 'And We made them leaders inviting to the Fire.' And making a thing another thing is an expression for procuring that attribute in it. It is said: So-and-so made this garment black or white, i.e., he brought about in it the attribute of blackness and the attribute of whiteness. So likewise here. The intended meaning of making them leaders of guidance and leaders of the Fire is: creating the attribute of guidance and the attribute of misguidance in them. If it is said: This verse cannot be taken according to its apparent meaning, because if the Creator of faith and disbelief is Allah, the Most High, then making one an imam in guidance and another an imam in misguidance has no meaning. For the imam of guidance: if he calls the believer to guidance, it is futile—since Allah has created guidance in the believer, his call to guidance is futile; and if he calls the disbeliever to guidance, it is impossible—because whoever Allah has created disbelief in him, it is impossible for him to become a believer. Likewise the statement concerning the imam calling to the Fire: it is not free from these two aspects. Thus it is established that on the doctrine of jabr (compulsion) there remains no benefit in making a person an imam in guidance and an imam in misguidance. And when this is established, we say: This making must have a ta'wil (interpretation). It is one of two matters: one of them is judgment and naming; the second is to carry it upon the conditions of the Resurrection, because on the Last Day they call their companions to the Fire, as He said concerning Pharaoh: 'He will go before his people on the Day of Resurrection and lead them into the Fire.' And what strengthens that is that this verse which you have adhered to, Allah mentioned it after the news of the people of Pharaoh. The answer to the first question: If we say that the act is an effect of the combination of power (qudra) together with the motive (da'i), then this question disappears. That is because the imams of misguidance, when they call a person to misguidance and beautify it in his heart, their call is what influences the strengthening of that motive in the heart of that person. Consequently, it is permissible to describe him as being an imam in it. As for the interpretation of 'making' as judgment and naming, its answer has already preceded. As for carrying it upon the conditions of the Resurrection, the answer to it is from two aspects: First: His statement 'And We made them' is a past tense, so carrying it upon the future is contrary to the apparent meaning. Second: Suppose the matter is as they said, but when the Most High has informed about them that they will be like that on the Resurrection, it is impossible that they not be like that; otherwise it would necessitate that the news of Allah—which must be truthful—turn into falsehood, and that is impossible. And that which leads to the impossible is impossible. The full elaboration has already preceded. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof and a detailed objection-and-answer sequence. The proof begins by citing two Quranic verses: 'And We made them leaders guiding by Our command' and 'And We made them leaders inviting to the Fire.' The author explains that 'making' (جعل) in Arabic means procuring an attribute in something, like making a garment black. Thus God creates the attributes of guidance and misguidance in people. The opponent objects that this cannot be literal: if God creates faith and disbelief, then making someone an imam of guidance calling believers is futile (they already have guidance), and calling disbelievers is impossible (they cannot change). So the literal meaning seems pointless, and the opponent proposes two alternative interpretations: (1) 'making' means merely judgment and naming (حكم وتسمية), or (2) it refers to the Hereafter. The author then gives two answers. First, he responds to the initial objection by invoking the principle that the act is caused by the combination of power (قدرة) and motive (داعي). The imams of misguidance strengthen the motive in people's hearts, so they are effective callers—hence they are called imams in misguidance. Second, he refutes the alternative interpretations: the past tense of 'made' cannot be reinterpreted as future except contrary to apparent meaning; and if it refers to the Hereafter, God's future news about them must be truthful—so it is impossible for them not to be like that, maintaining the literal import. Thus the proof stands.

Correction: The call of the leaders influences the motive (da'i) in the heart, strengthening guidance or misguidance, so it is not futile—it has real causal effect.

Checkpoint

D) By reinterpreting 'making' as judging.

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning of God 'making' leaders of guidance and misguidance is defended by understanding that acts are caused by power and motive, and the call of leaders influences that motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does that change the way you view your own role in da‘wah? Will you now speak more boldly, trusting that your words are part of the cause that God uses?
?
If God truly creates both guidance and misguidance in leaders, then the evil of a tyrant is not just his own doing but a divine making. Does that thought trouble you? How will you guard your tongue and actions today, knowing you could be an imam of either path?

Tenth Proof: God Creates Disbelief in Worshippers of Taghut
Author refutes a reinterpretation of 'making' by using linguistic consistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse about apes, swine, and worshippers of Taghut proves that God creates disbelief in people, based on the linguistic consistency of the verb 'made'.

Core Idea
The same verb 'made' for apes and swine must mean transformation for worshippers of Taghut too.

The author presents a linguistic argument based on Quran 5:60. The verse states that God made some people apes, swine, and worshippers of Taghut. The opponent tries to interpret 'made' as mere judgment for the worshippers, but the author shows that since the same verb is used for physical transformation, it must mean actual creation of disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author cites a verse, gives an opponent's claim, then refutes it.
2
Identify the three items
The verse mentions three things God 'made from them': apes, swine, and worshippers of Taghut. See how the author uses this parallelism.
The third item is 'عبدة الطاغوت' — worshippers of the false deity.
3
Understand the opponent's interpretation
Some claim 'making' (جعل) here means only judgment and naming (حكم وتسمية), not actual creation. The author rejects this.
This would make the verse metaphorical for the third item.
4
See the refutation by parallelism
The author argues that since 'جعل' is used identically for apes and swine (where it means physical transformation), it must mean the same for worshippers. Otherwise, the word would be used in both literal and figurative senses in one context, which is impossible.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, God literally created the attribute of worshipping Taghut in them, proving He creates disbelief.
This is a key proof for the Ash'ari position on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله تعالى : قل : هل أنبئكم بشر من ذلك ، مثوبة عند الله من لعنه الله ، وغضب عليه ، وجعل منهم القردة والخنازير ، وعبد الطاغوت ) وهذا تصريح بأنه تعالى جعلهم عبدة الطاغوت . ولا يمكن تفسير هذا الجعل بالحكم والتسمية . لأنه تعالى ذكر صيغة جعل ، ثم أسند هذه الصيغة إلى القردة ، ثم إلى الخنازير، ثم إلى عبدة الطاغوت . ولا شك أن المراد من جعل ، عند إسنادها إلى القردة والخنازير، هي التصبير ، والقلب من صفة إلى صفة . فوجب أن يكون المراد بإسنادها إلى عبدة الطاغوت هذا المعنى أيضاً ، وإلا لزم استعمال اللفظ الواحد دفعة في حقيقته ومجازه معاً . وإن ذلك محال
The tenth proof: His statement, the Most High: 'Say: Shall I inform you of something worse than that as recompense from Allah? [It is that] those whom Allah has cursed and with whom He became angry—He made from them apes and swine and worshippers of Taghut.' And this is an explicit statement that He, the Most High, made them worshippers of Taghut. And it is not possible to interpret this making (ja'l) as judgment and naming (hukm wa tasmiya), because the Most High mentioned the form 'making,' then attributed this form to apes, then to swine, then to worshippers of Taghut. And there is no doubt that the intended meaning of 'making' when attributed to apes and swine is transformation (tasbir) and changing from one attribute to another. Therefore, the intended meaning when attributed to worshippers of Taghut must also be this meaning; otherwise it would necessitate using one word at the same time in its literal and figurative meaning, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof, based on Quran 5:60. The verse states that God made some people apes, swine, and worshippers of Taghut. The author argues that this is an explicit statement that God made them worshippers of Taghut (i.e., created disbelief in them). The opponent might try to interpret 'making' (جعل) here as merely judgment and naming (حكم وتسمية)—that is, God declared them to be worshippers without actually creating the attribute. The author refutes this by pointing out that the same verb 'جعل' is applied to three things: apes, swine, and worshippers of Taghut. In the first two cases, 'making' clearly means physical transformation (تصبير) and changing from one form to another. Since the same word is used in the same context, it must carry the same meaning when applied to worshippers of Taghut—meaning God actually created the attribute of worshipping Taghut in them. If we were to interpret it as judgment for the third but transformation for the first two, we would be using one word in both its literal and figurative senses simultaneously, which is impossible. Therefore, the interpretation of mere judgment is invalid, and the literal meaning—that God creates disbelief—stands.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic consistency of the Quran proves that God creates the actions of His servants, including disbelief.

The Syllogism and the First Objection
A syllogism with premises supported by consensus and Quran, followed by an objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic proof that faith is a blessing from God, supported by consensus and a precise definition of blessing.

Core Idea
Faith is literally a blessing, and every blessing is from God.

The author opens with a clear syllogism: faith is a blessing, every blessing is from God, so faith is from God. He backs each premise with evidence from consensus and scripture. But an objector challenges both the claim that faith is a blessing and the nature of attribution to God. In this lesson, we follow the proof through its initial refutation, including a deeper definition of what a blessing truly is.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism and its support
Start by reading the opening syllogism and the evidence given for each premise. Notice the use of consensus for the first premise and the Qur'anic verse for the second.
The syllogism is the backbone of the proof.
2
Examine the objection
The objector first denies the consensus and then, even if he accepts, argues that attribution to God could be indirect (by command or help), using analogies of a father teaching and Moses attributing a killing to Satan.
3
Study the first part of the answer
The author responds by insisting that denial of the consensus is obstinacy. Then, anticipating persistence, he provides a deeper definition of 'blessing' as anything beneficial free from harm, which applies to faith.
This definition is crucial for the rest of the argument.
4
Reflect on the definition's role
Consider why the author defines 'blessing' in this way. It establishes a literal common ground that prevents metaphorical or ambiguous interpretations.
This method of finding a common denominator is a key logical tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : الإيمان نعمة . وكل نعمة فهي من الله تعالى . ينتج : أن الإيمان من الله تعالى . إنما قلنا : إن الإيمان نعمة . لإطباق الأمة على قولهم : الحمد لله على نعمة الإيمان . وإنما قلنا : إن كل نعمة فهي من الله تعالى . لقوله تعالى : ( وما بكم من نعمة فمن الله ) 

فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن الإيمان نعمة . والإجماع الذي ادعيتم : ممنوع . سلمنا : أنه نعمة ، وأن كل نعمة فمن الله ، حتى ينتج : أن الإيمان من الله . إلا أنا نقول : إن كل شيء كما يضاف إلى فاعله ، لأجل أنه فعله ، فكذلك قد يضاف إلى الأمر به ، والمعين عليه . لأجل أنه أمر به ، وأعان عليه . ألا ترى أن الأب إذا علم ولده الأدب ، وأعانه عليه ، صح أن يقال : هذا العلم إنما حصل له من ذلك الأب ومن وجهته ، وإن كان لا يقال : إنه فعله . ونظيره : قول موسى صلوات الله عليه ، لما قتل القبطي : ( هذا من عمل الشيطان ) وما أراد أن الشيطان قتله ، لكن أراد أنه وسوس إليه ، حتى حدث ما حدث . فكذا ههنا

والجواب : الدليل على أن الإيمان نعمة : الإجماع الذي نقلناه ، وإنكاره مكابرة . فإن أصروا على المنع . قلنا : الدليل على أن الإيمان نعمة : أن لفظ النعمة مستعمل في صور كثيرة . ولا بد من جعلها حقيقة في القدر المشترك بينها ، دفعاً للاشتراك والمجاز . وكونه أمراً منتفعـاً به خالياً عن كل جهات المضرة ، قدر مشترك . فوجب جعل اللفظ حقيقة فيه . ولأنا إذا قلنا : إن الله تعالى أعطى فلاناً نعمة، لم يفهم إلا ما ذكرناه . فثبت : أن لفظ النعمة اسم لكل أمر منتفع به ، خالي عن جميع جهات المضرة . والإيمان كذلك ، فوجب أن يكون نعمة

قوله : «لم لا يجوز أن يكون الإيمان من الله ، بمعنى أنه حصل بـأمـره وبإعانته ، ؟

قلنا : لأن كلمة ( من ( لابتداء الغاية. وإنما يصدق القول بأن هذه النعمة من الله تعالى، إذا كان ابتداء حدوثها من الله . فلو كان إحداثها وإيجادها من العبد ، لم يصدق عليها أنها من الله. وأقصى ما في الباب : أنه قد يطلق هذا اللفظ على سبيل المجاز في بعض المواضع . إلا أن المجاز خلاف الأصل . والله أعلم
The first proof: Faith is a blessing. And every blessing is from God Almighty. Conclusion: that faith is from God Almighty. We only said that faith is a blessing because of the consensus of the community on their saying: Praise be to God for the blessing of faith. And we only said that every blessing is from God Almighty because of His saying: (And whatever blessing you have is from God). 

If it is said: We do not accept that faith is a blessing. And the consensus that you claim is denied. We accept that it is a blessing, and that every blessing is from God, so that it follows that faith is from God. However, we say: Just as everything is attributed to its doer because he did it, so too it may be attributed to the one who commands it and helps with it, because he commanded it and helped with it. Do you not see that if a father teaches his child good manners and helps him with it, it is correct to say: This knowledge came to him from that father and from his direction, even though it is not said that he did it. And an analogy for this is the saying of Moses, peace be upon him, when he killed the Egyptian: (This is from the work of Satan). He did not mean that Satan killed him, but rather that Satan whispered to him until what happened happened. So it is here.

And the answer: The evidence that faith is a blessing is the consensus we transmitted, and denying it is obstinacy. If they insist on denial, we say: The evidence that faith is a blessing is that the word 'blessing' is used in many instances. It must be made a literal (ḥaqīqa) term for the common denominator among them, to avoid homonymy and metaphor. And it being something beneficial, free from all aspects of harm, is the common denominator. So it is necessary to make the term literal for it. And because when we say: God Almighty gave so-and-so a blessing, nothing is understood except what we mentioned. So it is established that the word 'blessing' is a name for every beneficial thing, free from all aspects of harm. And faith is like that, so it must be a blessing.

His saying: 'Why is it not permissible that faith is from God in the sense that it came about by His command and His help?'

We say: Because the word 'min' (from) indicates the beginning of the point of origin (ibtidā' al-ghāya). And the statement that this blessing is from God Almighty is only true if its origin of occurrence is from God. If its bringing into existence and creation were from the servant, it would not be correctly described as being from God. The most that can be said is that this word may be used metaphorically in some places. However, metaphor is contrary to the literal principle. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a complete argument in kalām style: a proof, an objection, and a detailed refutation. The author (presumably Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī from the title al-Maṭālib al-‘Āliya) begins with a syllogism: Faith is a blessing; every blessing is from God; therefore faith is from God. The first premise is supported by the consensus (ijmā‘) of the Muslim community, who thank God for the blessing of faith. The second premise is supported by Qur’ān 16:53.

The opponent objects on two levels. First, they deny that faith is a blessing, rejecting the claimed consensus. But even if they concede that faith is a blessing and that every blessing is from God, they argue that attribution of something to God can also be metaphorical—meaning God commanded or helped, not directly created. They give the analogy of a father teaching his son: the knowledge is attributed to the father even though he did not directly produce it, and of Moses attributing the killing to Satan, though Satan only whispered.

The author (the voice of the answer) responds with a more rigorous definition of ‘blessing’. They argue that the word ‘blessing’ is used for many things, so to avoid equivocation and metaphor it must be taken literally as the common denominator: anything beneficial free from harm. Faith fits this definition, so it is literally a blessing. The opponent’s analogy fails because the word ‘min’ in ‘from God’ indicates the beginning of existence (ibtidā’ al-ghāya). For something to be truly said to be from God, its very origination must be from Him, not merely command or help. Metaphorical usage is possible but is not the default (al-aṣl). Thus the original proof stands: faith is directly from God.

Key Terms
إيمان
نعمة
إجماع
حقيقة
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the consensus is not sufficient evidence or that 'blessing' is too broad a term to apply to faith.
Correction: The author shows that denying the consensus is unreasonable (مكابرة), and that 'blessing' has a precise literal meaning (beneficial free from harm) that faith fits.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence used by the author to support the premise that every blessing is from God?
A) Consensus of the community
B) The verse 'And whatever blessing you have is from God'
C) The definition of blessing as beneficial free from harm
D) The analogy of the father teaching his child

Key Takeaway
Faith is a blessing from God, as proven by consensus and the literal definition of blessing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your faith is a direct gift from God, or do you sometimes feel you earned it? How would your humility change if you internalized that every bit of your belief is a blessing from Him?
?
When you thank God for blessings, do you include your faith? If not, why? What would it mean for your gratitude if you realized that your ability to even say 'alhamdulillah' is itself a blessing from Him?
?
The opponent in this passage attributed goodness to God only indirectly. Is there any area of your life where you subtly think 'I did this' without recognizing God's direct creation? How would your reliance on God shift if you truly believed that every good thing—including your own actions—is directly from Him?

The Meaning of 'From God' in the Proof
An objection about metaphorical attribution and its refutation based on the meaning of 'min'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the preposition 'min' in 'from God' must indicate direct origination, not merely command or help.

Core Idea
The word 'min' requires direct creation, not indirect causation.

After establishing that faith is a blessing, the author faces a refined objection: could not faith be from God only in the sense of His command and assistance? The opponent provides analogies where indirect attribution is common. In this lesson, we see how the author defends the literal meaning of the preposition 'min' to preserve the directness of God's creative act.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
The objector asks why faith cannot be 'from God' in the sense of being brought about by His command and help, not by direct creation.
This is a subtle objection that challenges the literal interpretation.
2
Study the author's answer
The author explains that the word 'min' (from) indicates the beginning of origin (ibtidā' al-ghāya). Something is truly 'from God' only if its first point of existence comes from Him.
This is a linguistic-argumentative point.
3
Consider the principle of metaphor
The author concedes that metaphorical usage exists but reminds that the default (al-aṣl) is literal meaning. So without evidence, the literal sense prevails.
This principle is common in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.
4
Reflect on the analogies
The opponent's analogies (father teaching, Moses and Satan) involve indirect causation. The author shows they do not apply here because the preposition 'min' in the Quranic verse demands direct origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن الإيمان نعمة . والإجماع الذي ادعيتم : ممنوع . سلمنا : أنه نعمة ، وأن كل نعمة فمن الله ، حتى ينتج : أن الإيمان من الله . إلا أنا نقول : إن كل شيء كما يضاف إلى فاعله ، لأجل أنه فعله ، فكذلك قد يضاف إلى الأمر به ، والمعين عليه . لأجل أنه أمر به ، وأعان عليه . ألا ترى أن الأب إذا علم ولده الأدب ، وأعانه عليه ، صح أن يقال : هذا العلم إنما حصل له من ذلك الأب ومن وجهته ، وإن كان لا يقال : إنه فعله . ونظيره : قول موسى صلوات الله عليه ، لما قتل القبطي : ( هذا من عمل الشيطان ) وما أراد أن الشيطان قتله ، لكن أراد أنه وسوس إليه ، حتى حدث ما حدث . فكذا ههنا

?

?

Does this challenge your assumption that you have independent capacity to do good? Will you now examine your actions and acknowledge that even their existence is entirely from Allah?
?

The Dependency Chain: Act Depends on Will, Will Depends on Allah
Sanusi states the chain and proves the first link.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Sanusi's second argument: that human acts depend on human will, and human will depends on Allah's will, leading to the conclusion that all human acts ultimately depend on Allah's will.

Core Idea
Human will is the middle link connecting acts to Allah's will.

Sanusi begins his second proof by stating the overall chain of dependency. He will then prove each link using both Quranic evidence and rational arguments. This lesson introduces the structure and the first link: the dependence of the servant's act on his own will.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the Arabic passage that states the chain: act depends on will, will depends on Allah's will. Notice the logical structure.
The argument is presented as a syllogism: act depends on will, will depends on Allah, therefore act depends on Allah.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises: (1) The act of the servant depends on his will. (2) The will of the servant depends on Allah's will.
3
Understand the first proof
Sanusi now proves the first premise using Quran and reason. Read his Quranic evidence from the verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe...' and the rational argument about the capable person needing a preponderant.
The verse shows that willing faith leads to faith, so the act follows the will. Reason shows that a capable person cannot choose between opposites without a preponderant (will).
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite objection
Note that the Mu'tazilites consider this verse as evidence for free will. Sanusi turns it against them by showing it only proves the act-will link, not independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قوله تعالى : ( فمن شاء فليؤمن ، ومن شاء فليكفر ) وهذه الآية هي التي يظن المعتزلة أنها في غاية القوة من جانبهم . وتقريره : أن تقول : فعل العبد موقوف على مشيئة العبد ، ومشيئة العبد موقوفة على مشيئة الله تعالى . ينتج : أن فعل العبد موقوف على مشيئة الله تعالى . وإنما قلنا : إن فعل العبد موقوف على مشيئته ، للقرآن والعقل . أما القرآن . فقوله تعالى : فمن شاء فليؤمن ، ومن شاء فليكفر ) دلت هذه الآية : على أن مشيئة الإيمان مستعقبة للإيمان ، ومشيئة الكفر مستعقبة للكفر . وظاهر أيضاً : أن عند هذه المشيئة لا يصدر الفعل عن القادر . وأما العقل . فهو أن القادر ، قادر على الضدين. أعني : الكفر والإيمان والمعصية والطاعة . فيمتنع أن يترجح أحدهما على الآخر ، إلا بواسطة القصد والمشيئة

وإنما قلنا : إن مشيئة العبد موقوفة على مشيئة الله تعالى للقرآن والعقل أما القرآن . فقوله : ( وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله ) ومفعول هذه المشيئة : محذوف . فإما أن يكون التقدير : وما تشاءون إلا إن شاء الله ذلك الشيء ، أو يكون التقدير : وما تشاءون شيئاً ، إلا إن شاء الله مشيتكم لذلك الشيء

والأول محال . لأن زيداً ، إذا شاء حركة جسم، وشاء (عمرو) سكونه . فيلزم أن يقال : إن الله شاء حركته وسكونه معاً . وذلك محال . ولما بطل هذا تعين حمل الآية على الوجه الثاني - فيكون التقدير : وما تشاءون شيئاً إلا وشاء الله مشيتكم لذلك الشيء . وأيضاً : فالمشيئة مذكورة صريحاً ، ومفعول المشيئة غير مذكور صريحاً. فإذا حملنا هذه الآية على أن المراد : وما تشاءون شيئاً إلا ويشاء الله مشيئتكم لذلك الشيء . كنا قد أضمرنا ما لم يسبق ذكره . ولا شك وأن الأول : أولى . فثبت : أن تقدير الآية : وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله مشيئتكم لذلك الشيء . وهذا يدل على أن حصول المشيئة لنا موقوف على مشيئة الله تعالى .

وأما المعقول : فهو أنه يمكن أن يحصل للعبد مشيئة الإيمان ، وأن يحصل له مشيئة الكفر بدلاً عن مشيئة الإيمان . فحصول إحدى المشيئين بدلاً عن الأخرى ، لا بد وأن يكون لترجيح مرجح . فإن كان ذلك للعبد ، عاد الطلب فيه . ويلزم التسلسل . وهو محال . وإن كان هو الله فهو المطلوب

ثبت بالقرآن والبرهان : أن صدور الفعل عن العبد ، موقوف على مشيئة لذلك الفعل، وثبت أيضاً بالقرآن والبرهان أن حدوث تلك المشيئة في قلب العبد متوقف على إن شاء الله إحداث تلك المشيئة في قلبه ، وثبت في بدائه العقول : أن الموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء ، موقوف على الشيء . فيلزم القطع بأن صدور الفعل عن العبد ، موقوف على مشيئة الله . وذلك هو
The second proof: His statement the Exalted: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' This is the verse that the Mu'tazilites think is extremely strong on their side. Its exposition: That you say: The act of the servant depends on the will of the servant, and the will of the servant depends on the will of Allah the Exalted. It results: That the act of the servant depends on the will of Allah the Exalted. And we only said that the act of the servant depends on his will, by the Qur'an and reason. As for the Qur'an: His statement the Exalted: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' This verse indicates that the will of faith is followed by faith, and the will of disbelief is followed by disbelief. It is also apparent that with this will, the act does not issue from the capable one. As for reason: The capable one is capable of opposites, I mean, disbelief and faith, disobedience and obedience. It is impossible for one of them to be preponderant over the other except by means of intention and will.

And we only said that the will of the servant depends on the will of Allah the Exalted, by the Qur'an and reason. As for the Qur'an: His statement: 'And you do not will except that Allah wills.' The object of this willing is omitted. So either the estimation is: 'And you do not will except if Allah wills that thing,' or the estimation is: 'And you do not will anything, except if Allah wills your willing of that thing.'

The first is impossible. Because Zayd, if he wills the movement of a body, and 'Amr wills its stillness, it would necessitate that it be said: Allah willed its movement and its stillness together. That is impossible. Since this is invalid, the second interpretation is determined—so the estimation is: 'And you do not will anything except that Allah wills your willing of that thing.' Also: The willing is mentioned explicitly, while the object of willing is not mentioned explicitly. So if we interpret this verse to mean: 'And you do not will anything except that Allah wills your willing of that thing,' we would have implied what was not previously mentioned. And there is no doubt that the former is more appropriate. So it is established that the estimation of the verse is: 'And you do not will except that Allah wills your willing of that thing.' This indicates that the occurrence of willing for us depends on the will of Allah the Exalted.

As for the rational proof: It is possible that the servant has a will for faith, and that he has a will for disbelief instead of the will for faith. So the occurrence of one of the two wills instead of the other must be due to a preponderant. If that is from the servant, then the demand returns, and infinite regress follows—which is impossible. And if it is from Allah, then it is what is sought.

It is established by the Qur'an and proof: that the issuance of the act from the servant depends on a will for that act, and it is also established by the Qur'an and proof that the occurrence of that will in the heart of the servant depends on Allah's willing the bringing about of that will in his heart. And it is established in the primaries of intellects: that that which depends on that which depends on something, depends on that something. So it necessitates the certainty that the issuance of the act from the servant depends on the will of Allah. And that is.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-ḥujjah al-thāniyah) regarding divine will and human action. The author notes that the Mu'tazilites consider the verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe...' as strong evidence for free will, but he turns it against them. The argument proceeds in three steps: First, the act of the servant depends on his own will—proved by the verse (since willing faith leads to faith) and by reason (a capable person needs a preponderant). Second, the servant's will itself depends on Allah's will—proved by the verse 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' and by reason (otherwise infinite regress). For the verse, the author analyzes two possible interpretations and rejects the first (that Allah wills the very thing) because it would imply contradictory wills. The correct interpretation is that Allah wills the servant's willing. The rational proof shows that if the servant were the preponderant, it would lead to infinite regress, so the preponderant must be Allah. The conclusion: since the act depends on the will, and the will depends on Allah's will, the act ultimately depends on Allah's will.

Key Terms
المشيئة
الفعل
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'whoever wills' proves that humans have independent free will.
Correction: The verse only proves that the act follows the will, not that the will is independent of Allah. It is the first link in the chain of dependency.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of Sanusi's argument?
A) The servant's will depends on Allah's will
B) The servant's act depends on his own will
C) The servant's act depends directly on Allah's will
D) The servant's will is independent

Key Takeaway
The first link: the servant's act depends on his will, not directly on Allah's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your every desire to do good or evil is dependent on Allah's will? Or do you live as if your choices are autonomous, forgetting that even your inclination towards obedience is a gift from Him?
?
When you find yourself eager to pray or reluctant to sin, do you pause to recognize that your own will was moved by Allah? How would your gratitude deepen if you saw each righteous intention as a direct bestowal from Him?
?
The Mu'tazilites used the same verse to claim freedom from divine decree. Are there areas in your life where you subtly assume you are the ultimate decider, failing to submit to the reality that your will only functions within the bounds of Allah's will? What would change if you truly lived by this dependency?

Divine Decree
intermediate
6 min
Proof that Human Will Depends on Allah's Will
Sanusi proves the second premise using Quranic interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves the second premise of his argument: that human will itself depends on Allah's will, using both Quranic interpretation and rational reasoning.

Core Idea
Human will is created by Allah's will, not independent.

Having established that the act depends on the will, Sanusi now proves that the will itself depends on Allah's will. He uses the verse 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' and analyzes two possible interpretations, showing why only one is correct. This lesson focuses on that Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proof
Read the passage containing the verse 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' and the two interpretations.
The verse has an omitted object. Two interpretations are possible.
2
Analyze the first interpretation
Understand why the first interpretation (Allah wills the very thing you will) leads to contradiction, as shown in the example of Zayd and Amr.
3
Understand the second interpretation
See how the second interpretation (Allah wills your willing of the thing) avoids the contradiction and is linguistically more appropriate.
The second interpretation is the correct one and proves the dependency.
4
Reflect on the linguistic argument
Notice the author's point about the explicit mention of 'willing' and the implicit object, favoring the second interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قوله تعالى : ( فمن شاء فليؤمن ، ومن شاء فليكفر ) وهذه الآية هي التي يظن المعتزلة أنها في غاية القوة من جانبهم . وتقريره : أن تقول : فعل العبد موقوف على مشيئة العبد ، ومشيئة العبد موقوفة على مشيئة الله تعالى . ينتج : أن فعل العبد موقوف على مشيئة الله تعالى . وإنما قلنا : إن فعل العبد موقوف على مشيئته ، للقرآن والعقل . أما القرآن . فقوله تعالى : فمن شاء فليؤمن ، ومن شاء فليكفر ) دلت هذه الآية : على أن مشيئة الإيمان مستعقبة للإيمان ، ومشيئة الكفر مستعقبة للكفر . وظاهر أيضاً : أن عند هذه المشيئة لا يصدر الفعل عن القادر . وأما العقل . فهو أن القادر ، قادر على الضدين. أعني : الكفر والإيمان والمعصية والطاعة . فيمتنع أن يترجح أحدهما على الآخر ، إلا بواسطة القصد والمشيئة

وإنما قلنا : إن مشيئة العبد موقوفة على مشيئة الله تعالى للقرآن والعقل أما القرآن . فقوله : ( وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله ) ومفعول هذه المشيئة : محذوف . فإما أن يكون التقدير : وما تشاءون إلا إن شاء الله ذلك الشيء ، أو يكون التقدير : وما تشاءون شيئاً ، إلا إن شاء الله مشيتكم لذلك الشيء

والأول محال . لأن زيداً ، إذا شاء حركة جسم، وشاء (عمرو) سكونه . فيلزم أن يقال : إن الله شاء حركته وسكونه معاً . وذلك محال . ولما بطل هذا تعين حمل الآية على الوجه الثاني - فيكون التقدير : وما تشاءون شيئاً إلا وشاء الله مشيتكم لذلك الشيء . وأيضاً : فالمشيئة مذكورة صريحاً ، ومفعول المشيئة غير مذكور صريحاً. فإذا حملنا هذه الآية على أن المراد : وما تشاءون شيئاً إلا ويشاء الله مشيئتكم لذلك الشيء . كنا قد أضمرنا ما لم يسبق ذكره . ولا شك وأن الأول : أولى . فثبت : أن تقدير الآية : وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله مشيئتكم لذلك الشيء . وهذا يدل على أن حصول المشيئة لنا موقوف على مشيئة الله تعالى .

وأما المعقول : فهو أنه يمكن أن يحصل للعبد مشيئة الإيمان ، وأن يحصل له مشيئة الكفر بدلاً عن مشيئة الإيمان . فحصول إحدى المشيئين بدلاً عن الأخرى ، لا بد وأن يكون لترجيح مرجح . فإن كان ذلك للعبد ، عاد الطلب فيه . ويلزم التسلسل . وهو محال . وإن كان هو الله فهو المطلوب

ثبت بالقرآن والبرهان : أن صدور الفعل عن العبد ، موقوف على مشيئة لذلك الفعل، وثبت أيضاً بالقرآن والبرهان أن حدوث تلك المشيئة في قلب العبد متوقف على إن شاء الله إحداث تلك المشيئة في قلبه ، وثبت في بدائه العقول : أن الموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء ، موقوف على الشيء . فيلزم القطع بأن صدور الفعل عن العبد ، موقوف على مشيئة الله . وذلك هو
The second proof: His statement the Exalted: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' This is the verse that the Mu'tazilites think is extremely strong on their side. Its exposition: That you say: The act of the servant depends on the will of the servant, and the will of the servant depends on the will of Allah the Exalted. It results: That the act of the servant depends on the will of Allah the Exalted. And we only said that the act of the servant depends on his will, by the Qur'an and reason. As for the Qur'an: His statement the Exalted: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' This verse indicates that the will of faith is followed by faith, and the will of disbelief is followed by disbelief. It is also apparent that with this will, the act does not issue from the capable one. As for reason: The capable one is capable of opposites, I mean, disbelief and faith, disobedience and obedience. It is impossible for one of them to be preponderant over the other except by means of intention and will.

And we only said that the will of the servant depends on the will of Allah the Exalted, by the Qur'an and reason. As for the Qur'an: His statement: 'And you do not will except that Allah wills.' The object of this willing is omitted. So either the estimation is: 'And you do not will except if Allah wills that thing,' or the estimation is: 'And you do not will anything, except if Allah wills your willing of that thing.'

The first is impossible. Because Zayd, if he wills the movement of a body, and 'Amr wills its stillness, it would necessitate that it be said: Allah willed its movement and its stillness together. That is impossible. Since this is invalid, the second interpretation is determined—so the estimation is: 'And you do not will anything except that Allah wills your willing of that thing.' Also: The willing is mentioned explicitly, while the object of willing is not mentioned explicitly. So if we interpret this verse to mean: 'And you do not will anything except that Allah wills your willing of that thing,' we would have implied what was not previously mentioned. And there is no doubt that the former is more appropriate. So it is established that the estimation of the verse is: 'And you do not will except that Allah wills your willing of that thing.' This indicates that the occurrence of willing for us depends on the will of Allah the Exalted.

As for the rational proof: It is possible that the servant has a will for faith, and that he has a will for disbelief instead of the will for faith. So the occurrence of one of the two wills instead of the other must be due to a preponderant. If that is from the servant, then the demand returns, and infinite regress follows—which is impossible. And if it is from Allah, then it is what is sought.

It is established by the Qur'an and proof: that the issuance of the act from the servant depends on a will for that act, and it is also established by the Qur'an and proof that the occurrence of that will in the heart of the servant depends on Allah's willing the bringing about of that will in his heart. And it is established in the primaries of intellects: that that which depends on that which depends on something, depends on that something. So it necessitates the certainty that the issuance of the act from the servant depends on the will of Allah. And that is.

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-ḥujjah al-thāniyah) regarding divine will and human action. The author notes that the Mu'tazilites consider the verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe...' as strong evidence for free will, but he turns it against them. The argument proceeds in three steps: First, the act of the servant depends on his own will—proved by the verse (since willing faith leads to faith) and by reason (a capable person needs a preponderant). Second, the servant's will itself depends on Allah's will—proved by the verse 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' and by reason (otherwise infinite regress). For the verse, the author analyzes two possible interpretations and rejects the first (that Allah wills the very thing) because it would imply contradictory wills. The correct interpretation is that Allah wills the servant's willing. The rational proof shows that if the servant were the preponderant, it would lead to infinite regress, so the preponderant must be Allah. The conclusion: since the act depends on the will, and the will depends on Allah's will, the act ultimately depends on Allah's will.

الإرادة
التقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' means Allah wills the very things you will.
Correction: This interpretation is impossible because it would require Allah to will contradictions. The correct meaning is that Allah wills your act of willing.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the first interpretation of the verse?
A) It contradicts the Quran
B) It would require Allah to will opposites simultaneously
C) It is linguistically incorrect
D) It implies human will is independent

Key Takeaway
The correct interpretation of the Quran proves that human will depends on Allah's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision today, do you truly feel that your very desire to choose is a gift from Allah? Reflect on a time you felt proud of your own willpower. How does this lesson challenge that pride? Before you act tomorrow, pause and silently acknowledge that your will depends on Allah's will.
?
The verse says you do not will except that Allah wills. Does this truth frighten you or comfort you? Examine your heart: do you resent the idea that even your repentance is only possible by Allah's will? This week, when you struggle to do good, say: 'I cannot will this except if Allah wills.'
?
Sanusi proves that the first interpretation leads to contradiction. Have you ever accused Allah of injustice by thinking your free will is independent? Repent from that thought now and affirm: 'My will is in Allah's hand.'

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Rational Proof Against Infinite Regress
Sanusi provides rational proof and concludes the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that if human will were not dependent on Allah, it would lead to an infinite regress, and how Sanusi uses this to prove that Allah is the ultimate source of all volition.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in volition proves dependence on Allah.

After the Quranic proof, Sanusi provides a rational argument. He shows that if the servant's will were independent (i.e., if the servant himself were the preponderant that chooses between two opposing wills), it would lead to an infinite regress. Therefore, the preponderant must be Allah. This lesson covers that rational proof and the final conclusion of the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof
Read the passage that begins with 'As for the rational proof...' and follows the argument about the two possible wills.
The servant can potentially will faith or will disbelief. One will replaces the other.
2
Understand the need for a preponderant
Why must there be a preponderant (something that tips the balance) for one will to occur instead of the other? Because both are possible.
3
Consider the two possibilities for the preponderant
If the preponderant is the servant himself, then to will a specific will, the servant would need another will to choose that will, leading to infinite regress.
This infinite regress is impossible (must be avoided).
4
Conclusion: Allah is the preponderant
Since the preponderant cannot be the servant, it must be Allah. This establishes that the will of the servant depends on Allah's will.
5
Read the final conclusion
The final paragraph brings the two premises together and concludes that the act of the servant depends on Allah's will.
It also invokes the principle: 'that which depends on that which depends on something, depends on that something'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to Sanusi's rational argument, what would happen if the servant were the preponderant for his own will?

How the Quran Shows Human Will Depends on God's Will
The author analyzes the Quranic verse to show human will depends on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic argument that human will is dependent on God's will, and why the correct interpretation of the verse avoids contradiction.

Core Idea
The verse shows God wills our willing, not the thing itself.

In this lesson, we examine a key Quranic verse used in kalam to establish the relationship between human will and divine will. The author presents two possible interpretations of the omitted object in the verse, then uses rational argument to choose the one that avoids contradictions. This passage demonstrates how careful tafsir and logical reasoning work together in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the two interpretations being compared.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author presents two possible ways to understand the omitted object in the verse. Find them both and notice how they differ.
The first interpretation makes God's will directly about the same thing the servant wills; the second makes it about the servant's willing.
3
Understand why the first is impossible
Follow the author's example of Zayd and Amr willing opposite actions. See why that would force a contradiction in God's will.
This is a rational refutation based on the impossibility of simultaneous opposites in God's will.
4
See how the second interpretation resolves the issue
The second interpretation avoids the contradiction by making God's will pertain to the servant's act of willing, not to the conflicting outcomes.
The author also notes that this interpretation is linguistically more coherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إن مشيئة العبد موقوفة على مشيئة الله تعالى للقرآن والعقل أما القرآن . فقوله : ( وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله ) ومفعول هذه المشيئة : محذوف . فإما أن يكون التقدير : وما تشاءون إلا إن شاء الله ذلك الشيء ، أو يكون التقدير : وما تشاءون شيئاً ، إلا إن شاء الله مشيتكم لذلك الشيء والأول محال . لأن زيداً ، إذا شاء حركة جسم، وشاء عمرو سكونه . فيلزم أن يقال : إن الله شاء حركته وسكونه معاً . وذلك محال . ولما بطل هذا تعين حمل الآية على الوجه الثاني - فيكون التقدير : وما تشاءون شيئاً إلا وشاء الله مشيتكم لذلك الشيء . وأيضاً : فالمشيئة مذكورة صريحاً ، ومفعول المشيئة غير مذكور صريحاً. فإذا حملنا هذه الآية على أن المراد : وما تشاءون شيئاً إلا ويشاء الله مشيئتكم لذلك الشيء . كنا قد أضمرنا ما لم يسبق ذكره . ولا شك وأن الأول : أولى . فثبت : أن تقدير الآية : وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله مشيئتكم لذلك الشيء . وهذا يدل على أن حصول المشيئة لنا موقوف على مشيئة الله تعالى .
And we only say: The will of the servant is dependent on the will of God Almighty, by the Quran and reason. As for the Quran: His statement: (And you do not will except that God wills [the thing]). The object of this 'will' is omitted. So either the estimation is: And you do not will except if God wills that thing, or the estimation is: And you do not will a thing except if God wills your willing of that thing. And the first is impossible, because if Zayd wills the movement of a body, and Amr wills its rest, it would necessitate that it be said: God willed its movement and its rest together. And that is impossible. When this was invalidated, the carrying of the verse upon the second aspect became determined — so the estimation is: And you do not will a thing except and God wills your willing of that thing. Also: the 'will' is mentioned explicitly, and the object of the will is not mentioned explicitly. So if we carry this verse upon the meaning: And you do not will a thing except and God wills your willing of that thing, we would have concealed what was not previously mentioned. And there is no doubt that the first [interpretation with explicit object] is more appropriate. So it is established: that the estimation of the verse is: And you do not will except that God wills your willing of that thing. And this indicates that the occurrence of will for us is dependent on the will of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a Quranic argument proving that the human will is dependent on God's will. The author first states the principle, then examines the verse 'And you do not will except that God wills' (Qur'an 76:30). He notes that the object of 'will' in the verse is omitted, leading to two possible interpretations: (1) that God wills the same thing the servant wills, or (2) that God wills the servant's willing of that thing. The first is impossible because it would imply that God wills contradictory things when two servants will opposite actions. The second is therefore correct. Moreover, since the word 'will' is explicit but its object is not, the second interpretation (which supplies 'your willing' as the object) is more coherent because it does not introduce an unstated object that is not implied by the context. Thus the verse shows that our very act of willing is brought about by God's will.

Key Terms
مشيئة
تقدير
محذوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse means God directly wills the same action for both believers and disbelievers, leading to contradiction.
Correction: The correct interpretation is that God wills the servant's act of willing, not the action itself, thereby avoiding contradiction.

Checkpoint
Why is the first interpretation of the verse ('that God wills that thing itself') considered impossible?
A) It contradicts the literal wording of the verse.
B) It would imply that God wills contradictory things when two people will opposite actions.
C) It denies that humans have free will.
D) It makes the object of the will explicit.

Key Takeaway
The correct interpretation of the verse shows that God wills our willing, not the action itself, thereby preserving both divine sovereignty and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you truly feel that your very ability to choose depends on God willing that choice? How does this affect the way you approach your daily decisions?
?
Consider the times you have felt proud of your own choices. Does this lesson change how you view the source of your will? What should you say to yourself when you feel self-sufficient?
?
The verse shows that even your willing is from God. How does this impact your sense of accountability? Does it make you more or less responsible for your actions? Why?

Third Proof: God Attributes Actions to Himself and Humans
The author argues from verses where God attributes the same actions to both Himself and humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Quranic verses where God attributes the same actions to both humans and Himself to argue that human actions are also God's creation.

Core Idea
Actions are simultaneously attributed to humans and to God.

This lesson presents the third proof from the author, which draws on verses where God attributes the same actions to humans and then to Himself. This parallel attribution suggests that the action has both a human and a divine aspect. We'll examine five sets of verses and then look at an objection that begins to question the interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first states the premise, then lists five verses as evidence, then starts an objection.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so we only have the beginning of the objection.
2
Identify the five verses
Find each of the five verse pairs in the passage. Notice that in each pair, God first commands or describes an action by humans, then attributes the same action to Himself.
The verses cover faith, laughing/weeping, traveling, killing/throwing, and expulsion.
3
Understand the objection's first part
The objection addresses two specific verses. Read the objection's beginning: it claims the first verse about 'writing faith' means divine assistance, not direct creation. Then it starts discussing the second verse about making laugh and weep.
The objection is incomplete; we only see the first aspect of the discussion on the second verse.
4
Reflect on the proof's logic
Consider how the parallel attribution supports the claim that an action is both the servant's acquisition and God's creation. Think about why this is a strong argument within the theological framework.
This connects to the learning objective.

الحجة الثالثة : إنه تعالى أضاف أعمال العباد إليهم ، ثم إنه تعالى أضافها بأعيانها إلى نفسه . وذلك يدل على أن فعل العبد ، فعل الله تعالى بيان المقدمة الأولى ، بآيات : إحداها : قوله تعالى : ( يا أيها الذين آمنوا : آمنوا ) ثم قال : ( كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان ) وذلك يدل على أن إيمان العبد فعل الله تعالى ، والعبد معاً. وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( فليضحكوا قليلاً ، وليبكوا كثيراً ) ثم قال : ( وأنه هو أضحك وأبكى ) وثالثها : قوله تعالى : ( قل سيروا في الأرض ) وقال : ( هو الذي يسيرهم في البر والبحر ) ورابعها : قوله تعالى : ( فلم تقتلوهم . ولكن الله قتلهم ، وما رميت إذ رميت ولكن الله رمى ) فأضاف قتلهم ورميهم إلى نفسه وخامسها : قوله تعالى : ( إذ أخرجه الذين كفروا ) ثم قال : ( كما أخرجك ربك من بيتك بالحق ) فإن قيل : أما قوله : ( كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان ) فالمراد منه : الإعانة ، وهو خلق الألطاف . وأما قوله : ( هو أضحك وأبكى ) فالكلام فيه من وجهين : الأول : لا نسلم أن أضحك ، عبارة عن إيجاد الضحك ، بل المضحك والمبكي من
The third proof: God Almighty attributed the actions of the servants to them, then God Almighty attributed them identically to Himself. That indicates that the action of the servant is the action of God Almighty. Explanation of the first premise with verses: One of them: His statement: (O you who believe, believe) then He said: (He has written faith in their hearts) and that indicates that the faith of the servant is the act of God Almighty and the servant together. And the second: His statement: (Then let them laugh a little and weep much) then He said: (And that He is the one who makes laugh and makes weep). And the third: His statement: (Say, travel in the land) and He said: (He is the one who causes you to travel on land and sea). And the fourth: His statement: (You did not kill them, but God killed them, and you did not throw when you threw, but God threw) so He attributed their killing and his throwing to Himself. And the fifth: His statement: (When those who disbelieved expelled him) then He said: (As your Lord expelled you from your house with truth). If it is said: As for His saying: (He has written faith in their hearts), what is meant by it is assistance, and it is the creation of divine graces. And as for His saying: (He is the one who makes laugh and makes weep), the discussion about it has two aspects: First: we do not concede that 'makes laugh' is an expression for creating laughter, but rather the one who makes laugh and the one who make weep are from...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof based on verses where God attributes the same actions to both humans and Himself. The author first states the premise: God attributes actions to humans and then attributes those very same actions to Himself. This indicates that the action is both human and divine. He then lists five sets of verses as evidence: (1) 'O you who believe, believe' followed by 'He wrote faith in their hearts'; (2) 'let them laugh a little and weep much' followed by 'He makes laugh and makes weep'; (3) 'say travel in the land' followed by 'He causes you to travel'; (4) 'you did not kill them but God killed them; you did not throw but God threw'; (5) 'when those who disbelieved expelled him' followed by 'as your Lord expelled you'. Then an objection is raised: the first verse about writing faith means divine assistance and creating graces, not direct creation. As for the second verse about making laugh and weep, the objection begins with two aspects, but the text cuts off before completing the objection or giving the full answer. The passage is incomplete, but the method of proof is clear: the parallel attributions show that human actions are also God's acts.

All Acts Are from Allah: Qur'an 4:78
Ar-Razi presents the fourteenth proof using Qur'an 4:78.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ar-Razi uses Qur'an 4:78 to prove that all human acts, both obedience and sin, are created by Allah.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's own terms show that all good and evil, including our actions, come from Allah.

This lesson examines the fourteenth proof from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's al-Matalib al-Aliyah. Ar-Razi takes a verse often cited in debates about divine predestination and unpacks its implications. He shows that the key terms 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' in the Qur'an refer not only to external blessings and calamities but also to human acts of obedience and disobedience. This semantic point is crucial for the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the argument is structured: first clarifying the meaning of key terms, then applying them to the verse.
The passage uses two supporting verses to establish the semantic range.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah'
Ar-Razi explains that 'hasanah' can mean both a blessing and an act of obedience, while 'sayyi'ah' can mean both a calamity and an act of disobedience. Identify these from the supporting verses.
Qur'an 7:168 uses 'hasanāt' and 'sayyi'āt' in the context of testing, and 11:114 uses 'hasanāt' for good deeds that erase bad deeds.
3
Follow the logical inference
The verse 4:78 says 'if a good thing befalls them... if a bad thing befalls them... Say: All is from Allah.' Since the terms are general and include acts, it follows that all acts are from Allah.
The key is the generality of the verse and the inclusion of moral acts under these terms.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ar-Razi concludes that all acts of obedience and disobedience are from Allah. Consider why this matters for the doctrine of divine creation of actions.
This proof supports the Ash'ari position that Allah creates all actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة عشر : قوله تعالى : ( وإن تصبهم حسنة . يقولوا : هذه من عند الله ، وإن تصبهم سيئة . يقولوا : هذه من عندك . قل : كل من عند الله ) وجه الاستدلال : إن لفظ السيئة يقع على البلية تارة ، وعلى المعصية أخرى . وأيضاً : الحسنة تقع على النعمة تارة ، وعلى الطاعة أخرى . قال تعالى : ( وبلوناهم بالحسنات والسيئات. لعلهم يرجعون ) وقال : ( إن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات ) أي الطاعات والمعاصي.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إن قوله : ( إن تصبهم حسنة ) يفيد العموم في كل الحسنات . وقوله : ( وإن تصبهم سيئة ) يفيد العموم في كل السيئات . ثم قال بعده : ( قل : كل من عند الله ) فهذا تصريح بان الحسنات جميع والسيئات من الله تعالى . ولما ثبت أن الطاعات والمعاصي داخلتان تحت اسم الحسنة والسيئة ، ودل هذا النص على أن جميع الحسنات والسيئات من الله ، لزم القطع بأن جميع المعاصي والطاعات من الله تعالى
The fourteenth proof: His saying, the Most High: (And if a good thing befalls them, they say, 'This is from Allah.' And if a bad thing befalls them, they say, 'This is from you.' Say: All is from Allah). The method of inference: The word 'sayyi'ah' (evil/bad) applies to calamity sometimes, and to disobedience another time. And also: 'hasanah' (good) applies to blessing sometimes, and to obedience another time. The Most High said: (And We tested them with good things and bad things, that perhaps they would return) and He said: (Indeed good deeds remove bad deeds), meaning acts of obedience and acts of disobedience.
Once you know this, we say: His saying: (If a good thing befalls them) indicates generality in all good things. And His saying: (And if a bad thing befalls them) indicates generality in all bad things. Then He said after it: (Say: All is from Allah). So this is a declaration that all good things and bad things are from Allah, the Most High. And since it is established that acts of obedience and acts of disobedience are included under the term 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah', and this text indicates that all good things and bad things are from Allah, it necessarily follows that all acts of disobedience and acts of obedience are from Allah, the Most High.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the fourteenth proof that all acts, including human obedience and sin, are created by Allah. The proof is based on Qur'an 4:78. The author first explains that the words 'hasanah' (good) and 'sayyi'ah' (evil) in the Qur'an can refer to both worldly blessings/calamities and to acts of obedience/disobedience. He cites two other verses to show this dual usage: 'We tested them with good things and bad things' (7:168) and 'Good deeds remove bad deeds' (11:114), where the latter refers to acts. Then he applies this to the verse. Since the verse says 'if a good thing befalls them' and 'if a bad thing befalls them' in general, and then concludes 'Say: All is from Allah', it follows that all such things—including both types of 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah'—are from Allah. And since acts of obedience and disobedience are included in these categories, they too must be from Allah. The argument is straightforward: the verse is general, the categories include moral acts, therefore all moral acts are divinely created.

السيئة
الطاعة
المعصية
خلق الأعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse only refers to external events like blessings and calamities, not to human actions.
Correction: Ar-Razi shows through other Qur'anic verses that the terms 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' also apply to acts of obedience and disobedience, so the verse's scope includes moral actions.

Checkpoint

C) Other Qur'anic verses that use these terms for acts
D) A logical deduction from the verse's context

Key Takeaway
Ar-Razi's proof from Qur'an 4:78 establishes that all human actions, whether obedient or sinful, are created by Allah, because the verse's general terms include such acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The verse says even your acts of obedience are from Allah. How does that affect your worship? Are you tempted to think you have earned something, or does it drive you to thankfulness and humility? What is one specific act of worship you can perform today with this mindset?

Objection and Partial Refutation on 'Hasanah' and 'Sayyi'ah'
Ar-Razi enumerates four objections to the moral interpretation of 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah', then begins a refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised by Ar-Razi against interpreting 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' as moral acts, and how the author begins to refute them by distinguishing between types of evil.

Core Idea
Objections to the verse's moral meaning unmask the deeper debate over divine causality and human responsibility.

In this passage, Ar-Razi presents four arguments that the Qur'anic terms 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' refer only to material prosperity and drought, not to acts of obedience and sin. Then the author begins his response by examining the context of subsequent verses. Understanding this objection and its partial refutation is key to grasping the kalam discourse on divine agency and human acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's first three arguments
Notice how Ar-Razi uses consensus (first argument), linguistic usage (second), and homonymy (third) to argue that the verse refers to material conditions.
The third argument relies on the principle that a homonym cannot bear two meanings simultaneously.
2
Examine the fourth argument and its two sub-points
The fourth argument cites subsequent verses to show that the intended meaning is not moral. Pay attention to the two sub-points: the wonder verse and the 'from yourself' verse.
This argument begins to bridge into the refutation.
3
Identify the author's refutation strategy
The author responds by distinguishing between 'sayyi'ah' as calamity (from Allah) and as sin (from the servant). See how he reconciles the verses.
The refutation is cut off but the direction is clear.
4
Note the incomplete end and connect to the larger argument
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the partial refutation shows the author's method: differentiating meanings of the same word to resolve apparent contradictions.
This sets up the need for a complete reply in later sections.

Source Text

فإن قيل : المراد ههنا بالحسنة والسيئة ، ليس هو الطاعة والمعصية .ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : اتفاق الكل على أن هذه الآية نازلة في معنى الخصب والجدب قال المفسرون : كانت المدينة مملوءة من النعم ، وقت مقدم النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فلما ظهر عناد اليهود ونفاق المنافقين، أمسك الله عنهم بعض الإمساك ، كما جرت عادته في جميع الأمم . قال تعالى : ( وما أرسلنا في قرية ، من نبي إلا أخذنا أهلها) فعند هذا قال اليهود والمنافقون : ما رأينا أعظم شؤماً من هذا الرجل. نقصت ثمارنا، وغلت أسعارنا منذ قدم فكان قوله : إن تصبهم حسنة ) يعني الخصب ورخص السعر وتتابع الأمطار . قالوا : ( هذا من عند الله ، وإن تصبهم سيئة ) وهي الجدب وغلاء الأسعار . قالوا : هذا من شؤم محمد - Then it is said: What is meant here by 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' is not obedience and disobedience. Evidence for this includes several aspects:
First: The consensus of all that this verse was revealed concerning the meaning of fertility and drought. The commentators said: Medina was full of blessings at the time of the Prophet's (peace be upon him) arrival. When the stubbornness of the Jews and the hypocrisy of the hypocrites became apparent, Allah withheld some of it, as was His custom with all nations. The Most High said: (And We did not send a prophet to any city but that We seized its people). Then the Jews and hypocrites said: We have not seen anyone more ill-omened than this man. Our fruits have decreased and our prices have risen since he came. So His saying: 'If a good thing befalls them' means fertility, low prices, and successive rains. They said: 'This is from Allah.' 'And if a bad thing befalls them,' which is drought and high prices, they said: 'This is from the ill-omen of Muhammad (peace be upon him).' So it is established that what is meant here by 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' is not acts of obedience and disobedience, but rather low prices and famine.
Second: When 'hasanah' is intended to mean good and obedience, one does not say 'it befell me' but rather 'I did it'. In the speech of the Arabs, it is not said: 'A good thing befell so-and-so' in the sense that he did a good deed, nor 'a bad thing befell him' in the sense that he committed a sin. Rather, we say: If it meant what you mention, He would have said: 'If you do a good deed.'
Third: The word 'hasanah' is used homonymously for acts of obedience and for worldly benefits. Here the commentators agree that benefits are intended, so it is impossible that acts of obedience be intended, since it is not permissible to use a homonym in both of its meanings simultaneously.
Fourth: The Most High said after this verse what indicates that what is meant by 'hasanah' and 'sayyi'ah' in these verses is not acts of obedience and disobedience. Its explanation is in two ways:
The first: The Most High said after this verse: 'So what is the matter with these people that they can hardly understand any statement?' If knowledge and recognition were by the creation of Allah, this wonder would have no meaning. Because the reason that this knowledge does not occur, according to those who say that the Creator of the actions of servants is Allah, is that He did not create them and bring them into existence. And that negates this wonder. So when this wonder occurred, it indicates that knowledge and recognition are only attained by the servant's own bringing into existence.
The second: The Most High said after this verse: 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah, and whatever evil befalls you is from yourself.' The method of inference from it: The word 'sayyi'ah' sometimes applies to calamity and trial, and sometimes applies to sin and disobedience. Then the Most High attributed the evil to Himself in His saying: 'Say: All is from Allah.' And He attributed the evil in the last part of this verse to the servant in His saying: 'And whatever evil befalls you is from yourself.' Reconciliation between these two verses is necessary. When 'sayyi'ah' in the sense of calamity and trial is attributed to Allah, it becomes necessary that 'sayyi'ah' in the sense of disobedience be attributed to the servant, so that
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous proof, followed by a refutation that is partially preserved. The objector argues that the verse refers only to material prosperity and drought, not to moral acts. He gives four arguments: (1) Consensus among commentators that the verse was revealed about fertility and drought, citing the historical context of Medina; (2) Linguistic usage: one says 'a good deed befell me' (اصابتني حسنة) is not Arabic; rather, it would be 'you did a good deed' (أصبتم حسنة); (3) The word 'hasanah' is a homonym (مشترك) meaning both obedience and worldly benefits, but here the commentators agree it means benefits, so it cannot mean obedience; (4) The subsequent verses indicate that the intended meaning is not moral acts, explained in two ways. The first is that the verse 'What is the matter with these people that they can hardly understand any statement?' implies that understanding comes from the servant's own action, not from Allah's creation, otherwise the wonder would be pointless. The second is the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' (4:79), which attributes evil to the servant, while the earlier verse attributes all to Allah. To reconcile, 'sayyi'ah' as calamity is from Allah, but 'sayyi'ah' as sin is from the servant. The passage cuts off here, but the argument is that the objection fails because the context shows the verse applies to both types, but the answer is that moral evil is attributable to the servant in some sense. Note: The author seems to be presenting the objection and then beginning a refutation that distinguishes between types of evil. The answer is incomplete in the scanned text.
