Answering Objections to a Quranic Contradiction
Author answers two objections to his resolution of an apparent Quranic contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his resolution of an apparent Quranic contradiction against two key objections: a variant reading and the Mu'tazilite position on human actions.

Core Idea
A mutawatir reading cannot be overturned by a shadhdh reading, and 'from Allah' means empowerment, not direct creation.

In this passage, the author has claimed to remove an apparent contradiction between two adjacent verses. But objections arise: does a variant reading undermine his argument? And what about the Mu'tazilite claim that all actions come from the servant? The author answers both, showing the strength of his position through careful reasoning about the authority of transmitted readings and the semantics of attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic passage slowly. Pay attention to the structure: it starts with a claim, then presents two objections with their answers. Notice the conjunctive phrases 'يزول التناقض' and 'ولا يقال'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two objections
The author mentions two objections: first, about a variant reading; second, about Mu'tazilite doctrine. Underline or note them.
3
Examine the answer to the first objection
The author gives two reasons why the variant reading does not undermine the argument. First, the reading is rejected. Second, even if considered, it cannot contradict the mutawatir reading.
This protects the integrity of the transmitted Quran.
4
Examine the answer to the second objection
The author clarifies the meaning of 'from so-and-so'. Good deeds are 'from Allah' in the sense of empowerment and aid, while evil deeds are 'from the servant' in the sense of creation.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction with Mu'tazilite views.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the author's responses together remove the contradiction. Note that the argument relies on the authority of mutawatir readings and a nuanced understanding of divine attribution.
This is a classic example of Islamic theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يزول التناقض بين هاتين الآيتين المتجاورتين ولا يقال : هذا مدفوع من وجهين : الأول : إن بعضهم قرأ ( فمن نفسك ) ؟ الثاني : إنه تعالى أضاف الحسنة إلى نفسه ، حيث قال : ( ما أصابك من حسنة فمن الله ) تعالى ، وعند المعتزلة : أن الطاعات والمعاصي كلها من العباد - لأنا نقول : أما الأول . فجوابه : من وجهين : الأول : إن هذه القراءة مهجورة مردودة ، وأنها جارية مجرى تغيير القرآن على سبيل التصحيف والتحريف . وهي طريقة مذمومة الثاني : إن القراءة التي تمسكنا بها ، لا يمكن الطعن فيها . فإنها متواترة . تدل على قولنا . وإذا ثبت ذلك فنقول : هذه القراءة الشاذة . إن كانت منافية لتلك القراءة المتواترة ، وجب القطع بكونها مردودة ، وإن لم تكن منافية لها ، فهي لا تضرنا في هذا المطلوب وأما الثاني فجوابه : إنا إذا قلنا في الشيء المعين : إنه من فلان . فقد يراد منه : أنه بتخليقه وتكوينه . وقد يراد به : أنه هو الذي أقدره عليه وأعانه عليه وأزال الموانع العائقة منه ، وحصل الشرائط المعتبرة فيه إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : أما كون السيئة من العبد ، فليس المراد أنها حصلت بإقدار العبد . وذلك لأن القدرة على المعصية ، ما حصلت من العبد البتة . ولما تعذر حمل اللفظ على هذا ، وجب حمله على أن المراد منه : أنها حصلت بإيجاده وتكوينه . وأما كون الحسنة من الله ، فهو يحتمل أن يكون المراد منه : أنها حصلت بإقدار الله وبإعانته عليه ، فوجب حمل اللفظ عليه ، إزالة للتناقض بين الآيات
The contradiction between these two adjacent verses is removed. And it is not said: This is refuted from two aspects: First: Some read (But from yourself?). Second: He (Exalted) attributed the good to Himself, where He said: (Whatever good befalls you is from Allah) Exalted, and according to the Mu'tazila: all acts of obedience and disobedience are from the servants. But we say: As for the first, its answer is from two aspects: First: This reading is abandoned and rejected, and it runs in the course of changing the Quran by way of falsification and alteration, and it is a blameworthy method. Second: The reading to which we hold cannot be impugned, for it is mutawatir (widely transmitted) and supports our statement. And if that is established, we say: This irregular reading, if it contradicts that mutawatir reading, then it must be definitively rejected, and if it does not contradict it, then it does not harm us in this objective. And as for the second, its answer: We, when we say concerning a specific thing that it is from so-and-so, it may mean that it is by his creation and formation, and it may mean that he is the one who empowered him over it, aided him in it, removed the hindering obstacles from it, and obtained the conditions considered in it. If this is established, we say: As for the evil being from the servant, the intended meaning is not that it occurred by the servant's empowerment, because ability for disobedience never occurs from the servant at all. And since it is impossible to carry the expression to this, it must be carried to mean that it occurred by his bringing into existence and formation. And as for the good being from Allah, it is possible that the intended meaning is that it occurred by Allah's empowering and aiding him. So it was necessary to carry the expression to that, removing the contradiction between the verses.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The author claims that the apparent contradiction between two adjacent verses is resolved. An objector raises two objections: first, a variant reading might affect the interpretation; second, the Mu'tazilite position that all actions come from the servant contradicts the verse attributing good to Allah. The author answers the first objection with two points: (a) the variant reading is rejected as irregular and not mutawatir; (b) even if it were considered, it does not contradict the mutawatir reading, so it does not harm the argument. For the second objection, he explains that phrases like 'from so-and-so' can mean either creation or empowerment. Since the servant never truly has ability for disobedience, 'evil from the servant' means the servant creates it, while 'good from Allah' means Allah empowers and aids. This resolves the contradiction.

Key Terms
التناقض
القراءة الشاذة
المتواترة
الإقدار
التخليق
التكوين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any variant reading can undermine a mutawatir reading, and 'from Allah' always implies direct creation.
Correction: A non-mutawatir reading cannot overturn a mutawatir one, and 'from Allah' can mean empowerment, not direct creation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author defends his resolution by dismissing the variant reading as non-mutawatir and clarifying that 'from Allah' refers to empowerment, not creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do a good deed, do you genuinely feel that it is only through Allah's empowerment and aid, or do you secretly take pride in your own ability? This lesson forces you to examine whether you truly attribute your goodness to Allah or to yourself.
?

?
If you encountered a Quranic verse that seemed difficult, would you be tempted to accept a weak interpretation or variant reading that made it easier for you? This lesson warns against such carelessness. Are you willing to submit to the mutawatir reading even when it challenges your desires?

The Broader Meaning of Hasana and Sayyi'a
Objection that hasana/sayyi'a mean only worldly benefit/harm; author refutes with broader meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the terms hasana and sayyi'a in the Qur'an refer not only to worldly benefits and harms but also to acts of obedience and disobedience, and why restricting them is arbitrary.

Core Idea
Hasana and sayyi'a include both worldly outcomes and moral actions.

This lesson addresses an objection that limits the terms 'hasana' and 'sayyi'a' to purely worldly benefit and harm. The author refutes this by showing that these terms also cover acts of obedience and disobedience. Understanding this broader scope is essential for interpreting verses about divine decree and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the clear structure: the opponent's claim followed by the author's rebuttal.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The objector says that 'hasana' and 'sayyi'a' only mean worldly benefits and harms. Find that in the text.
The phrase 'المنافع والمضار' indicates benefits and harms.
3
Understand the author's response
The author says the terms also encompass 'acts of obedience and disobedience' (الطاعات والمعاصي). Notice that he calls the restriction 'pure arbitrariness' (تحكم محض).
This shows the author argues for a comprehensive meaning.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on why this matters for discussions of qadar: if hasana and sayyi'a include moral acts, then divine decree covers both worldly outcomes and moral choices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المراد من الحسنة والسيئة ; المنافع والمضار ، قلنا : قد بينا أن لفظ الحسنة والسيئة ، كما يتناولان المنافع والمضار ، فقد يتناولان الطاعات والمعاصي . فتخصيصه بأحدهما تحكم محض
His statement: 'The intended meaning of hasana (good) and sayyi'a (evil) is benefits and harms.' We said: We have already shown that the words hasana and sayyi'a, just as they encompass benefits and harms, so also they encompass acts of obedience and disobedience. So specifying them to one is pure arbitrariness.

Commentary
This section is an objection-and-answer pair. The opponent claims that when the verses mention 'hasana' and 'sayyi'a', they refer only to worldly benefits and harms, not to moral good and evil. The author responds by stating that these terms have a broader application: they also include acts of obedience and disobedience. Therefore, restricting them solely to worldly benefits is an arbitrary interpretation without justification.

Interpreting Verses: General Wording Over Specific Occasion
Objection and answer on the scope of a verse about hasana and sayyi'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic scholars prioritize the general wording of a verse over its specific occasion of revelation when deriving rulings.

Core Idea
Scriptural rulings follow the general wording, not the specific cause.

Sometimes opponents try to limit a verse's application by pointing to its historical context. In this passage, Al-Matalib al-Aliyah addresses an objection about a verse concerning agricultural prosperity and drought, showing that the principle of 'general wording' overrides the specific occasion. This lesson introduces a foundational usul al-fiqh principle that protects the universal applicability of Qur'anic teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims the verse was only revealed about physical benefits and harms, not about moral good and evil. Note how they restrict the verse's scope.
This is a common type of argument — using the cause of revelation to limit meaning.
2
Identify the author's response
The author gives a concise ruling: 'Consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not to the specificity of the occasion.' Understand why this principle is crucial for theological consistency.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about how this principle applies to other verses. If a verse has a general wording, it applies to all relevant cases, not just the original context.
This protects the universal message of the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : الآية إنما نزلت في المنافع والمضار، والخصب والجدب ) قلنا : العبرة بعموم قوله : اللفظ، لا بخصوص السبب
His statement: 'The verse was only revealed concerning benefits and harms, and fertility and drought.' We said: Consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not to the specificity of the occasion.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The opponent argues that the verse about 'hasana' and 'sayyi'a' was revealed specifically in the context of agricultural prosperity and adversity, so it should not be applied to moral actions. The author gives a classic usul al-fiqh principle: the ruling follows the general wording, not the specific cause. Therefore, the verse's general term applies to all cases, including acts of obedience and disobedience.

Key Terms
العبرة بعموم اللفظ
خصوص السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a verse was revealed about a specific event, it only applies to that event.
Correction: The principle 'al-ibrah bi-umum al-lafz la bi-khusus al-sabab' teaches that the ruling is based on the general wording, not the specific occasion, unless there is evidence to restrict it.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what determines the application of a verse?
A) The specific occasion of revelation
B) The general wording
C) The consensus of scholars
D) The literal meaning only

Key Takeaway
The generality of a verse's wording, not its specific occasion of revelation, determines its scope of application.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you used a specific story or event to avoid applying a clear Quranic principle in your life. What would it cost you to accept the general wording as fully binding on you right now?
?
If you truly believed that every general verse addresses you personally, which area of your life would be most disrupted? Are you ready to let that disruption happen?

Permissibility of Using 'Asaba' for Divine Acts
Objector denies using 'asaba' for acts; author permits it with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author permits using 'asaba' (to befall) for acts of obedience and disobedience, and how this connects to divine tawfiq, 'awn, and khidhlan.

Core Idea
Divine success and abandonment 'befall' us—language reflects qadar.

The objector argues that we cannot say acts of obedience or disobedience 'befell' us. But the author shows that Arabic usage allows phrases like 'divine success befell me,' which refer to God's facilitation. This lesson examines that exchange and its implications for how we speak about divine action and human acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objector makes a linguistic claim, and the author immediately rejects it.
The author's response begins with 'la nusallim' (we do not accept).
2
Identify the key examples
Find the three examples: tawfiq (success), 'awn (aid), and khidhlan (abandonment). Notice how each is used with 'asaba.'
3
Understand the intended meaning
The author clarifies that these expressions refer to divine facilitation for obedience or the weighing of scales toward obedience. This shows that 'asaba' can be used for divine actions affecting human acts.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this linguistic point matters for qadar debates. It shows that common speech already acknowledges divine involvement in human actions.

He provides examples: people do say 'divine success befell me' or 'divine abandonment befell so-and-so'. Refuting the Alleged Contradiction in the Verse
The author refutes the objection of contradiction by denying it and providing examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the interpretation of hasana and sayyi'a as moral acts against the charge of internal contradiction in the Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Attributing evil to oneself does not contradict divine sovereignty.

The opponent argues that interpreting 'hasana' (good) and 'sayyi'a' (evil) in the verse as acts of obedience and disobedience leads to a contradiction: one part says good is from Allah, another says evil is from yourself, implying the servant creates evil. The author denies this contradiction and begins to answer by citing the example of Prophet Ibrahim, who attributed sickness to himself but healing to Allah. This lesson examines the objection and the author's initial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and denial
Start by reading the opponent's claim that interpreting hasana and sayyi'a as moral acts creates a contradiction between the beginning and end of the verse. Notice the author's immediate response: 'We do not accept that.'
2
Examine the first part of the answer
The author first addresses the verse 'What is the matter with these people that they scarcely understand any statement?' He notes that the opponent's argument relies on the idea that praise and blame require the servant to be the agent. The full answer is postponed.
The author says the answer will come later, indicating this is a deferral.
3
Examine the second part: the example of Ibrahim
The author then addresses the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself.' He provides the example of Prophet Ibrahim, who said 'When I am sick, then He heals me.' Ibrahim attributed sickness to himself but healing to Allah.
This shows that attributing evil to oneself is permissible and does not negate Allah's sovereignty.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the Ibrahim example resolves the apparent contradiction. The opponent thought the verse implied the servant creates evil, but the example shows that attributing negative events to oneself is just a linguistic expression, not a claim of independent creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « لو حملنا الحسنة والسيئة هنا على الطاعات والمعاصي ، لزم التناقض بين أول الآية وبين آخرها ، قلنا : لا نسلم . أما قوله تعالى : ( فمال هؤلاء القوم لا يكادون يفقهون حديثاً ) فنقول : وجه تمسك الخصم به : أن المدح والذم لا يصحان ، إلا إذا كان العبد موجداً . وسيأتي الجواب عن هذه الشبهة ، إن شاء الله تعالى . وأما قوله تعالى : ( وما أصابك من سيئة فمن نفسك ) فالجواب عن تمسكهم به من وجوه : الأول : إنه تعالى قال حكاية عن إبراهيم - عليه السلام ـ أنه قال : وإذا مرضت فهو يشفين ) أضاف المرض إلى نفسه ، والشفاء إلى الله . ولم .
His statement: 'If we carry hasana and sayyi'a here to mean acts of obedience and disobedience, a contradiction would be necessary between the beginning of the verse and its end.' We said: We do not accept that. As for His statement Exalted: (What is the matter with these people that they scarcely understand any statement?), we say: The opponent's clinging to this is that praise and blame are not valid unless the servant is the agent. And the answer to this doubt will come, if Allah wills. And as for His statement Exalted: (Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself), the answer to their clinging to it is from several aspects: First: He Exalted narrated from Ibrahim (peace be upon him) that he said: 'And when I am sick, then He heals me.' He attributed the sickness to himself, and the healing to Allah. And not...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This is the final objection-and-answer in the window. The opponent argues that if 'hasana' and 'sayyi'a' are interpreted as moral acts, then the verse would contradict itself because one part says 'whatever good befalls you is from Allah' and another says 'whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' — implying the servant creates evil, not Allah. The author denies the contradiction. He first postpones fully answering the first part about 'praise and blame require the servant to be the agent' (a standard Ash'ari position will come later). Then he addresses the verse 'whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' by using the example of Prophet Ibrahim (AS): Ibrahim attributed sickness to himself but healing to Allah. This shows that one can attribute negative outcomes to oneself without denying divine sovereignty. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is that this is permissible.

Misconception: Attributing evil to oneself (e.g., 'I caused my own sickness') contradicts Allah's absolute sovereignty over all events.
Correction: The example of Ibrahim shows that attributing negative outcomes to oneself is a permissible linguistic expression; it does not deny that Allah is the ultimate creator of all things, including sickness and healing.

The Rhetorical Interpretation of Evil in Q. 4:79
Rhetorical interpretation of evil in Q.4:79 using Ibrahim's analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical commentators interpret the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' as a rhetorical denial, reconciling it with God's sole creation.

Core Idea
Evil is attributed to humans as a rhetorical question, not as creation.

This lesson examines a key objection to God's sole creation: if God creates all, why does the Quran say evil is from ourselves? The author presents the interpretation of Ibrahim's statement as a model for understanding this verse as a rhetorical denial. By recognizing this rhetorical device, the apparent contradiction is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the opening lines that attribute evil to us and good to God, and note that this does not impair God's being Creator of all.
This sets up the problem.
2
Examine the Ibrahim analogy
Understand how commentators interpret Ibrahim's 'This is my Lord' as a rhetorical interrogative of denial, and see the parallel to Q.4:79.
The analogy is the key to the interpretation.
3
Apply the interpretation
See how the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' is interpreted as a rhetorical question, avoiding contradiction with 'All is from Allah'.
This resolves the apparent conflict.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Confirm that the verse is a denial of human creation, not an affirmation, preserving God's sole creatorship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقدح ذلك في إقراره بأن خالق المرض والشفاء، هو الله تعالى . فكذا ههنا . أضاف السيئة إلينا ، والحسنة إلى نفسه . ولا يقدح ذلك في كونه تعالى خالقاً للكل . الثاني : إن أكثر المفسرين . قالوا في تفسير قول إبراهيم - عليه السلام - : ( هذا ربي (۱) ) : أنه ذكر هذا استفهاماً على سبيل الإنكار . كأنه قال : هذا ربي ؟ فههنا يحتمل أن يكون المراد ذلك . كأنه قيل : الإيمان الذي وقع على وفق قصده : من الله . والكفر الذي وقع خلاف قصده : من نفسه . وهذا محال . لأنه لو وقع بإيقاعه ، لما وقع إلا ما قصده واختاره . فلما حكمنا بأن الإيمان الذي وقع على وفق قصده : من الله ، لا منه . فكيف يمكن أن يقال : الكفر الذي وقع على خلاف مقصده : منه ؟ والحاصل : إن قوله : ( وما أصابك من سيئة ، فمن نفسك ؟ ) : محمول على الاستفهام، على سبيل الإنكار ، حتى يلزم التناقض بين أول الآية وآخرها . ومما يدل على أن المراد من قوله : قل : كل من عند الله ) : جميع الممكنات والمحدثات وجهان : الأول : البرهان القاطع العقلي. وهو أن كل موجود . فهو إما واجب لذاته ، وإما ممكن لذاته . والواجب لذاته واحد . وهو الله - سبحانه - والممكن لذاته كل ما سواه . ثم إن الممكنات لذاته، إن استغنى عن المؤثر ، فحينئذ ينسد باب الاستدلال بحدوث العالم وبجوازه على وجود الصانع ، ويلزم نفي الصانع . وأما إن كان الممكن لذاته ، محتاجاً إلى المؤثر ، وجب أن يثبت هذا الحكم في جميع الممكنات ، وأن لا يختلف لكونه حيواناً أو جماداً ، أو فلكاً أو ملكاً ، أو حركة أو سكوناً ، أو فعلاً أو قولاً . لأنه لما كان الإمكان منشأ للحاجة ، وجب تحقق الحاجة في جميع الممكنات . ولأجل قوة هذا البرهان وظهوره . قال سبحانه : ( فمال هؤلاء القوم ، لا يكادون يفقهون حديثاً ) على سبيل التعجب . لأنه لما كان الإمكان هو المنشأ للحاجة . فالحكم في بعض الممكنات بالحاجة ، وفي بعضها بعدم الحاجة : عجيب خارج عن المعقول والوجه الثاني في بيان ما ذكرناه : أنه تعالى قال في آخر هذه الآية : من يطع الرسول ، فقد أطاع الله ، ومن تولى ، فما أرسلناك عليهم حفيظاً ) يعني : ما أرسلناك إليهم . لأجل أن تجعلهم مؤمنين ، عارفين واصلين إلى الحق . فإن ذلك ليس في وسعك . بل لا يحصل ذلك إلا بقدرتي وإرشادي . وهذا آخر تقرير هذه الحجة . والله أعلم
That does not impair his affirmation that the Creator of illness and cure is Allah, the Exalted. So likewise here. He attributed evil to us, and good to Himself. And that does not impair that He, Exalted, is the Creator of all. Second: Most of the commentators said in the interpretation of the saying of Ibrahim, peace be upon him: 'This is my Lord' (Q. 6:76) that he mentioned this as an interrogative in the way of denial. As if he said: 'This is my Lord?' So here it is possible that the intended meaning is that. As if it was said: The faith that occurred in accordance with His intention is from Allah, and the disbelief that occurred contrary to His intention is from himself. And this is impossible. Because if it occurred by His causing, nothing would occur except what He intended and chose. Since we have judged that faith that occurred in accordance with His intention is from Allah, not from him, how can it be said that disbelief that occurred contrary to His intention is from him? The summary: His saying: 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' (Q. 4:79) is interpreted as an interrogative in the way of denial, so that a contradiction arises between the beginning of the verse and its end. And among what indicates that the intended meaning of His saying: 'Say: All is from Allah' (Q. 4:78) is all possible and originated things are two aspects: First: The decisive rational proof. It is that every existent is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. The necessary by itself is one, and He is Allah, Exalted, and the possible by itself is everything other than Him. Then if the possible by itself is independent of the efficient cause, then the door of inference from the temporality of the world and its contingency to the existence of the Maker would be closed, and the denial of the Maker would follow. But if the possible by itself is in need of the efficient cause, then it is necessary to establish this judgment for all possibles, and that it should not differ whether it is an animal or stone, or celestial sphere or angel, or movement or stillness, or action or speech. Because since possibility is the source of need, it is necessary that need be realized in all possibles. Because of the strength of this proof and its clarity, He, Exalted, said: 'What is the matter with these people that they do not understand any statement?' (Q. 4:78) in the way of wonder. Because since possibility is the source of need, judging some possibles to be in need and others not in need is strange and irrational. The second aspect in explaining what we mentioned: that He, Exalted, said at the end of this verse: 'Whoever obeys the Messenger has indeed obeyed Allah; and whoever turns away, We have not sent you as a guardian over them' (Q. 4:80) meaning: We have not sent you to them in order to make them believers, knowers, reaching the truth. For that is not within your capacity. Rather, it is not obtained except by My power and guidance. And this is the end of the exposition of this argument. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defends the attribution of good to God and evil to humans against the objection that it contradicts God being the sole Creator. The author first states that attributing evil to humans and good to God does not impair God's being Creator of all. Then, in the 'second' point, the author explains the interpretation of Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' as a rhetorical denial, analogously suggesting that the phrase 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' is also a rhetorical question, not a statement of creation. This interpretation resolves the apparent contradiction between affirming that all is from God and yet evil is from the self. Next, the author provides two proofs that 'all is from Allah' encompasses all possible and originated things. The first proof is a rational demonstration: every existent is either necessary or possible; the necessary is God, and the possible needs an efficient cause; this need applies universally, so any possible—whether action or thing—must be from God. The second proof cites a Quranic verse indicating that even acts of obedience or disbelief are not within human capacity but only by God's power and guidance. The passage concludes the argument.

Key Terms
حسنة
سيئة
استفهام إنكار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Q. 4:79 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' means humans create their own evil actions independently of God.
Correction: The classical interpretation shows it is a rhetorical denial; God is the sole Creator of all, including evil, but the attribution to humans is for accountability.

Checkpoint
How do the commentators interpret Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' to support the argument?
A) As an affirmation of faith
B) As a rhetorical interrogative of denial
C) As a statement of ignorance
D) As a metaphor for seeking guidance

Key Takeaway
Evil attributed to humans in Q. 4:79 is a rhetorical denial, not an affirmation of independent creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'it was written for me' to avoid feeling guilty? How does this lesson's interpretation of evil from yourself confront that excuse?
?
Consider a hardship you faced. Did you believe it was from God or from your own actions? How does the rhetorical reading of Q. 4:79 change how you see that trial?
?
The passage uses Ibrahim's rhetorical question as a model. Have you ever used questioning to deny false ideas in your own heart? What would it take to sincerely say 'this is my Lord?' as a denial of anything else?

Two Proofs That All Things Are from Allah
Two proofs that all possible things are from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof (based on necessity and possibility) and the Quranic proof (from Q. 4:80) that all possible and originated things are from Allah alone.

Core Idea
Possibility is the source of need; therefore every possible thing requires God's causation.

Having established that the verse 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' is a rhetorical denial, the author now provides two proofs that 'All is from Allah' refers to all possible and originated things. The first is a decisive rational argument based on the distinction between the Necessary and the possible. The second is a Quranic reference showing that even human actions are within God's power alone.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational proof
Learn the distinction between the Necessary Being (God) and possible beings, and the consequence if a possible were independent of an efficient cause.
This is the core logical argument.
2
Note the universal application
Recognize that possibility necessitates need for all possibles, without exception, whether in animate or inanimate, actions or speech.
The argument covers all categories.
3
Appreciate the strength of the proof
See how the Quran itself expresses wonder that some deny this clear reasoning.
The verse Q.4:78 is cited as a wonder.
4
Examine the second proof
Read the Quranic verse Q.4:80 that shows even obedience and disobedience are not within human capacity but only by God's power and guidance.
This confirms the rational proof.
5
Conclude the argument
Synthesize that both proofs together establish that all things, including human actions, are from Allah.

Source Text

It is that every existent is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. The necessary by itself is one, and He is Allah, Exalted, and the possible by itself is everything other than Him. Then if the possible by itself is independent of the efficient cause, then the door of inference from the temporality of the world and its contingency to the existence of the Maker would be closed, and the denial of the Maker would follow. But if the possible by itself is in need of the efficient cause, then it is necessary to establish this judgment for all possibles, and that it should not differ whether it is an animal or stone, or celestial sphere or angel, or movement or stillness, or action or speech. Because since possibility is the source of need, it is necessary that need be realized in all possibles. Because of the strength of this proof and its clarity, He, Exalted, said: 'What is the matter with these people that they do not understand any statement?' (Q. 4:78) in the way of wonder. Because since possibility is the source of need, judging some possibles to be in need and others not in need is strange and irrational. The second aspect in explaining what we mentioned: that He, Exalted, said at the end of this verse: 'Whoever obeys the Messenger has indeed obeyed Allah; and whoever turns away, We have not sent you as a guardian over them' (Q. 4:80) meaning: We have not sent you to them in order to make them believers, knowers, reaching the truth. For that is not within your capacity. Rather, it is not obtained except by My power and guidance. And this is the end of the exposition of this argument. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defends the attribution of good to God and evil to humans against the objection that it contradicts God being the sole Creator. The author first states that attributing evil to humans and good to God does not impair God's being Creator of all. Then, in the 'second' point, the author explains the interpretation of Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' as a rhetorical denial, analogously suggesting that the phrase 'Whatever evil befalls you is from yourself' is also a rhetorical question, not a statement of creation. This interpretation resolves the apparent contradiction between affirming that all is from God and yet evil is from the self. Next, the author provides two proofs that 'all is from Allah' encompasses all possible and originated things. The first proof is a rational demonstration: every existent is either necessary or possible; the necessary is God, and the possible needs an efficient cause; this need applies universally, so any possible—whether action or thing—must be from God. The second proof cites a Quranic verse indicating that even acts of obedience or disbelief are not within human capacity but only by God's power and guidance. The passage concludes the argument.

Key Terms
برهان عقلي
ممكن لذاته
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some actions or beings are independent of God's creation because they result from human free will.
Correction: The rational proof shows that all possibles, including actions, are in need of an efficient cause, which is God.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, what would follow if a possible being were independent of an efficient cause?
A) It would still need God
B) The door of inference to the existence of the Maker would be closed
C) It would become necessary by itself
D) Nothing would change

Key Takeaway
Every possible being, including human actions, is caused by Allah; denying this for any possible would undermine the proof for God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your every action—including your sins—is from Allah, how does that change your view of your own disobedience? Are you willing to accept that even your ability to repent is a gift from Him, not your own doing?
?
The proof shows that possibility is the source of need. In what area of your life are you living as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that you are entirely dependent on Allah's causation at every moment?
?
If even the Prophet could not make people believe, where do you place your trust when you call others to Allah? Are you trying to control outcomes that only Allah can bring about?

Proving Disbelief is from Allah
Two arguments proving disbelief is from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments used by scholars to prove that disbelief, like faith, is from Allah.

Core Idea
If faith is from Allah, disbelief must also be from Allah — no one can be the source of only one opposite.

After establishing that faith is from Allah, the author turns to the question of disbelief. Some might think that disbelief could be from the servant while faith is from God, but the author provides two arguments to show this is inconsistent. The first is based on consensus, the second on logical analysis of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Note the structure: it begins with a premise, then presents two arguments.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument is a simple appeal to consensus: no one distinguishes between faith and disbelief in terms of their source. Identify this in the text.
3
Analyze the second argument
Follow the second argument step by step. It uses a conditional: if the servant could create disbelief, then that power would either also create faith or not. Each branch leads to a contradiction. Break it down.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The conclusion is that since faith is not from the servant, disbelief cannot be either. Understand why this follows logically.
This argument relies on the principle that power cannot be limited to one opposite without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن الإيمان من الله ، وأردنا أن نبين أيضاً : أن الكفر من الله تعالى . فلنا فيه وجوه : الأول : إنه لا قائل بالفرق . الثاني : العبد لو قدر على إيجاد الكفر . فالقدرة الصالحة لإيجاد الكفر . إما أن تكون صالحة لإيجاد الإيمان، أو لا تكون . فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يعود ذلك إلى القول بأن إيمان العبد منه . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون القادر على الشيء غير قادر على ضده . وذلك عندهم باطل . وأيضاً : على هذا التقدير تكون القدرة موجبة للمقدور . وذلك عندهم يمنع من كون القادر قادراً على الفعل . فثبت : أنه لما لم يكن الإيمان منه ، وجب أن لا يكون الكفر منه.
And when it is established that faith is from Allah, and we want to also demonstrate that disbelief is from Allah, Exalted, we have arguments for it: First: There is no one who differentiates (between them). Second: If the servant were capable of bringing about disbelief, then the power capable of bringing about disbelief is either capable of bringing about faith or not. If the first, then that would return to the saying that the servant's faith is from him. If the second, then one capable of a thing is not capable of its opposite, and that is invalid according to them. Also, on this assumption, power necessitates the thing capable, and that according to them prevents the capable one from being capable of the action. So it is established: since faith is not from him, it must be that disbelief is not from him either.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
Following the proof that faith is from Allah, this passage offers arguments to show that disbelief must also be from Allah. The author presents two arguments. The first is a consensus argument: there is no one who differentiates between the two (i.e., no scholar claims faith is from God but disbelief is from man). The second is a logical argument based on power. Suppose the servant has power to create disbelief. That power must either also be capable of creating faith or not. If it can create faith, then faith would be from the servant, contradicting the previous conclusion. If it cannot create faith, then the power would be limited to one act and unable to produce its opposite, which the author claims is invalid according to the opponents' own principles. Additionally, such a power would necessitate its effect, preventing free choice. Therefore, since faith is not from the servant, disbelief cannot be from the servant either, so it must be from Allah.

Key Terms
الكفر
القدرة
الضد
القادر
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disbelief is from the servant while faith is from Allah.
Correction: The arguments show that this is inconsistent; if faith is from Allah, disbelief must also be from Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument's main conclusion?
A) The servant can create both faith and disbelief
B) Disbelief must be from Allah because faith is from Allah
C) Power can only produce one of two opposites
D) The servant has no power at all

Key Takeaway
Disbelief, like faith, is from Allah, as shown by consensus and logical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If disbelief is from Allah, how does that change the way you view someone who rejects faith? Are you more compassionate or more fearful? What does this lesson demand of you in how you treat non-Muslims?
?
This argument shows that your own faith is not ultimately from your effort. Does that humble you or make you complacent? What would it mean to truly live as if your faith is a gift from Allah?
?
If you ever struggle with doubts or moments of disbelief, does knowing that disbelief is from Allah relieve you or terrify you? How can you use this truth to draw closer to Allah rather than despair?

Guidance is from Allah Alone
Concludes a proof by citing Quran 4:80 on divine guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why faith and guidance to truth are exclusively from Allah's power and guidance, not from human effort.

Core Idea
Guidance to faith comes only from Allah’s power, not human capacity.

In the previous proof, the author argued that faith originates from Allah. Here he reinforces that argument with a second aspect: a direct citation from the Qur'an. The verse 'Whoever obeys the Messenger has obeyed Allah...' is used to show that even the Prophet himself cannot compel belief—only Allah can grant guidance. This lesson unpacks that verse and the underlying theological principle of divine sovereignty over guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and verse citation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author quotes the Qur'an and then interprets it to support his argument.
The verse is from Surah An-Nisa (4:80).
2
Understand the Prophet's role
The author explains that the Prophet was not sent to force people to become believers. This refutes the idea that guidance is within human power, even for the Prophet.
3
Grasp the exclusive divine role
The passage concludes that faith only occurs 'by My power and My guidance' — meaning it is entirely Allah's act, not a human achievement. This is the core theological point.
This reinforces the doctrine of qadar (divine decree) regarding guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممكنات بالحاجة ، وفي بعضها بعدم الحاجة : عجيب خارج عن المعقول والوجه الثاني في بيان ما ذكرناه : أنه تعالى قال في آخر هذه الآية : من يطع الرسول ، فقد أطاع الله ، ومن تولى ، فما أرسلناك عليهم حفيظاً ) يعني : ما أرسلناك إليهم . لأجل أن تجعلهم مؤمنين ، عارفين واصلين إلى الحق فإن ذلك ليس في وسعك . بل لا يحصل ذلك إلا بقدرتي وإرشادي وهذا آخر تقرير هذه الحجة . والله أعلم
The possible things by need, and some by lack of need: strange outside of reason. And the second aspect in explaining what we mentioned: that He, exalted, said at the end of this verse: 'Whoever obeys the Messenger has obeyed Allah, and whoever turns away, We have not sent you as a guardian over them' meaning: We have not sent you to them in order that you make them believers, knowers, reaching the truth. For that is not within your capacity. Rather, that does not occur except by My power and My guidance. And this is the end of the exposition of this proof. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of an earlier proof (likely the fourteenth) concerning the origin of faith. The author first notes a strange claim about contingent things being linked to need or lack of need, which is irrational. He then presents the second aspect of his argument: citing the verse 'Whoever obeys the Messenger has obeyed Allah...' (Quran 4:80). He explains that Allah did not send the Prophet to force people to become believers—that is not within human power. Rather, faith and guidance to the truth come solely through Allah's power (qudrah) and guidance (irshad). The passage ends by declaring this the final exposition of that proof.

Faith is a Good Deed from Allah
Al-Razi defines faith as a good deed and deduces it is from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first part of the fifteenth proof: how faith is defined as a good deed and why that implies it is from Allah.

Core Idea
Faith is a good deed because it is free from ugliness, and every good deed is from Allah.

Imam al-Razi begins his fifteenth proof by establishing that faith is a good deed. He defines a good deed as an action free from all aspects of ugliness, then supports this with Quranic references. Then he argues that every good deed is from Allah using the verse 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah' (4:79). This leads to the conclusion that faith is from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Define a good deed
Read the author's definition: a good deed is an action free from all aspects of ugliness. Compare this with faith.
This is the first premise.
2
Find the Quranic evidence
Notice the two Quranic verses used to support that faith is a good deed. Identify which verses they are.
The verses are 41:33 and 16:90, interpreted as the testimony of faith.
3
See the second premise
Read how the author proves that every good deed is from Allah using the verse 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah' (4:79).
The indefinite noun in conditional context implies generality.
4
Draw the conclusion
Combine the two premises to conclude that faith is from Allah.
This is the desired result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشر : الإيمان حسنة ، وكل حسنة فمن الله ، ينتج : أن الإيمان من الله . بيان المقدمة الأولى : إن الحسنة هي الفعل الخالي عن جميع جهات القبح ، والإيمان كذلك . فكان حسنة . ولأنهم اتفقوا على أن قوله : ومن أحسن قولاً : من دعا إلى الله ) (۱) المراد به : كلمة الشهادة وقيل في قوله : ( إن الله يأمر بالعدل والإحسان ) (۲) : إن ذلك هو قول : لا إله إلا الله . فثبت : أن الإيمان حسنة . وإنما قلنا : إن كل حسنة فمن الله . لقوله تعالى : ( ما أصابك من حسنة فمن الله ) وهو نكرة في موضع الشرط، فيفيد الاستغراق . وإذا ثبت أن الإيمان من الله ، وأردنا أن نبين أيضاً : أن الكفر من الله تعالى . فلـنـا فـيـه وجـوه : الأول : إنه لا قائل بالفرق . الثاني : العبد لو قدر على إيجاد الكفر . فالقدرة الصالحة لإيجاد الكفر . إما أن تكون صالحة لإيجاد الإيمان، أو لا تكون . فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يعـود ذلك إلى القول بأن إيمان العبد منه . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون القادر على الشيء غير قادر على ضده . وذلك عندهم باطل . وأيضاً : على هذا التقدير تكون القدرة موجبة للمقدور . وذلك عندهم يمنع من كون القادر قادراً على الفعل . فثبت : أنه لما لم يكن الإيمان منه ، وجب أن لا يكون الكفر منه . الثالث : إن العبد لما لم يكن موجداً للإيمان . فبأن لا يكون موجداً للكفر أولى . وذلك لأنك لا ترى في الدنيا إنساناً يرضى بالكفر والجهل والضلال . بل إنما يريد الحق والإيمان. فلما نص تعالى على أن الإيمان الذي وقع على قصده ، ليس منه ، بل من الله . والكفر الذي وقع على خلاف قصده بأن لا يكون منه ، بل من الله : كان أولى . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من كون الإيمان من الله ، هو أن الله أقدره عليه ، وهداه إليه ؟ قلنا : فعلى هذا التقدير . الذي من الله هو الاقدار والتمكين . فأما تعين الإيمان فليس من الله . وهو على خلاف الآية وأيضاً : فجميع الشرائط مشتركة بالنسبة إلى الإيمان والكفر . وهو القدرة ، والعقل . والدلائل . ثم إن العبد باختيار نفسه، أوجد أحد الضدين دون الآخر . فلا بد وأن يكون ذلك بإعانة الله ، وبترجيح داعيته على ما حققنا هذا الكلام في دليل الداعي . وهو المطلوب . والله أعلم
The fifteenth proof: Faith is a good deed, and every good deed is from Allah, resulting in: faith is from Allah. Explanation of the first premise: The good deed is the action free from all aspects of ugliness, and faith is likewise. So it is a good deed. And because they agreed that His saying 'And who is better in speech than one who invites to Allah' (41:33) is intended as the word of testimony, and it was said in His saying 'Indeed, Allah commands justice and excellence' (16:90) that it is the saying 'There is no god but Allah'. So it is established that faith is a good deed. And we say: every good deed is from Allah due to His saying 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah' (4:79), and it is an indefinite noun in the condition position, so it indicates generality. And when it is established that faith is from Allah, and we also want to show that disbelief is from Allah the Exalted, we have three aspects: First: There is no one who makes a distinction. Second: If the servant had power to create disbelief, the power that is valid for creating disbelief: either it is valid for creating faith or not. If the first, then that returns to the saying that the servant's faith is from him. If the second, then the one who is able to do something is not able to do its opposite, and that is invalid according to them. Also: on this assumption, the power necessitates the thing willed, and that according to them prevents the capable one from being capable of the act. So it is established: since faith is not from him, it is necessary that disbelief is not from him. Third: Since the servant is not the creator of faith, it is even more so that he is not the creator of disbelief. That is because you do not see in this world any person who is pleased with disbelief, ignorance, and misguidance. Rather, he only wants truth and faith. So when Allah the Exalted stated that faith which occurred by his intention is not from him, but from Allah, and disbelief which occurred contrary to his intention should not be from him, but from Allah: it is more appropriate. If they say: Why is it not permissible that the meaning of 'faith is from Allah' is that Allah empowered him upon it and guided him to it? We say: On this assumption, what is from Allah is the empowerment and enabling. As for the specification of faith, it is not from Allah, and that contradicts the verse. Also: all the conditions are shared with respect to faith and disbelief: power, intellect, and evidence. Then the servant by his own choice brings about one of the two opposites without the other. So it must be by Allah's help and by the preponderance of His incentive, as we have verified this speech in the proof of the incentive. And that is what is sought. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth proof (al-hujjah al-khamisah 'ashar) that both faith and disbelief are from Allah. It begins by establishing that faith (iman) is a good deed (hasanah), defined as an act free from all aspects of ugliness. Two Quranic verses are cited to support this: 'Who is better in speech than one who invites to Allah' (41:33) interpreted as the testimony of faith, and 'Indeed, Allah commands justice and excellence' (16:90) interpreted as 'there is no god but Allah'. Then the second premise: every good deed is from Allah, based on 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah' (4:79) which uses an indefinite noun in a conditional context, implying generality. So faith is from Allah. The author then wants to prove that disbelief (kufr) is also from Allah, and gives three arguments (wujuh): (1) No one makes a distinction between the two; (2) If the servant could create disbelief, the same power would have to be capable of creating faith, otherwise the power would be limited in a way that undermines the concept of capability—this is explained in detail; (3) Since the servant is not the creator of faith, it is even more unlikely that he creates disbelief, as no one desires disbelief. After these three aspects, an objection (fain qalu) is raised: perhaps 'faith from Allah' means Allah empowers and guides. The author answers: that would only mean empowerment, not the specification of faith itself, and it contradicts the verse. Moreover, all conditions for both faith and disbelief are shared, so the actual determination of one over the other must be by Allah's help and the preponderance of His incentive, as argued in the proof of the incentive (dafil al-da'i).

Key Terms
الإيمان
الحسنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, what is a good deed?
A) Any action that is praised
B) An action free from all aspects of ugliness
C) An action that brings reward
D) An action that is obligatory

Key Takeaway
Faith is a good deed because it is free from ugliness, and every good deed is from Allah, so faith is from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If faith is a good deed that comes from Allah, how often do you truly thank Him for the gift of your faith? And when you feel your faith weaken, do you turn to Him as the source of all good deeds, or do you rely on your own efforts?
?
Al-Razi says faith is free from all ugliness. What ugly elements in your heart—such as hypocrisy, doubt, or pride—are you tolerating in your faith? How can you use this lesson to beg Allah to purify them?
?
If every good deed, even the smallest act of worship, is from Allah, why do you sometimes take pride in your own obedience? How can you train yourself to attribute every good action to Allah's grace and not to your own merit?

Three Arguments that Disbelief is from Allah
Al-Razi offers three proofs that disbelief is from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments Imam al-Razi provides to prove that disbelief is also from Allah.

Core Idea
If faith is from Allah, then disbelief must also be from Allah to avoid logical contradictions.

Having established that faith is from Allah, Imam al-Razi now turns to disbelief. He presents three compelling arguments to show that disbelief cannot be from the servant alone. These arguments range from the impossibility of distinguishing between the two, to the nature of power, and the observation that no one desires disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first argument
Read the first argument: no one makes a distinction between faith and disbelief in terms of origin.
This is straightforward.
2
Analyze the second argument
Carefully follow the logical steps: if the servant could create disbelief, that power must also be able to create faith. Otherwise, contradictions arise.
This is the most intricate argument.
3
Grasp the third argument
Observe that no one desires disbelief; people always want faith. So disbelief occurs against their wish, thus from Allah.
This argument uses human psychology.
4
Reflect on the implication
All three arguments lead to the same conclusion: disbelief is from Allah.
This completes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشر : الإيمان حسنة ، وكل حسنة فمن الله ، ينتج : أن الإيمان من الله . بيان المقدمة الأولى : إن الحسنة هي الفعل الخالي عن جميع جهات القبح ، والإيمان كذلك . فكان حسنة . ولأنهم اتفقوا على أن قوله : ومن أحسن قولاً : من دعا إلى الله ) (۱) المراد به : كلمة الشهادة وقيل في قوله : ( إن الله يأمر بالعدل والإحسان ) (۲) : إن ذلك هو قول : لا إله إلا الله . فثبت : أن الإيمان حسنة . وإنما قلنا : إن كل حسنة فمن الله . لقوله تعالى : ( ما أصابك من حسنة فمن الله ) وهو نكرة في موضع الشرط، فيفيد الاستغراق . وإذا ثبت أن الإيمان من الله ، وأردنا أن نبين أيضاً : أن الكفر من الله تعالى . فلـنـا فـيـه وجـوه : الأول : إنه لا قائل بالفرق . الثاني : العبد لو قدر على إيجاد الكفر . فالقدرة الصالحة لإيجاد الكفر . إما أن تكون صالحة لإيجاد الإيمان، أو لا تكون . فإن كان الأول فحينئذ يعـود ذلك إلى القول بأن إيمان العبد منه . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون القادر على الشيء غير قادر على ضده . وذلك عندهم باطل . وأيضاً : على هذا التقدير تكون القدرة موجبة للمقدور . وذلك عندهم يمنع من كون القادر قادراً على الفعل . فثبت : أنه لما لم يكن الإيمان منه ، وجب أن لا يكون الكفر منه . الثالث : إن العبد لما لم يكن موجداً للإيمان . فبأن لا يكون موجداً للكفر أولى . وذلك لأنك لا ترى في الدنيا إنساناً يرضى بالكفر والجهل والضلال . بل إنما يريد الحق والإيمان. فلما نص تعالى على أن الإيمان الذي وقع على قصده ، ليس منه ، بل من الله . والكفر الذي وقع على خلاف قصده بأن لا يكون منه ، بل من الله : كان أولى . فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من كون الإيمان من الله ، هو أن الله أقدره عليه ، وهداه إليه ؟ قلنا : فعلى هذا التقدير . الذي من الله هو الاقدار والتمكين . فأما تعين الإيمان فليس من الله . وهو على خلاف الآية وأيضاً : فجميع الشرائط مشتركة بالنسبة إلى الإيمان والكفر . وهو القدرة ، والعقل . والدلائل . ثم إن العبد باختيار نفسه، أوجد أحد الضدين دون الآخر . فلا بد وأن يكون ذلك بإعانة الله ، وبترجيح داعيته على ما حققنا هذا الكلام في دليل الداعي . وهو المطلوب . والله أعلم
The fifteenth proof: Faith is a good deed, and every good deed is from Allah, resulting in: faith is from Allah. Explanation of the first premise: The good deed is the action free from all aspects of ugliness, and faith is likewise. So it is a good deed. And because they agreed that His saying 'And who is better in speech than one who invites to Allah' (41:33) is intended as the word of testimony, and it was said in His saying 'Indeed, Allah commands justice and excellence' (16:90) that it is the saying 'There is no god but Allah'. So it is established that faith is a good deed. And we say: every good deed is from Allah due to His saying 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah' (4:79), and it is an indefinite noun in the condition position, so it indicates generality. And when it is established that faith is from Allah, and we also want to show that disbelief is from Allah the Exalted, we have three aspects: First: There is no one who makes a distinction. Second: If the servant had power to create disbelief, the power that is valid for creating disbelief: either it is valid for creating faith or not. If the first, then that returns to the saying that the servant's faith is from him. If the second, then the one who is able to do something is not able to do its opposite, and that is invalid according to them. Also: on this assumption, the power necessitates the thing willed, and that according to them prevents the capable one from being capable of the act. So it is established: since faith is not from him, it is necessary that disbelief is not from him. Third: Since the servant is not the creator of faith, it is even more so that he is not the creator of disbelief. That is because you do not see in this world any person who is pleased with disbelief, ignorance, and misguidance. Rather, he only wants truth and faith. So when Allah the Exalted stated that faith which occurred by his intention is not from him, but from Allah, and disbelief which occurred contrary to his intention should not be from him, but from Allah: it is more appropriate. If they say: Why is it not permissible that the meaning of 'faith is from Allah' is that Allah empowered him upon it and guided him to it? We say: On this assumption, what is from Allah is the empowerment and enabling. As for the specification of faith, it is not from Allah, and that contradicts the verse. Also: all the conditions are shared with respect to faith and disbelief: power, intellect, and evidence. Then the servant by his own choice brings about one of the two opposites without the other. So it must be by Allah's help and by the preponderance of His incentive, as we have verified this speech in the proof of the incentive. And that is what is sought. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth proof (al-hujjah al-khamisah 'ashar) that both faith and disbelief are from Allah. It begins by establishing that faith (iman) is a good deed (hasanah), defined as an act free from all aspects of ugliness. Two Quranic verses are cited to support this: 'Who is better in speech than one who invites to Allah' (41:33) interpreted as the testimony of faith, and 'Indeed, Allah commands justice and excellence' (16:90) interpreted as 'there is no god but Allah'. Then the second premise: every good deed is from Allah, based on 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah' (4:79) which uses an indefinite noun in a conditional context, implying generality. So faith is from Allah. The author then wants to prove that disbelief (kufr) is also from Allah, and gives three arguments (wujuh): (1) No one makes a distinction between the two; (2) If the servant could create disbelief, the same power would have to be capable of creating faith, otherwise the power would be limited in a way that undermines the concept of capability—this is explained in detail; (3) Since the servant is not the creator of faith, it is even more unlikely that he creates disbelief, as no one desires disbelief. After these three aspects, an objection (fain qalu) is raised: perhaps 'faith from Allah' means Allah empowers and guides. The author answers: that would only mean empowerment, not the specification of faith itself, and it contradicts the verse. Moreover, all conditions for both faith and disbelief are shared, so the actual determination of one over the other must be by Allah's help and the preponderance of His incentive, as argued in the proof of the incentive (dafil al-da'i).

Key Terms
الكفر
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since humans choose disbelief, it must be from them, not from Allah.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if faith is from Allah, then disbelief must also be from Allah; otherwise, logical contradictions arise. Human desire alone does not determine the source of an action.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, why is it 'more appropriate' that disbelief is from Allah?
A) Because the servant is not the creator of faith
B) Because no one desires disbelief
C) Both A and B
D) Because Allah commands disbelief

Key Takeaway
Through three logical arguments, al-Razi demonstrates that disbelief, like faith, must be from Allah to avoid contradictions in divine power and human will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a moment of doubt or spiritual distance, do you attribute it to your own weakness or to Allah's decree? How does al-Razi's argument challenge you to respond with humility and supplication rather than despair?
?
If disbelief is from Allah, then your own faith is entirely a gift from Him. How does this realization change the way you approach your next prayer or act of worship? Will you rely more on His grace or your own effort?
?
Consider someone you know who has left the faith or is struggling with disbelief. According to this lesson, their disbelief is from Allah. How does this affect your attitude towards them? Will it lead you to arrogance or to greater compassion and prayer on their behalf?

Objection and Answer: The Meaning of 'Faith is from Allah'
Al-Razi refutes an objection about the meaning of 'faith from Allah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that 'faith is from Allah' could mean Allah empowers and guides, and al-Razi's response that this is insufficient.

Core Idea
If 'faith from Allah' merely means empowerment, then the specific choice of faith would still be from the servant, contradicting the verse.

After presenting the three arguments for disbelief being from Allah, Imam al-Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps 'faith is from Allah' means only that Allah gave the servant power and guidance. He refutes this by showing that empowerment alone does not explain the actual occurrence of faith, and that all conditions are shared, so the determination must be from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objectors propose that 'faith from Allah' means only empowerment and guidance.
This is a common reinterpretation.
2
Follow the rebuttal
Al-Razi points out that this reduces Allah's role to merely giving ability, not the actual occurrence of faith.
He says the specification of faith is not from Allah on this view.
3
Grasp the final point
Since all conditions are shared, the servant's choice of one opposite must be by Allah's help and the preponderance of His incentive.
This refers to the proof of the incentive (da'i).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Sixteenth Proof: Gratitude for Faith Implies It Is from Allah
Argues that praiseworthiness of faith proves Allah created it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that Muslims praise Allah for faith proves that faith must be created by Allah, and how textual and rational evidence support this.

Core Idea
Praise is only for what one does; praising Allah for faith shows He created it.

This lesson presents a logical argument grounded in both revelation and reason: the universal practice of thanking Allah for the blessing of faith only makes sense if faith itself is Allah's creation. The author cites a Qur'anic verse to show that praising someone for what they did not do is blameworthy, and then uses the linguistic definition of praise to reinforce the point. An objection is raised, but the passage cuts off before the full response is given.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's core claim
Identify the main argument: if faith were not created by Allah, it would not be praiseworthy. But it is praiseworthy, so it must be created by Allah.
2
Examine the textual evidence
Look at the Qur'anic verse (3:188) that condemns loving to be praised for what one did not do. See how this supports the premise that praise requires authorship.
3
Understand the rational evidence
Consider the linguistic definition of praise as commendation for a benefaction that actually originated from the praised one. If the action is not his, praise is impossible.
4
Analyze the objection
Read the objection: perhaps praising Allah for faith means thanking Him for guidance and empowerment, not for creating faith itself. Also note Thumamah's view that we do not praise Allah for faith at all.
The passage ends here; the answer is missing.
5
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Assess how the combined textual and rational evidence builds a strong case, and consider how the objection might be answered based on other proofs from the same work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشر : لو لم يكن الإيمان بخلق الله ، لما حسن من العبد أن يحمد الله على الإيمان . وقد حسن ذلك ، فوجب أن يكون الإيمان بخلق الله تعالى . بيان الملازمة : بالنص والمعقول . أما النص : فهو أنه تعالى حكم بأن كل من أحب أن يحمد على ما لم يفعل، كان مذموماً . قال تعالى : ( ويحبون أن يحمدوا بما لم يفعلوا (۱) ) وأما المعقول . فهو أن الحمد في اللغة عبارة عن مدح الفاعل على الإحسان الذي صدر منه . فإذا لم يكن الفعل فعلاً له ، امتنع مدحه عليه . وإنما قلنا : إنه يحسن من العبد أن يحمد الله على الإيمان : لإطباق الأمة على قولهم : الحمد لله على نعمة الإيمان . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنا بحمد الله على الإيمان ، بمعنى : أنه تعالى أعاننا على الإيمان ، وأقدرنا عليه ، وأرشدنا إلى كيفية تحصيله ؟ وأيضاً : فمذهب ( ثمامة بن الأشرس ، إنا لا نحمد الله على الإيمان ، بل الله تعالى
The sixteenth proof: If faith were not by Allah's creation, it would not be good for the servant to praise Allah for faith. And it is good, so it is necessary that faith is by Allah's creation. Explanation of the connection: by text and reason. As for the text: He, exalted, ruled that whoever loves to be praised for what he did not do is blameworthy. He said: 'They love to be praised for what they did not do' (3:188). As for reason: praise in language is the commendation of the doer for the benefaction that originated from him. So if the action is not his action, it is impossible to commend him for it. And we say: it is good for the servant to praise Allah for faith due to the consensus of the community upon their saying: 'Praise be to Allah for the blessing of faith.' If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that we praise Allah for faith in the sense that He aided us upon faith, empowered us, and guided us to the way to acquire it? Also: the school of Thumamah ibn al-Ashras: we do not praise Allah for faith, but rather Allah the Exalted...
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the sixteenth proof (al-hujjah al-sadisah 'ashar) that faith must be created by Allah. The argument is: if faith were not created by Allah, then it would not be proper for the servant to praise (hamd) Allah for faith. But it is proper, as evidenced by the community's consensus on saying 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman'. Therefore, faith must be Allah's creation. The author explains the connection (mulazamah) between the premise and the conclusion using both textual and rational evidence. The textual evidence is Quran 3:188, which condemns those who love to be praised for what they did not do. The rational evidence is that praise linguistically is commendation of the doer for his own benefaction; if the action is not his, praise is impossible. An objection is then raised: perhaps praising Allah for faith means thanking Him for empowerment and guidance, not for creating faith itself. The objection also mentions Thumamah ibn al-Ashras' view that we do not praise Allah for faith. The text cuts off before the answer is complete.

Key Terms
الحمد
الإيمان
خلق الله
النص
المعقول
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praising Allah for faith does not prove Allah created faith; we are just thanking Him for helping us to believe.
Correction: The proof shows that praise is only proper for an act one did oneself. If faith were entirely our own doing, praising Allah for it would be like praising someone for what they did not do, which is condemned in the Quran.

Checkpoint
What is the textual evidence used in the sixteenth proof?
A) 'Whoever obeys the Messenger has obeyed Allah.'
B) 'They love to be praised for what they did not do.'
C) 'Whatever good befalls you is from Allah.'
D) 'And who is better in speech than one who invites to Allah?'

Key Takeaway
The praiseworthiness of faith, confirmed by consensus and supported by scripture and reason, demonstrates that faith must be Allah's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman', do you truly believe that your very faith is a direct creation of Allah? Search your heart: have you ever secretly considered your faith something you achieved on your own? How does this affect your humility before Allah?
?
The argument uses the linguistic definition of hamd to prove that Allah must be the doer of faith. Does your praise of Allah ever become a routine phrase without reflecting on what it implies about your own agency? What would change in your daily life if you genuinely internalized that every moment of belief is a direct gift from Allah?
?
You are faced with a choice to commit a sin: does your faith feel like a possession you can hold onto or a constant creation by Allah that could be withdrawn? In that moment, will you rely on your own strength or beg Allah to sustain the iman He creates? Write one specific situation this week where you will remind yourself that iman is from Allah.

Third Proof: The Argument from Priority (اولى)
A proof that faith and disbelief are both created by God using priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that since the servant cannot create faith (which he desires), it is even more fitting that he does not create disbelief (which he hates), and how this proves both are from God.

Core Idea
If you cannot produce what you love, you are even less able to produce what you hate—so both are from God.

This lesson presents the third proof from Imam al-Sanusi that both faith and disbelief are created by God. The argument uses a principle of priority: if a person cannot bring about something they want, they are even less likely to bring about something they despise. The passage then addresses a possible objection and reinforces the conclusion that all actions depend on God's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion, and then the objection and response.
Pay attention to the word 'أولى' (more fitting) which is the key principle.
2
Understand the principle of اولى
The argument hinges on the idea that if someone cannot do something they desire and strive for (create faith), then it is even more certain that they cannot do something they detest (create disbelief). This is a logical priority argument.
The author reinforces this by observing that no one is pleased with disbelief.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection suggests that 'faith from God' means only empowerment and guidance. The author refutes this by showing that the specific determination of faith would then not be from God, contradicting the verse, and that all conditions are equal, so the servant's choice requires God's assistance and preponderance of motive.
This connects to the earlier proof from motive (دليل الداعي).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن العبد لما لم يكن موجداً للإيمان . فبأن لا يكون موجداً للكفر أولى . وذلك لأنك لا ترى في الدنيا إنساناً يرضى بالكفر والجهل والضلال . بل إنما يريد الحق والإيمان. فلما نص تعالى على أن الإيمان الذي وقع على قصده ، ليس منه ، بل من الله . والكفر الذي وقع على خلاف قصده بأن لا يكون منه ، بل من الله : كان أولى
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من كون الإيمان من الله ، هو أن الله أقدره عليه ، وهداه إليه ؟ قلنا : فعلى هذا التقدير . الذي من الله هو الاقدار والتمكين . فأما تعين الإيمان فليس من الله . وهو على خلاف الآية وأيضاً : فجميع الشرائط مشتركة بالنسبة إلى الإيمان والكفر . وهو القدرة ، والعقل . والدلائل . ثم إن العبد باختيار نفسه، أوجد أحد الضدين دون الآخر . فلا بد وأن يكون ذلك بإعانة الله ، وبترجيح داعيته على ما حققنا هذا الكلام في دليل الداعي . وهو المطلوب . والله أعلم
Third: Since the servant is not the creator of faith, then it is more fitting that he is not the creator of disbelief. That is because you do not see in the world any person who is pleased with disbelief, ignorance, and misguidance. Rather, he only desires truth and faith. So when the Most High stated that faith which occurs according to his intention is not from him, but from God, and that disbelief which occurs contrary to his intention is not from him, but from God, it is more fitting. If they say: Why may it not be that the meaning of faith being from God is that God empowered him for it and guided him to it? We say: On this assumption, what is from God is empowerment and enabling. As for the specific determination of faith, it is not from God, and this contradicts the verse. Also, all conditions (capacity, intellect, proofs) are common to both faith and disbelief. Then the servant, by his own choice, brings about one of the two opposites without the other. That must be with God's assistance and the preponderance of his motive, as we have explained this discussion in the proof from motive. That is the desired conclusion. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof in a series of arguments that both faith and disbelief are created by God. It is a self-contained argument followed by an objection-and-answer. The author begins by stating the core premise: since the servant does not create faith (which he desires and seeks), it is even more fitting (اولى) that he does not create disbelief (which he loathes). This uses the logical principle that if someone cannot produce something they prefer, they are even less able to produce something they detest. Then he cites that God's texts indicate both that the faith which aligns with the servant's intention is not from him but from God, and that disbelief which opposes his intention is also not from him but from God. The objection then raises: perhaps 'faith from God' means that God gave the servant power and guidance. The author responds by distinguishing between empowerment (اقدار/تمكين) and the actual determination of the act (تعين الإيمان). The latter is not from God on this interpretation, which contradicts the verse. Furthermore, all conditions (power, intellect, proofs) are shared equally for both faith and disbelief. The servant, by his own choice, selects one of the two opposites, but this choice necessarily requires God's assistance and the preponderance of his inner motive (ترجيح داعيته), as detailed in the proof from motive (دليل الداعي). The conclusion is that both faith and disbelief are ultimately from God.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الكفر
الخلق
الاقدار
التمكين
الداعي
الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The servant creates his own actions independently of God.
Correction: This argument shows that since the servant cannot even create what he desires (faith), he is even less able to create what he hates (disbelief), so all actions are created by God.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the third proof that faith and disbelief are both created by God?
A) The servant does not create faith because he hates it.
B) Since the servant does not create faith (which he desires), it is more fitting that he does not create disbelief (which he hates).
C) God creates faith directly but disbelief is from the servant.
D) The servant creates faith but not disbelief.

Key Takeaway
The argument from priority shows that both faith and disbelief are created by Allah because the servant cannot create even what he desires, so how can he create what he hates?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you cannot produce faith in your heart by your own power, do you truly rely on Allah for your faith, or do you assume you control your own salvation? What changes if you honestly acknowledge that even your desire for good is a gift from Him?
?
When you feel pleasure in obedience or aversion to sin, remember that even that inclination is from Allah. How does this realization affect your gratitude for good deeds and your humility about avoiding evil? Will you thank Him tonight for the very desire to pray?
?
The argument shows that both good and bad deeds are created by Allah. Does this make you feel helpless or humble? How should this truth shape your repentance—does it make you more hopeful (since Allah can change your heart) or more fearful? Commit now to one action that reflects your dependence on Allah for your faith.
