The Sixteenth Proof: Faith is Created Because We Praise God for It
Razi presents the sixteenth proof that faith is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument that if faith were not created by God, praising Him for it would be improper, and the evidence for this from text, reason, and consensus.

Core Idea
Praising God for faith proves He created it, because praise is only for what one does.

Razi presents a short but powerful proof: Muslims unanimously praise God for the blessing of faith. But praise is only appropriate if the praised agent actually performed the act. Therefore, faith must be God's creation. In this lesson, we examine the logical structure of this argument and the two lines of evidence supporting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the opening conditional: if faith were not created by God, praising Him for it would not be commendable. Since it is commendable, faith must be created.
This is the heart of the proof.
2
Examine the textual evidence
The author cites Quran 3:188, which condemns those who love to be praised for what they did not do. This links praise to actual agency.
3
Examine the rational evidence
The definition of praise (al-hamd) requires that the benefit actually come from the one praised. If faith is not from God, praise is impossible.
4
Consider the consensus
The entire community says 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman.' This universal practice backs the proof.
The author will later defend this consensus against objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشر : لو لم يكن الإيمان بخلق الله ، لما حسن من العبد أن يحمد الله على الإيمان . وقد حسن ذلك ، فوجب أن يكون الإيمان بخلق الله تعالى . بيان الملازمة : بالنص والمعقول . أما النص : فهو أنه تعالى حكم بأن كل من أحب أن يحمد على ما لم يفعل، كان مذموماً . قال تعالى : ( ويحبون أن يحمدوا بما لم يفعلوا ) وأما المعقول . فهو أن الحمد في اللغة عبارة عن مدح الفاعل على الإحسان الذي صدر منه . فإذا لم يكن الفعل فعلاً له ، امتنع مدحه عليه . وإنما قلنا : إنه يحسن من العبد أن يحمد الله على الإيمان : لإطباق الأمة على قولهم : الحمد لله على نعمة الإيمان
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنا بحمد الله على الإيمان ، بمعنى : أنه تعالى أعاننا على الإيمان ، وأقدرنا عليه ، وأرشدنا إلى كيفية تحصيله ؟ وأيضاً : فمذهب ثمامة بن الأشرس ، إنا لا نحمد الله على الإيمان ، بل الله تعالى يحمدنا عليه . كما قال تعالى : ( فأولئك كان سعيهم مشكوراً ) وقال : ( ومن تطوع خيراً . فإن الله شاكر عليم ) وقال : ( وما يفعلوا من خير فلن يكفروه )
والحاصل : إنا نحمد الله على الإقدار على الإيمان . والله تعالى يشكرنا على فعل الإيمان. وعند هذا قال : ثمامة : لا يجوز أن يقال : الحمد لله على نعمة الإيمان ، ومنع هذا الإجماع .
والجواب عن الأول : إن الإيمان غير ، والقدرة على الإيمان غير فهب أنا نشكره على أن أعطانا القدرة على الإيمان، لكن كيف نشكره على الإيمان ، مع أنه تعالى ما أعطانا الإيمان ؟
وأما السؤال الثاني . فهو باطل . لأن إجماع الأمة على قولهم : الحمد لله على نعمة الإيمان: معلوم بالتواتر . فكان منكره منكراً للإجماع . والذي يدل على أن هذا الإجماع معلوم بالتواتر : أن المعتزلة ذكروا أن ثمامة بن الأشرس إنما منع هذا الإجماع عند جعفر بن حرب ، جواباً عن كلام رجل من أهل السنة ، احتج بهذا الدليل . قالوا : فلما سمع جعفر بن حرب هذا الكلام ، قال : لما شنعت هذه المسألة ، سهلت . وهذا يدل على أن جعفر بن حرب كان معترفاً بأن منع هذا الإجماع : شنيع .
وأما الآيات الدالة على كون العبد مشكوراً على الطاعات : فهي لا تنافي كون الله تعالى مشكوراً على تلك الطاعات. لأن الشكر هو التعظيم . وتعظيم العبد الله ، ليس إلا المدح والثناء . وتعظيم الله للعبد ، ليس إلا إيصال الثواب إليه . ولا منافاة بين الأمرين . فثبت : أن كون العبد مشكوراً على الطاعات ، لا ينافي كون الله مشكوراً عليها . والله أعلم
The sixteenth proof: If faith were not created by God, then it would not be commendable for the servant to praise God for faith. But it is commendable, so it is necessary that faith is created by God Almighty. Explanation of the connection: by scriptural text and reason. As for the scriptural text: He Almighty ruled that whoever loves to be praised for what he has not done is blameworthy. He said: (They love to be praised for what they have not done). As for reason: praise (al-hamd) in language is the commendation of an agent for the beneficence that proceeds from him. If the action is not his action, it is impossible to praise him for it. We only say that it is commendable for the servant to praise God for faith because of the consensus of the community saying: 'Praise be to God for the blessing of faith.' If it is said: Why may it not be said that we praise God for faith, meaning that He Almighty assisted us in faith, empowered us for it, and guided us to the method of acquiring it? And also: the school of Thumama ibn Ashras is that we do not praise God for faith; rather, God Almighty praises us for it, as He said: (Those – their effort is appreciated) and He said: (And whoever volunteers good – then indeed, God is Appreciative and Knowing) and He said: (And whatever good they do, they will not be denied it). The conclusion: we praise God for empowering us for faith, and God Almighty thanks us for performing faith. At this point, Thumama said: It is not permissible to say 'Praise be to God for the blessing of faith,' and he denied this consensus. The answer to the first: Faith is other than, and the ability to have faith is other than; supposing we thank Him for giving us the ability to have faith, but how can we thank Him for faith, when He Almighty did not give us faith? As for the second question, it is false, because the consensus of the community on saying 'Praise be to God for the blessing of faith' is known by tawatur (mass transmission). So its denier is a denier of consensus. What indicates that this consensus is known by tawatur is that the Mu'tazila mentioned that Thumama ibn Ashras only denied this consensus in the presence of Ja'far ibn Harb, in response to the speech of a man from Ahl al-Sunna who argued with this proof. They said: When Ja'far ibn Harb heard this speech, he said: 'When this issue became objectionable, it became easy.' This indicates that Ja'far ibn Harb acknowledged that denial of this consensus is objectionable. As for the verses indicating that the servant is thanked for acts of obedience, they do not contradict that God Almighty is thanked for those acts of obedience, because thanks (shukr) is magnification. The magnification of God by the servant is nothing but praise and commendation. The magnification of the servant by God is nothing but bestowing reward upon him. There is no contradiction between the two matters. So it is established that the servant being thanked for acts of obedience does not negate God being thanked for them. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the sixteenth proof that faith is created by God. It is structured as a full argument with an objection-and-answer. The author begins by stating the conditional: if faith were not created by God, it would not be commendable (لا يحسن) for the servant to praise (يحمد) God for it. Since it is commendable (as evidenced by the community's consensus on saying 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman'), it must be that faith is created by God. The author then explains the logical connection (بيان الملازمة) using both scriptural and rational evidence. Scripturally, God condemns those who love to be praised for what they did not do (Quran 3:188). Rationally, praise (الحمد) linguistically means praising an agent for a benefit that actually came from him; if the act is not his, praise is impossible. The objection offers two alternative interpretations: first, that praising God for faith means thanking Him for assistance, empowerment, and guidance to acquire it; second, the position of Thumama ibn Ashras that we do not thank God for faith at all, but rather God thanks us. The author answers the first by distinguishing between faith and the capacity for faith: thanking God for the capacity does not equate to thanking Him for the act of faith itself. The second objection is refuted by citing the consensus (إجماع) of the Muslim community, known by tawatur (mass transmission), that saying 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman' is legitimate. The author further strengthens this by noting that even prominent Mu'tazila figures like Ja'far ibn Harb admitted that denying this consensus is objectionable. Finally, the author addresses the verses that speak of God thanking the servant for good deeds (e.g., being appreciative of effort). These do not conflict, because 'thanks' (shukr) means magnification: the servant's magnification of God is praise, while God's magnification of the servant is rewarding. Both can coexist without contradiction.

Key Terms
الحمد
الإيمان
الخلق
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main scriptural evidence used in this proof?
A) 'They love to be praised for what they have not done' (Quran 3:188)
B) 'And whoever volunteers good – then indeed, God is Appreciative'
C) 'So their effort is appreciated'
D) 'And whatever good they do, they will not be denied it'

Key Takeaway
The community's unanimous praise of God for the blessing of faith implies that faith is directly created by God, because praise is only proper for what one actually does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-ḥamdu lillāh ʿalā niʿmat al-īmān', do you truly believe that your very faith is a direct creation of God, not a product of your own effort? How does this realization affect your humility before Him?
?
Is there any part of your faith (īmān) that you secretly attribute to your own intelligence, good deeds, or spiritual efforts rather than to God's creation? Will you repent of that hidden pride and acknowledge His sole agency?
?
Imagine standing before God and praising Him for your faith — if He did not create it, that praise would be like loving to be praised for what you did not do. Does this thought terrify you enough to examine the sincerity of your gratitude?

Answering Objections to the Sixteenth Proof
Razi addresses objections to the sixteenth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections to the sixteenth proof and how Razi refutes them by distinguishing faith from its capacity, defending consensus, and clarifying the meaning of shukr.

Core Idea
Objections are refuted by distinguishing faith from its capacity and by upholding the consensus of the community.

The sixteenth proof rests on the commendability of praising God for faith. But opponents raised two objections: first, that praising God for faith might only mean thanking Him for the ability to acquire it; second, that Thumama ibn Ashras denied that we praise God for faith at all. In this lesson, we see how Razi systematically dismantles these objections, using precise distinctions and the authority of consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
The objection suggests that praising God for faith means thanking Him for assisting, empowering, and guiding to faith—not for creating the faith itself.
This objection shifts the basis of praise.
2
Study Razi's first answer
Razi distinguishes between faith (the act) and capacity for faith. Thanking for capacity is not the same as thanking for the act itself.
He argues: even if we thank God for empowerment, we still need to account for thanking Him for the faith itself.
3
Read the second objection
Thumama ibn Ashras claimed that we do not praise God for faith; rather, God praises us. He cited verses about God being appreciative.
4
Study Razi's second answer
Razi defends consensus by its widespread transmission (tawatur) and notes that even opponents found Thumama's denial objectionable. He then explains that shukr (thanks) means magnification, which applies differently to God and servant without contradiction.
The consensus is so strong that denying it is like denying a mass-transmitted report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة عشر : لو لم يكن الإيمان بخلق الله ، لما حسن من العبد أن يحمد الله على الإيمان . وقد حسن ذلك ، فوجب أن يكون الإيمان بخلق الله تعالى . بيان الملازمة : بالنص والمعقول . أما النص : فهو أنه تعالى حكم بأن كل من أحب أن يحمد على ما لم يفعل، كان مذموماً . قال تعالى : ( ويحبون أن يحمدوا بما لم يفعلوا ) وأما المعقول . فهو أن الحمد في اللغة عبارة عن مدح الفاعل على الإحسان الذي صدر منه . فإذا لم يكن الفعل فعلاً له ، امتنع مدحه عليه . وإنما قلنا : إنه يحسن من العبد أن يحمد الله على الإيمان : لإطباق الأمة على قولهم : الحمد لله على نعمة الإيمان
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنا بحمد الله على الإيمان ، بمعنى : أنه تعالى أعاننا على الإيمان ، وأقدرنا عليه ، وأرشدنا إلى كيفية تحصيله ؟ وأيضاً : فمذهب ثمامة بن الأشرس ، إنا لا نحمد الله على الإيمان ، بل الله تعالى يحمدنا عليه . كما قال تعالى : ( فأولئك كان سعيهم مشكوراً ) وقال : ( ومن تطوع خيراً . فإن الله شاكر عليم ) وقال : ( وما يفعلوا من خير فلن يكفروه )

Since it is commendable (as evidenced by the community's consensus on saying 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman'), it must be that faith is created by God. The author then explains the logical connection (بيان الملازمة) using both scriptural and rational evidence. Scripturally, God condemns those who love to be praised for what they did not do (Quran 3:188). Rationally, praise (الحمد) linguistically means praising an agent for a benefit that actually came from him; if the act is not his, praise is impossible. The objection offers two alternative interpretations: first, that praising God for faith means thanking Him for assistance, empowerment, and guidance to acquire it; second, the position of Thumama ibn Ashras that we do not thank God for faith at all, but rather God thanks us. The author answers the first by distinguishing between faith and the capacity for faith: thanking God for the capacity does not equate to thanking Him for the act of faith itself. The second objection is refuted by citing the consensus (إجماع) of the Muslim community, known by tawatur (mass transmission), that saying 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman' is legitimate. The author further strengthens this by noting that even prominent Mu'tazila figures like Ja'far ibn Harb admitted that denying this consensus is objectionable. Finally, the author addresses the verses that speak of God thanking the servant for good deeds (e.g., being appreciative of effort). These do not conflict, because 'thanks' (shukr) means magnification: the servant's magnification of God is praise, while God's magnification of the servant is rewarding. Both can coexist without contradiction.

Key Terms
الشكر
الحمد
الإيمان
القدرة
الإجماع
التواتر
التعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is sufficient to thank God for the ability to have faith, and that explains why we say 'al-hamdu lillah 'ala ni'mat al-iman'.
Correction: Thanking for the ability is not the same as thanking for the act itself. The phrase thanks God for the blessing of faith, not just the capacity. If faith were not created by God, this praise would be inappropriate.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute Thumama's claim that we do not praise God for faith?
A) By citing verses that command praise
B) By arguing that the consensus of the community is mass-transmitted and cannot be denied
C) By saying that Thumama was not a Muslim
D) By redefining faith

Key Takeaway
The objections against the proof fail because faith and its capacity are distinct, the consensus is unassailably transmitted, and divine and human 'thanks' are not contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Alhamdulillah for my iman', do you truly believe that your faith is a direct gift from Allah, or do you tend to attribute your faith to your own efforts? How does this lesson's distinction between capability and actual faith challenge your daily gratitude?
?
Thumama denied that we should praise Allah for faith itself, claiming rather that Allah praises us. Have you ever felt proud of your own obedience as if it were your own achievement? How does Razi's refutation correct that pride?
?
The consensus on praising Allah for faith is so strong that even opponents found its denial objectionable. In your heart, do you share this consensus, or do you sometimes feel that your faith is a result of your own choices? What would change if you truly internalized that every moment of belief is a gift?

The Impermissibility of Using Ahad Reports for Certain Matters
Al-Razi formulates an objection against using ahad reports for certain issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that ahad reports, being speculative, cannot be used as proof for certain doctrinal issues like qada and qadr.

Core Idea
Speculative proof cannot establish certain truth.

Al-Razi has established that ahad reports provide only speculation. Now he applies this conclusion to the very topic of his book: the issue of predestination. He asserts that predestination is a certain and decisive matter, so relying on speculative reports is methodologically unsound. This brief passage formulates the objection that will be answered in subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the key terms. Pay attention to the phrase 'قطعية يقينية'.
This phrase emphasizes the certainty of the matter.
2
Identify the two premises
Al-Razi states two premises: that qada/qadr is certain, and that ahad reports yield only speculation. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that using speculative proof for a certain matter is impermissible. Reflect on why this is a valid logical inference.
The reasoning is deductive: if the matter is certain, its proof must be certain.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This passage sets up an objection. Think about what kind of answer might follow in the text.
The phrase 'هذا تقرير هذا السؤال' indicates the objection is complete.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is the formulation of an objection (taqrir al-su'al) based on the preceding conclusion about ahad reports. The author first affirms the conclusion: ahad reports only give speculation (zann). Then he applies it to the topic of this book: the issue of predestination (qada wa qadr). He asserts that this issue is certain and decisive (qat'iyya yaqiniyya). Since ahad reports are only speculative (zanni), using them as proof for a certain matter is impermissible. This sets up the objection against those who use ahad reports to argue about predestination. The phrase 'this is the formulation of this objection' indicates the end of the objection and prepares for the answer that follows (not included here).

Answering the Objection of Cumulative Ahad Hadith
The author states an objection about cumulative ahad hadith and gives two answers regarding qada wa qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's two answers to the objection that cumulative ahad hadith can produce certainty, and why speculative reports can still be useful in supporting rational proofs.

Core Idea
Cumulative ahad hadith seem to produce certainty, but the same logic would force acceptance of false doctrines.

The author presents an objection to using ahad hadith for the certain issue of qada wa qadar. Critics argue that even if each report is speculative, their cumulative mass might reach certainty. The author gives two subtle answers: one showing the objection is self-defeating, and another explaining the supporting role of speculative reports. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage's restatement of the issue: qada wa qadar is certain, ahad reports are only speculative, so they cannot be used. Recognize the implicit objection: perhaps the cumulative total of many such reports could reach certainty.
2
Identify the first answer
Find the author's first response: he grants that cumulative reports might reach certainty, but notes this would allow anthropomorphists to claim certainty for their reports, which is unacceptable. So the objection is self-defeating.
This is a refutation by parity of reasoning.
3
Understand the second answer
See how the author concedes that the reports yield only speculation, yet he is content. The real benefit is when rational proofs already establish the truth, the reports strengthen certainty and remove doubts.
Speculative reports have a supporting, not foundational, role.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this passage balances rational and revealed evidence. Certainty comes from rational proofs, while scriptural evidence reinforces and dispels doubts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا فلنرجع إلى مطلوبنا من هذا الكتاب . فنقول : مسألة القضاء والقدر ، مسألة قطعية يقينية ، وخبر الواحد لا يفيد إلا الظن والتمسك بالحجة الظنية في المسألة القطعية لا يجوز . هذا تقرير هذا السؤال والجواب من وجهين : الأول : هب أن كل واحد من هذه الأخبار غير معلوم الصحة ، إلا أن مجموعها ، ربما بلغ مبلغ القطع واليقين. وبهذا الطريق تمسكنا بالمعجزات المروية بالأحاد. إلا أن هذا الطريق ينتقض بأخبار التشبيه . للمشبهة أن يقولوا : إن مجموعها بلغت مبلغ التواتر . فإن منعناهم عن ذلك ، كان الخصومنا في هذه المسألة أن يمنعونا عنه أيضاً الثاني : نسلم أنها لا تفيد إلا الظن . ونكتفي منها بالظن . بل فيه فائدة أخرى . وهي : إن الدلائل العقلية إذا دلت على صحة قولنا ، ثم رأينا أن ظواهر القرآن والأخبار تؤكد تلك العقليات ، قوي اليقين وزالت الشبهات . وبالله التوفيق .
And when this is established, let us return to our purpose in this book. We say: the question of al-qada wa al-qadr (divine decree and predestination) is a certain and decisive issue, and a khabar al-wahid (single-narrator report) yields only speculation (al-dhann), and relying on a speculative proof in a certain issue is not permissible. This is the formulation of this question. And the answer is from two aspects: First: Suppose that each one of these reports is not known to be sound, yet their totality may reach the level of certainty and decisiveness. By this method we have held to the miracles reported through single-narrator reports. However, this method is refuted by the reports of tashbih (anthropomorphism). For the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) may say that their totality has reached the level of tawatur (mass transmission). If we prevent them from that, then our opponents in this issue would also prevent us from it. Second: We concede that they yield only speculation, and we are content with speculation. Rather, there is another benefit in it, namely: when rational proofs indicate the correctness of our position, and then we see that the apparent meanings of the Quran and the reports confirm those rational matters, certainty is strengthened and doubts disappear. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The author first restates the position: the issue of qada wa qadar (divine decree) is a certain (qat'i) matter, but ahad hadith (single-narrator reports) only yield speculation (dhann), so they cannot be used as proof for a certain issue. Then he gives the objection implicitly: perhaps the cumulative mass of such reports could reach certainty. The author answers with two points. First, he grants that in principle the cumulative total of weak reports might reach certainty, and he notes that this is the method used to establish miracles (i.e., many single reports collectively give certainty). However, this same method would backfire here, because the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) could use the same argument to claim that the many hadith supporting anthropomorphism (tashbih) also reach tawatur (mass transmission) and thus prove their doctrine. If we deny them that, they would in turn deny our use of cumulative reports for other matters. So this objection is self-defeating. Second, the author makes a concession: even if we admit that these reports are only speculative (dhann), that is sufficient. The real benefit of such reports is not to establish the doctrine on their own, but to reinforce rational proofs. When rational arguments have already established the truth, and then we see that the apparent meanings of the Quran and sunnah support those rational conclusions, our certainty is strengthened and doubts are removed. Thus, speculative reports have a supporting role, not a foundational one.

Key Terms
أخبار الآحاد
الظن
القطع
القضاء والقدر
التواتر
التشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cumulative ahad hadith always yield certainty and therefore can be used as proof for any issue.
Correction: The author shows that this method would also allow anthropomorphists to claim certainty for their hadith, which is unacceptable, so the method cannot be blindly applied.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument that cumulative ahad hadith about qada wa qadar produce certainty?
A) Because ahad hadith can never produce certainty even in aggregate.
B) Because the same argument would force acceptance of anthropomorphic hadith as certain.
C) Because qada wa qadar is not a certain issue.
D) Because the Quran alone suffices without hadith.

Key Takeaway
Cumulative ahad hadith cannot establish certainty on their own for the issue of qada wa qadar, but they can strengthen rational proofs when used in support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The anthropomorphists used the same method of cumulative hadith to claim certainty for their false beliefs. Are there any areas of your faith where you are using a method of proof that could be equally used by proponents of falsehood? How can you ensure your methodology is not self-defeating?
?
The author says that scriptural evidence strengthens certainty only when rational proofs are already established. Is your certainty in qada wa qadar built on rational evidence or on hadith? If the hadith were weaker, would your faith still stand? What does your answer reveal about the foundation of your belief?

Objections 3-4: Adam's Slip and the Real Cause of Expulsion
The Mu'tazila present their third and fourth objections to the Adam-Moses hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's third and fourth objections to the Adam-Moses hadith concerning the nature of Adam's slip and the true reason for humanity's expulsion from Paradise.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue Adam's slip was minor and expulsion was predestined, not punishment.

The Mu'tazila raise several objections against using the Adam-Moses hadith to prove predestination. The third and fourth objections focus on the nature of Adam's sin and the actual cause of humanity's expulsion. They assert that Adam's slip was either a minor sin or leaving the better, so no blame attaches, and that Moses knew the expulsion was not due to Adam but God's plan. This sets the stage for a deeper theological debate on free will and divine decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Mu'tazila's claim that Adam's slip was either tark al-afdal or saghira, and therefore no blame is deserved. Note the logical implication: if Moses did it, it would indicate ignorance.
Consider the concept of 'tark al-afdal' in Islamic theology.
2
Read the fourth objection
Read the objection that Moses accused Adam of causing misery, but Moses knew the expulsion was God's decree. He cites Quran 2:30.
The Mu'tazila use Scripture to argue that Adam's action was not the cause.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself how these objections challenge the predestination argument. What do they say about human responsibility?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الدليل دل على أن الزلة التي صدرت من آدم ، كانت من باب ترك الأفضل . أو من باب الصغائر. وعلى كلا التقديرين ، فإنه لا يجوز إلحاق الذم به ، بسبب ذلك. فلو فعله موسى ، لدل على جهله . وحاشاه ، عنه الرابع : أنه قال لأدم: «أنت الذي أشقيت الناس ، وأخرجتهم من الجنة ، وقد علم موسى عليه السلام أن شقاء الخلق وإخراجهم من الجنة ، ما كان بسبب آدم ، بل الله خلقهم ليكونوا في أول الأمر في الأرض . قال تعالى : وإذا قال ربك للملائكة : إني جاعل في الأرض خليفة ) (١) الخامس : إن آدم عليه السلام احتج بما ليس بحجة . إذ لو قلنا : إن الله لما كتب عليه ذلك ، كان ذلك حجة لآدم . وقد كتب الله الكفر أيضاً على فرعون ، وهامان ، وسائر الكفار . فوجب أن يكونوا معذورين فيه . ولما بطل ذلك ، علمنا فساد هذه الحجة السادس : إن الخبر اشتمل على أن رسول الله عليه السلام : صوّب آدم في هذا الاستدلال . فلما ثبت أن هذا الدليل خطأ ، لزم أن يكون تصويب النبي عليه السلام ، لآدم عليه السلام . في هذا الاستدلال : خطأ السابع : إن الدلائل العقلية قامت على فساد القول بالجبر ، والقرآن من أوله إلى آخره ناطق بفساد الجبر . وخبر الواحد إذا ورد على خلاف العقل ، وخلاف القرآن : وجب تأويله ، إن قبل التأويل، ورده إن لم يقبل التأويل وهذا الخبر يقبل التأويل من وجوه : • الأول : لعله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان قد حكى هذا الكلام عن اليهود ، إلا أن الراوي حين دخل ، ما سمع تلك الحكاية ، بل سمع هذا القدر ، فظن أنه إنما حكاه عن نفسه ، وعن مذهبه • الثاني : لعله عليه السلام قال : « فحج آدم موسى - بنصب آدم ـ أي و أن موسى غلبه وجعله محجوجاً ، وأن الذي ذكره آدم ليس بحجة ولا بعذر . وقد بينا : أنه لو كان ذلك عذراً ، لوجب أن يكون كل الكفار معذورين . وذكرنا أيضاً : أن وقوع التفاوت في الإعراب ، في أخبار الآحاد : غير مستبعد • الثالث : وهو الوجه الأقوى أنه ليس المراد من هذه المناظرة : الذم على المعصية ، ولا الاعتذار منه يعلم الله . بل موسى سأله عن السبب الحامل له على تلك الزلة ، حتى خرج بسببها من الجنة . فقال آدم صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن خروجي من الجنة ، ما كان بسبب تلك الزلة ، بل كان بسبب أن الله كان قد كتب علي أني أخرج إلى الأرض ، وأكون خليفة فيها . وهذا المعنى كان مكتوبا في التوراة ، فلا جرم كانت حجة آدم قوية، وصار موسى عليه السلام في ذلك كالمغلوب الرابع : إن قوله : « كيف تلومني على أمر قدره الله علي قبل أن يخلقني بأربعين سنة ) ؟ ليس المراد من لفظ التقدير ههنا : الخلق والتكوين . لأن فعل الإنسان، قبل خلق ذاته بأربعين سنة : محال . بل المراد من هذا التقدير : هو أنه تعالى كتب ذلك في اللوح المحفوظ . والتقدير بهذا التفسير مما لا نزاع في إثباته البتة فهذا جملة كلام المعتزلة على استدلال أصحابنا بهذا الخبر : والجواب : أما قوله : « رواية أبي هريرة مضطربة في هذا الخبر قلنا : جميع الروايات مشتملة على التعليل بتقدير الله . ودليلنا : هو هذا القدر المشترك . قوله ثانياً : كيف يشافه الولد والده بالسوء ، لا سيما إذا كان الولد من
Third: Proof indicates that the slip that occurred from Adam was of the type of leaving the better, or of the type of minor sins. On both estimates, it is not permissible to attach blame to him because of that. So if Moses had done it, it would indicate his ignorance; far be it from him. Fourth: He said to Adam: 'You are the one who made people miserable and brought them out of Paradise.' And Moses (peace be upon him) knew that the misery of creation and their expulsion from Paradise was not due to Adam; rather, God created them to be originally on earth. He said, the Exalted: 'And when your Lord said to the angels: Indeed I am placing a vicegerent on earth' (al-Baqara 30). Fifth: Adam (peace be upon him) argued with what is not a proof. For if we said that God wrote that upon him, that would be a proof for Adam. And God also wrote disbelief upon Pharaoh, Haman, and all the disbelievers. So they would have to be excused in it. And when that is invalid, we know the corruption of this argument. Sixth: The report includes that the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) approved Adam in this reasoning. So when it is established that this proof is wrong, then necessarily the Prophet's (peace be upon him) approval of Adam (peace be upon him) in this reasoning would be wrong. Seventh: Rational proofs have established the corruption of the doctrine of jabr (compulsion), and the Quran from its beginning to its end speaks of the corruption of jabr. And if an individual report (khabar al-wahid) comes contrary to reason and contrary to the Quran, its interpretation (ta'wil) is obligatory, if it accepts interpretation, and it is rejected if it does not accept interpretation. And this report accepts interpretation in several ways: First: Perhaps he (peace be upon him) had related this speech from the Jews, but the narrator when he entered did not hear that narration; rather he heard this amount and thought that he had related it from himself and his doctrine. Second: Perhaps he (peace be upon him) said: 'So Adam defeated Moses' with Adam in the accusative – meaning that Moses overcame him and made him refuted, and that what Adam mentioned is not a proof nor an excuse. And we have shown: If that were an excuse, then all disbelievers would have to be excused. And we also mentioned that occurrence of variation in grammar in individual reports is not unlikely. Third: This is the strongest view: the intended meaning of this dialogue is not blame for the sin, nor excuse from it – God knows. Rather, Moses asked him about the cause that led him to that slip, such that he left Paradise because of it. So Adam (peace be upon him) said: My exit from Paradise was not due to that slip; rather it was because God had written upon me that I should go out to earth and be a vicegerent therein. And this meaning was written in the Torah. So inevitably Adam's argument was strong, and Moses (peace be upon him) became like one overcome. Fourth: His statement: 'Why do you blame me for a matter which God decreed upon me forty years before He created me?' The intended meaning of 'decree' (taqdir) here is not creation and bringing into being, because an action of a human before the creation of his own self by forty years is impossible. Rather, the intended meaning of 'decree' is that He, the Exalted, wrote that in the Preserved Tablet. And 'decree' in this interpretation is something about whose establishment there is absolutely no dispute. This is the sum of the speech of the Mu'tazila concerning the inference of our companions by this report. And the answer: As for his saying: 'The narration of Abu Hurayra is disordered in this report,' we say: All the narrations include the reason of God's decree (taqdir). And our proof is this shared content. His saying, secondly: 'How can a son address his father with evil, especially if the son is from...'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's objections to the use of the hadith of Adam and Moses as proof for predestination (jabr), followed by the author's initial rebuttal. The Mu'tazila enumerate seven objections, though only the third through seventh are fully detailed here. 

First, the third objection: Adam's slip was either a case of leaving the better (tark al-afdal) or a minor sin (saghira), so blame cannot attach; if Moses did it, it would show ignorance of this, which is impossible. 

Fourth: Moses knew that humanity's misery and expulsion were not due to Adam's sin but because God willed them to be on earth (citing Quran 2:30). 

Fifth: If predestination were an excuse, then Pharaoh and all disbelievers would be excused, which is absurd. 

Sixth: The Prophet's approval of Adam's argument would be erroneous if Adam's argument were invalid. 

Seventh: Rational proofs and the Quran together refute jabr; an individual report contradicting both must be interpreted or rejected. The hadith can be interpreted in four ways: (1) The Prophet was merely quoting Jews; (2) The Prophet actually said 'Adam defeated Moses' meaning Moses refuted him, i.e., Adam's argument was invalid; (3) The dialogue was about the cause of expulsion, not blame for sin; (4) 'Decree' means writing in the Preserved Tablet, not creation. 

Then the author summarizes: 'This is the sum of the Mu'tazila's speech.' The answer begins by rebutting the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is disordered, asserting that all versions share the same reasoning about God's decree. The response is cut off as it addresses the second objection about a son addressing his father harshly.

Key Terms
زلة
ترك الأفضل
صغائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Adam's sin was the direct cause of humanity's expulsion from Paradise.
Correction: The fourth objection argues that God had already planned to place humans on earth before the sin, so the sin was not the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, why should Adam not be blamed for his slip?
A) Because he repented.
B) Because the slip was either tark al-afdal or a minor sin, which does not warrant blame.
C) Because Moses forgave him.
D) Because the slip was predestined.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that Adam's slip was not blameworthy and that the expulsion was predestined, not a punishment for his action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you blame someone for a mistake, do you first consider whether it was truly blameworthy or merely a minor slip? How does the Mu'tazila's argument challenge your impulse to assign fault? Will you pause before accusing this week?
?
Moses knew the expulsion was God's decree, yet he still addressed Adam. How do you balance trusting God's ultimate plan with holding yourself and others accountable for actions? Are you using God's decree as an excuse not to change something you could?
?
If Adam's 'sin' was only leaving the better, how often do you settle for 'good enough' instead of striving for what is best? Is there an area of your life where you are content with mediocrity when God expects excellence? What will you do about it starting today?

Objections 5-6: Predestination as Excuse and Prophet's Approval
The Mu'tazila present the fifth and sixth objections against the predestination argument from the Adam-Moses hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's fifth and sixth objections: if predestination were an excuse, all disbelievers would be excused, and the Prophet's approval of Adam's reasoning would be erroneous if the reasoning were invalid.

Core Idea
Predestination as universal excuse is absurd; Prophet's approval can't endorse a false argument.

The Mu'tazila continue their assault on the predestination argument. The fifth objection highlights the absurdity of using predestination as an excuse: if it excused Adam, it would also excuse Pharaoh and every disbeliever, which is clearly false. The sixth objection targets the Prophet's reported approval of Adam's argument: if the argument is invalid, then the Prophet's approval would be an error, which is impossible. These objections force defenders to either reject the hadith or reinterpret it.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth objection
Read the objection that if predestination were an excuse, then Pharaoh and all disbelievers would be excused. Note the logical conclusion: the argument is corrupt.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Read the sixth objection
Read the objection that if Adam's argument is invalid, then the Prophet's approval would be a mistake, which is impossible.
This challenges the reliability of the hadith content.
3
Consider the combined weight
How do these two objections together challenge the use of the hadith? What do they imply about the need for reinterpretation?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الدليل دل على أن الزلة التي صدرت من آدم ، كانت من باب ترك الأفضل . أو من باب الصغائر. وعلى كلا التقديرين ، فإنه لا يجوز إلحاق الذم به ، بسبب ذلك. فلو فعله موسى ، لدل على جهله . وحاشاه ، عنه الرابع : أنه قال لأدم: «أنت الذي أشقيت الناس ، وأخرجتهم من الجنة ، وقد علم موسى عليه السلام أن شقاء الخلق وإخراجهم من الجنة ، ما كان بسبب آدم ، بل الله خلقهم ليكونوا في أول الأمر في الأرض . قال تعالى : وإذا قال ربك للملائكة : إني جاعل في الأرض خليفة ) (١) الخامس : إن آدم عليه السلام احتج بما ليس بحجة . إذ لو قلنا : إن الله لما كتب عليه ذلك ، كان ذلك حجة لآدم . وقد كتب الله الكفر أيضاً على فرعون ، وهامان ، وسائر الكفار . فوجب أن يكونوا معذورين فيه . ولما بطل ذلك ، علمنا فساد هذه الحجة السادس : إن الخبر اشتمل على أن رسول الله عليه السلام : صوّب آدم في هذا الاستدلال . فلما ثبت أن هذا الدليل خطأ ، لزم أن يكون تصويب النبي عليه السلام ، لآدم عليه السلام . في هذا الاستدلال : خطأ السابع : إن الدلائل العقلية قامت على فساد القول بالجبر ، والقرآن من أوله إلى آخره ناطق بفساد الجبر . وخبر الواحد إذا ورد على خلاف العقل ، وخلاف القرآن : وجب تأويله ، إن قبل التأويل، ورده إن لم يقبل التأويل وهذا الخبر يقبل التأويل من وجوه : • الأول : لعله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان قد حكى هذا الكلام عن اليهود ، إلا أن الراوي حين دخل ، ما سمع تلك الحكاية ، بل سمع هذا القدر ، فظن أنه إنما حكاه عن نفسه ، وعن مذهبه • الثاني : لعله عليه السلام قال : « فحج آدم موسى - بنصب آدم ـ أي و أن موسى غلبه وجعله محجوجاً ، وأن الذي ذكره آدم ليس بحجة ولا بعذر . وقد بينا : أنه لو كان ذلك عذراً ، لوجب أن يكون كل الكفار معذورين . وذكرنا أيضاً : أن وقوع التفاوت في الإعراب ، في أخبار الآحاد : غير مستبعد • الثالث : وهو الوجه الأقوى أنه ليس المراد من هذه المناظرة : الذم على المعصية ، ولا الاعتذار منه يعلم الله . بل موسى سأله عن السبب الحامل له على تلك الزلة ، حتى خرج بسببها من الجنة . فقال آدم صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن خروجي من الجنة ، ما كان بسبب تلك الزلة ، بل كان بسبب أن الله كان قد كتب علي أني أخرج إلى الأرض ، وأكون خليفة فيها . وهذا المعنى كان مكتوبا في التوراة ، فلا جرم كانت حجة آدم قوية، وصار موسى عليه السلام في ذلك كالمغلوب الرابع : إن قوله : « كيف تلومني على أمر قدره الله علي قبل أن يخلقني بأربعين سنة ) ؟ ليس المراد من لفظ التقدير ههنا : الخلق والتكوين . لأن فعل الإنسان، قبل خلق ذاته بأربعين سنة : محال . بل المراد من هذا التقدير : هو أنه تعالى كتب ذلك في اللوح المحفوظ . والتقدير بهذا التفسير مما لا نزاع في إثباته البتة فهذا جملة كلام المعتزلة على استدلال أصحابنا بهذا الخبر : والجواب : أما قوله : « رواية أبي هريرة مضطربة في هذا الخبر قلنا : جميع الروايات مشتملة على التعليل بتقدير الله . ودليلنا : هو هذا القدر المشترك . قوله ثانياً : كيف يشافه الولد والده بالسوء ، لا سيما إذا كان الولد من
Third: Proof indicates that the slip that occurred from Adam was of the type of leaving the better, or of the type of minor sins. On both estimates, it is not permissible to attach blame to him because of that. So if Moses had done it, it would indicate his ignorance; far be it from him. Fourth: He said to Adam: 'You are the one who made people miserable and brought them out of Paradise.' And Moses (peace be upon him) knew that the misery of creation and their expulsion from Paradise was not due to Adam; rather, God created them to be originally on earth. He said, the Exalted: 'And when your Lord said to the angels: Indeed I am placing a vicegerent on earth' (al-Baqara 30). Fifth: Adam (peace be upon him) argued with what is not a proof. For if we said that God wrote that upon him, that would be a proof for Adam. And God also wrote disbelief upon Pharaoh, Haman, and all the disbelievers. So they would have to be excused in it. And when that is invalid, we know the corruption of this argument. Sixth: The report includes that the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) approved Adam in this reasoning. So when it is established that this proof is wrong, then necessarily the Prophet's (peace be upon him) approval of Adam (peace be upon him) in this reasoning would be wrong. Seventh: Rational proofs have established the corruption of the doctrine of jabr (compulsion), and the Quran from its beginning to its end speaks of the corruption of jabr. And if an individual report (khabar al-wahid) comes contrary to reason and contrary to the Quran, its interpretation (ta'wil) is obligatory, if it accepts interpretation, and it is rejected if it does not accept interpretation. And this report accepts interpretation in several ways: First: Perhaps he (peace be upon him) had related this speech from the Jews, but the narrator when he entered did not hear that narration; rather he heard this amount and thought that he had related it from himself and his doctrine. Second: Perhaps he (peace be upon him) said: 'So Adam defeated Moses' with Adam in the accusative – meaning that Moses overcame him and made him refuted, and that what Adam mentioned is not a proof nor an excuse. And we have shown: If that were an excuse, then all disbelievers would have to be excused. And we also mentioned that occurrence of variation in grammar in individual reports is not unlikely. Third: This is the strongest view: the intended meaning of this dialogue is not blame for the sin, nor excuse from it – God knows. Rather, Moses asked him about the cause that led him to that slip, such that he left Paradise because of it. So Adam (peace be upon him) said: My exit from Paradise was not due to that slip; rather it was because God had written upon me that I should go out to earth and be a vicegerent therein. And this meaning was written in the Torah. So inevitably Adam's argument was strong, and Moses (peace be upon him) became like one overcome. Fourth: His statement: 'Why do you blame me for a matter which God decreed upon me forty years before He created me?' The intended meaning of 'decree' (taqdir) here is not creation and bringing into being, because an action of a human before the creation of his own self by forty years is impossible. Rather, the intended meaning of 'decree' is that He, the Exalted, wrote that in the Preserved Tablet. And 'decree' in this interpretation is something about whose establishment there is absolutely no dispute. This is the sum of the speech of the Mu'tazila concerning the inference of our companions by this report. And the answer: As for his saying: 'The narration of Abu Hurayra is disordered in this report,' we say: All the narrations include the reason of God's decree (taqdir). And our proof is this shared content. His saying, secondly: 'How can a son address his father with evil, especially if the son is from...'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's objections to the use of the hadith of Adam and Moses as proof for predestination (jabr), followed by the author's initial rebuttal. The Mu'tazila enumerate seven objections, though only the third through seventh are fully detailed here. 

First, the third objection: Adam's slip was either a case of leaving the better (tark al-afdal) or a minor sin (saghira), so blame cannot attach; if Moses did it, it would show ignorance of this, which is impossible. 

Fourth: Moses knew that humanity's misery and expulsion were not due to Adam's sin but because God willed them to be on earth (citing Quran 2:30). 

Fifth: If predestination were an excuse, then Pharaoh and all disbelievers would be excused, which is absurd. 

Sixth: The Prophet's approval of Adam's argument would be erroneous if Adam's argument were invalid. 

Seventh: Rational proofs and the Quran together refute jabr; an individual report contradicting both must be interpreted or rejected. The hadith can be interpreted in four ways: (1) The Prophet was merely quoting Jews; (2) The Prophet actually said 'Adam defeated Moses' meaning Moses refuted him, i.e., Adam's argument was invalid; (3) The dialogue was about the cause of expulsion, not blame for sin; (4) 'Decree' means writing in the Preserved Tablet, not creation. 

Then the author summarizes: 'This is the sum of the Mu'tazila's speech.' The answer begins by rebutting the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is disordered, asserting that all versions share the same reasoning about God's decree. The response is cut off as it addresses the second objection about a son addressing his father harshly.

Key Terms
حجة
تصويب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith proves that predestination is a valid excuse for any sin.
Correction: The fifth objection shows that if it were an excuse, it would excuse even the worst disbelievers, which is false.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the fifth objection draw from the idea that predestination would excuse Adam?
A) Predestination excuses all sins.
B) If predestination excused Adam, it would also excuse disbelievers, which is false, so the argument is invalid.
C) Adam was not truly predestined.
D) Moses was wrong to blame Adam.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that predestination cannot be a universal excuse, and that the Prophet's approval cannot endorse an invalid argument, forcing reinterpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'it was written' to avoid taking responsibility? How does the Mu'tazila's reductio against universal excuse expose the danger of that thought?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that if Adam's reasoning were false, the Prophet's approval would be impossible. What does this teach you about the responsibility you have to ensure the arguments you accept are sound, especially when they come from respected sources?
?
The lesson shows that even a prophet's argument can be questioned. Are you willing to examine your own deeply held beliefs with the same critical honesty, or do you hide behind authority to avoid uncomfortable truths?

Objection 7 and the Initial Response: Interpreting the Hadith
The Mu'tazila present the seventh objection and four interpretations, followed by the start of the author's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's seventh objection that the hadith contradicts reason and Quran, and their four reinterpretations, as well as the beginning of the author's response.

Core Idea
The hadith must be interpreted to align with reason and Quran, not taken literally as jabr.

The seventh and most comprehensive objection asserts that rational proofs and the Quran both refute jabr (compulsion), so the individual report (khabar al-wahid) of this hadith must be interpreted or rejected. The Mu'tazila offer four plausible reinterpretations that preserve free will. The author then begins a response, though it is cut off. This lesson explores the core conflict between textual evidence and rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the seventh objection and its first two interpretations
Read the objection's statement that rational proofs and Quran refute jabr, and the first two ways: the Prophet was quoting Jews, or the grammar indicates Moses defeated Adam.
Note the reliance on variant readings and context.
2
Read the third and fourth interpretations
Read the third (strongest) interpretation: the dialogue was about the cause of expulsion, not blame for sin; and the fourth: 'decree' means writing in the Preserved Tablet, not creation.
These preserve the hadith's validity while rejecting jabr.
3
Read the beginning of the response
Read the author's initial rebuttal against the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is disordered. Note that it is cut off.
The response is incomplete; the author was addressing the second objection about a son speaking harshly to his father.

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's objections to the use of the hadith of Adam and Moses as proof for predestination (jabr), followed by the author's initial rebuttal. The Mu'tazila enumerate seven objections, though only the third through seventh are fully detailed here.

Fifth: If predestination were an excuse, then Pharaoh and all disbelievers would be excused, which is absurd. 

Sixth: The Prophet's approval of Adam's argument would be erroneous if Adam's argument were invalid. 

Seventh: Rational proofs and the Quran together refute jabr; an individual report contradicting both must be interpreted or rejected. The hadith can be interpreted in four ways: (1) The Prophet was merely quoting Jews; (2) The Prophet actually said 'Adam defeated Moses' meaning Moses refuted him, i.e., Adam's argument was invalid; (3) The dialogue was about the cause of expulsion, not blame for sin; (4) 'Decree' means writing in the Preserved Tablet, not creation. 

Then the author summarizes: 'This is the sum of the Mu'tazila's speech.' The answer begins by rebutting the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is disordered, asserting that all versions share the same reasoning about God's decree. The response is cut off as it addresses the second objection about a son addressing his father harshly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith is a clear proof for jabr (compulsion) and cannot be interpreted otherwise.
Correction: The Mu'tazila show that the hadith is open to multiple interpretations that preserve free will and align with reason and Quran.

Checkpoint
According to the strongest interpretation (third), what was the real reason for Adam's expulsion?
A) Adam's sin.
B) God had written that Adam would go to earth as a vicegerent.
C) Moses' anger.
D) The Jews' conspiracy.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that the hadith must be interpreted in a way that does not contradict reason and Quran, offering four plausible alternatives, while the author's rebuttal is incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith that seems to say your sins were predestined, does your heart secretly welcome the excuse to avoid responsibility? How can you train yourself to reject that comfort and instead seek a proper interpretation like the Mu'tazila did?
?
Think of a sin you have committed and for which you blamed your circumstances, upbringing, or even 'Allah's decree'. Can you now see how that was a misuse of the concept of qadar? What concrete step can you take today to own that sin fully and repent without excuses?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that reason must judge revelation when there is an apparent contradiction. Are you willing to let your God-given intellect challenge your received interpretations, even if it makes you uncomfortable? What is one belief you hold that you have never critically examined, and will you give yourself permission to question it in light of this lesson?

Mu'tazila's Second Objection: Grammatical Reinterpretation
Mu'tazila's second objection: grammatical reading and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila challenge the Ash'ari reading of the hadith through a grammatical alternative and its implications.

Core Idea
A single shift in case can invert the entire argument.

The Mu'tazila first target the wording of the hadith, suggesting that a different grammatical reading changes its meaning entirely. They then argue that if Adam's excuse were accepted, it would set a dangerous precedent. This lesson unpacks their second objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second objection and its translation. Pay attention to the key terms: 'بنصب آدم' and 'أخبار الآحاد'.
2
Identify the alternative reading
The Mu'tazila propose that 'Adam' could be in the accusative case, making Moses the one who argued against Adam. Notice how this flips the usual understanding.
3
Analyze the consequence
The Mu'tazila argue that if Adam's excuse were valid, all disbelievers would be excused. Consider why this is a slippery slope argument.
4
Consider the reliability argument
They note that variation in iʿrāb (inflection) in solitary reports is not improbable. Reflect on how this weakens the Ash'ari reliance on the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لعله عليه السلام قال : « فحج آدم موسى - بنصب آدم ـ أي و أن موسى غلبه وجعله محجوجاً، وأن الذي ذكره آدم ليس بحجة ولا بعذر . وقد بينا : أنه لو كان ذلك عذراً ، لوجب أن يكون كل الكفار معذورين . وذكرنا أيضاً : أن وقوع التفاوت في الإعراب ، في أخبار الآحاد : غير مستبعد
•
الثالث : وهو الوجه الأقوى أنه ليس المراد من هذه المناظرة : الذم على المعصية ، ولا الاعتذار منه يعلم الله . بل موسى سأله عن السبب الحامل له على تلك الزلة ، حتى خرج بسببها من الجنة . فقال آدم صلى الله عليه وسلم : إن خروجي من الجنة ، ما كان بسبب تلك الزلة ، بل كان بسبب أن الله كان قد كتب علي أني أخرج إلى الأرض ، وأكون خليفة فيها . وهذا المعنى كان مكتوبا في التوراة ، فلا جرم كانت حجة آدم قوية، وصار موسى عليه السلام في ذلك كالمغلوب
الرابع : إن قوله : « كيف تلومني على أمر قدره الله علي قبل أن يخلقني بأربعين سنة ) ؟ ليس المراد من لفظ التقدير ههنا : الخلق والتكوين . لأن فعل الإنسان، قبل خلق ذاته بأربعين سنة : محال . بل المراد من هذا التقدير : هو أنه تعالى كتب ذلك في اللوح المحفوظ . والتقدير بهذا التفسير مما لا نزاع في إثباته البتة
فهذا جملة كلام المعتزلة على استدلال أصحابنا بهذا الخبر :
The second: Perhaps he—peace be upon him—said: 'So Adam argued against Moses' with accusative (naṣb) of Adam—meaning that Moses overcame him and made him argued against, and that what Adam mentioned is neither an argument nor an excuse. And we have shown: if that were an excuse, it would be necessary that all disbelievers be excused. And we also mentioned: that the occurrence of difference in grammatical inflection in solitary reports (akhbār al-āḥād) is not improbable.

The third: And it is the strongest view that the intended purpose of this debate was not blame for disobedience nor excuse from it—God knows. Rather, Moses asked him about the cause that led him to that slip, such that he left the Garden because of it. So Adam—peace be upon him—said: 'My leaving the Garden was not because of that slip, but because God had decreed upon me that I should go out to the earth and be a vicegerent (khalīfa) therein.' And this meaning was written in the Torah, so indeed Adam's argument was strong, and Moses—peace be upon him—became like the vanquished in that.

The fourth: His saying: 'Why do you blame me for a matter that God destined for me forty years before He created me?' The meaning of the word 'destiny' (taqdīr) here is not creation and bringing into being (khalq wa takwīn), for an action of a human forty years before the creation of his essence is impossible. Rather, the meaning of this 'taqdīr' is that He—exalted—wrote that in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-maḥfūẓ). And 'taqdīr' with this interpretation is something about whose affirmation there is no dispute at all.

So this is the sum of the Mu'tazila's speech on the inference of our companions by this report.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents three objections from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari use of the hadith of Adam and Moses to support predestination. The author summarizes their arguments before responding.

The second objection: The Mu'tazila claim that the hadith might be read with 'Adam' in the accusative case, meaning that Moses vanquished Adam, not the other way around. They argue that if Adam's excuse were valid, all disbelievers would be excused. They also note that variation in iʿrāb (grammatical inflection) in solitary reports is not improbable.

The third objection (considered the strongest by the Mu'tazila): The debate was not about blaming sin, but about the cause of expulsion from the Garden. Adam attributed his expulsion to God's decree that he would be khalīfa on earth, which was already in the Torah. Thus, Adam's argument was valid.

The fourth objection: The 'taqdīr' (destiny) mentioned in the hadith does not mean God's creation of the act, but His writing in the Preserved Tablet, which is undisputed.

The passage concludes by stating this is the sum of the Mu'tazila's arguments against the Ash'ari inference from this hadith.

Key Terms
أخبار الآحاد
الإعراب
النصب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila's second objection, what grammatical change would invert the meaning of the hadith?
A) Changing 'Adam' to the genitive case
B) Reading 'Adam' in the accusative case
C) Adding a conjunction
D) Removing the verb 'ḥajja'

Key Takeaway
A single grammatical change can reverse the apparent argument of the hadith, showing the importance of iʿrāb in interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or hadith, how often do you check the grammatical case (iʿrāb) of each word? Could a single diacritic change your understanding of a verse? What does that imply about your responsibility to learn Arabic properly?
?
The Mu'tazila reinterpreted a hadith to avoid a theological problem. Are you ever tempted to reinterpret clear texts to fit your own desires or to avoid a difficult teaching? How does the danger of subjective interpretation weigh on your heart?
?
If you were confronted with the argument that your excuse for a sin—if accepted—would logically excuse everyone, including disbelievers, would you still cling to that excuse? How does this reductio ad absurdum expose the weakness of self-justification before Allah? Will you abandon your excuses today?

Mu'tazila's Third Objection: The Real Cause of Expulsion
Mu'tazila's third objection: debate purpose and Adam's explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that Adam's expulsion was due to God's decree of vicegerency, not his sin.

Core Idea
The debate was about the cause of expulsion, not about sin.

The Mu'tazila consider their third objection the strongest. They reinterpret the debate's purpose, arguing that Moses questioned the cause of expulsion, and Adam attributed it to God's pre-written decree in the Torah. This lesson explains that perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the phrase 'وهو الوجه الأقوى' (it is the strongest view).
2
Identify the intended purpose of the debate
The Mu'tazila claim Moses asked about the cause of the slip (zalla) that led to expulsion, not to blame for sin.
3
Understand Adam's response
Adam explains his expulsion was because God decreed he would be a khalīfa on earth, not because of the slip.
4
Note the scriptural support
This meaning was already in the Torah, making Adam's argument strong and Moses appear overcome.

Mu'tazila's Fourth Objection: Meaning of 'Taqdīr'
Mu'tazila's fourth objection: redefining 'taqdīr' as written decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's distinction between 'taqdīr' as creation and 'taqdīr' as writing in the Preserved Tablet.

Core Idea
Taqdīr here means God's writing, not His creative act.

The fourth objection addresses the term 'taqdīr' (destiny) in the hadith. The Mu'tazila argue that it does not refer to God's creation of an act but to His pre-ordination in the Preserved Tablet. This lesson examines their reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the phrase 'ليس المراد من لفظ التقدير ههنا : الخلق والتكوين'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's refutation of the creation interpretation
They argue that it is impossible for a human action to be created 40 years before the human's own existence.
3
Learn their alternative interpretation
They claim that 'taqdīr' means God's writing in the Preserved Tablet, which is a matter of consensus.
4
Consider the conclusion
The Mu'tazila conclude their objections with a summary statement, noting that this interpretation is undisputed.

Refuting Claims of Narration Inconsistency and Abuse
Author answers the first two Mu'tazilite objections to the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's responses to the first two Mu'tazilite objections: that the hadith is inconsistent and that a son should not speak abusively to his father.

Core Idea
The hadith's common element—attribution to God's decree—suffices; Adam's words were a complaint, not abuse.

The Mu'tazila raise eight objections to the hadith of Adam and Moses debating predestination. The author begins by addressing the first two: the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is inconsistent and the charge that Adam (a prophet) spoke abusively to his father. Let's see how he defends the hadith's integrity and Adam's conduct.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the two objections and the author's short replies. Notice that each answer is direct and categorical.
The first answer uses the concept of 'common element' (قدر مشترك).
2
Analyze the first objection
The Mu'tazila claimed that Abu Hurayra's narration is inconsistent. The author responds that all versions share the common element of attributing the matter to God's decree. This common element is sufficient proof.
3
Analyze the second objection
The Mu'tazila asked how a prophet could speak abusively. The author clarifies that it was not abuse but a complaint (شكاية). This distinction is crucial.
Complaint to God about a trial is permissible.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice the author's approach: he does not deny the narration but reinterprets it. He also uses a minimal common denominator to preserve the argument.

الظاهر .
وقوله خامساً : ( لو كان ذلك حجة لآدم ، لكان أيضاً حجة لفرعون وهامان ، قلنا : هذه المناظرة إنما وقعت بين آدم وبين موسى . وهذه الحجة قوية بشرط أن تقع المناظرة بين إنسانين . إلا أن عندنا المناظرة مع الله ممتنعة . فلله - سبحانه - أن يقول : هذا العذر ، وإن كان عذراً فيها بين الخلق . إلا أني لا أقبله من فرعون وهامان ، وأقبله من آدم . لأن أفعالي معللة بمحض الإلهية وقوله ( سادساً (۱) ] : ( لعله - عليه السلام ـ إنما حكى هذا الكلام عن
اليهود ، قلنا : هذا سوء ظن بالراوي وقوله سابعاً : « لعل الإعراب بخلاف ما ذكرتم ، قلنا : هذا أيضاً سوء
ظن بالراوي .
وقوله ثامناً : « لعل المراد بهذا التقدير : أنه تعالى كتب ذلك على آدم ، قلنا : هب أنه كذلك. لكن الله تعالى لما أخبر عنه ، امتنع أن لا يقع ، وإلا لزم أن ينقلب خبر الله من كونه صدقاً إلى كونه كذباً ، وإنه محال
واعلم : أنه لما رجع حاصل الاستدلال بهذا الخبر إلى هذا الحرف . فتقول : هذا الحرف حاصل ، بدون التمسك بهذا الخبر . لأن القرآن دل على أنه تعالى أخبر عن بعضهم ، أنه لا يؤمن . حينئذ يتم هذا الاستدلال ، من غير
And the answer:
As for his saying: 'The narration of Abu Hurayra is inconsistent in this report,' we said: All the narrations include the explanation by God's decree. And our proof is this common element.
His second saying: 'How can a son speak abusively to his father, especially when the son is among the greatest prophets?' We said: We do not accept that this is a case of abusive speech. Rather, it was a complaint.
His third saying: 'What issued from Adam was of the minor sins, so it is not permissible to blame him for it.' We said: We do not accept that it was of the minor sins; rather, it was of the major sins. Except that we say: That incident occurred before prophethood. 'Because of Adam,' we said: Why not?
And his fourth saying: 'The expulsion of people from the Garden should not be said... because the expulsion coinciding with that slip was added to it apparently.'
And his fifth saying: 'If that were an argument for Adam, it would also be an argument for Pharaoh and Haman.' We said: This debate only occurred between Adam and Moses. And this argument is strong on the condition that the debate occurs between two humans. However, in our view, debate with God is impossible. For God—glorified—can say: This excuse, even if it is an excuse among creatures, I do not accept it from Pharaoh and Haman, but I accept it from Adam. Because my actions are based solely on ulūhiyya (divine lordship).
And his sixth saying: 'Perhaps he—peace be upon him—merely narrated this speech from the Jews.' We said: This is a bad opinion of the narrator.
And his seventh saying: 'Perhaps the grammatical inflection is contrary to what you mentioned.' We said: This is also a bad opinion of the narrator.
And his eighth saying: 'Perhaps what is meant by this taqdīr is that God wrote that upon Adam.' We said: Granted that. But since God informed about it, it is impossible for it not to occur; otherwise, it would necessitate that God's news turns from truth to falsehood, which is impossible.
And know: when the essence of the inference by this report reduces to this point, you say: This point is established without adhering to this report. For the Qur'an indicates that God informed about some of them that they will not believe. Then this inference is complete, without...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is the author's systematic refutation of each Mu'tazilite objection, followed by a concluding independent argument.

The author answers eight points:
1. To the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is inconsistent, he responds that all versions share the common element of attributing the matter to God's decree.
2. To the objection that a son should not speak abusively to his father, he says it was not abuse but a complaint (shikāya).
3. To the claim that Adam's sin was minor and thus not blameworthy, the author asserts it was a major sin, but argues it occurred before prophethood (thus before his infallibility). The response ends abruptly.
4. The author's brief response to the objection about expulsion coinciding with the slip is cut off; the text only states the objection partially.
5. To the objection that if Adam's excuse were valid it would justify Pharaoh and Haman, the author distinguishes: this debate was between humans, and God may accept excuses from some and not others by His absolute will.
6. To the suggestion that the Prophet merely relayed a Jewish story, he labels it a bad opinion of the narrator.
7. To the grammatical argument, he again calls it a bad opinion of the narrator.
8. To the claim that taqdīr means writing in the Preserved Tablet, he argues that even so, once God informs of something, its occurrence is necessary; otherwise God's word would become false, which is impossible.

Finally, the author concludes that the same conclusion (that the disbelievers are not excused due to predestination) can be derived directly from the Qur'an (e.g., verses stating God informed that some will not believe), without relying on the hadith. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
التقدير
الشكاية
المشافهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is inconsistent?
A) He agrees it is inconsistent and rejects the hadith.
B) He says all versions share the common element of attributing the matter to God's decree.
C) He says only one version is authentic.
D) He says the inconsistency is due to scribal errors.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the hadith by focusing on its shared core (God's decree) and reclassifying Adam's speech as a complaint, not abuse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself arguing with someone, do you first look for common ground or focus on the differences? How can you apply the author's method of focusing on the shared element in your own disagreements?
?
Have you ever justified harsh words by calling them 'just a complaint'? What standard would you use to ensure your speech is not abusive, especially when you are upset?
?
If a friend's story about a past event seems inconsistent, are you quick to dismiss them, or do you try to find the core truth? How does this lesson challenge you to be more charitable in evaluating others' accounts?

Refuting the Objection of Minor Sin and Before Prophethood
Author responds to the third objection about Adam's sin being minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's response to the Mu'tazilite claim that Adam's sin was minor and thus not blameworthy, and the additional point that it occurred before prophethood.

Core Idea
Adam's sin was major, not minor, and it occurred before his prophethood.

The third Mu'tazilite objection argues that Adam committed only a minor sin (صغيرة), so blaming him is unjust. The author counters by asserting it was a major sin (كبيرة) and adds that it happened before prophethood, when he was not yet infallible. However, the text of his answer is cut off; we will work with what is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the author's response
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the answer is fragmented; it says 'We do not accept...' and then ends abruptly.
OCR risk: the text may be incomplete.
2
Identify the two points
The author makes two points: 1) Adam's sin was major, not minor. 2) That incident occurred before prophethood. The second point implies that before prophethood, prophets are not yet infallible.
3
Consider the incomplete ending
The text ends with 'قلنا : لم لا' (we said: why not?), which might be a question to the Mu'tazila. This shows the answer continues beyond what we have.
The answer is not fully preserved.
4
Reflect on the significance
The author's claim that it was a major sin and before prophethood defends the possibility of blame. This is important for the overall debate on predestination.

Refuting the Fourth Objection: Expulsion from the Garden
The fourth Mu'tazilite objection is presented, but the author's answer is missing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth Mu'tazilite objection regarding the expulsion from the Garden coinciding with Adam's slip, even though the author's response is missing from the text.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that the expulsion coinciding with the slip makes it appear as punishment, but the answer is not preserved.

The fourth objection states that the expulsion from the Garden should not be attributed to Adam's slip because the expulsion happened to coincide with it, making it seem like punishment. Unfortunately, the author's answer to this objection is not extant in the source text. We will examine the objection itself and consider what a response might entail.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the fourth objection. Note that there is no author response following it in the text.
The text only contains the objection, then moves to the fifth.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the expulsion of people from the Garden (due to Adam's sin) should not be said to be a punishment, because the expulsion coincided with the slip only apparently, not causally.
The wording is fragmented: 'ما كان يجوز أن يقال' (it should not be said).
3
Consider the missing answer
Since the author's answer is absent, we can only speculate. Typical responses might argue that the expulsion was indeed a consequence or that God's actions are not bound by human categories.
This lesson highlights the importance of textual completeness.
